The Restoration Drama
ail! m n^ al w r ’ A n The t Go J ned » °f Manners
« W. Dymock, bydney), gives us
ike first historical view of the rise and fall
C0 “ 1C dra “ atis ts of the Restoration,
therein, continually, he has to contra-
st A iha a n lay ‘ A e n< l thereupon we have to
SS f the h . oaor of being the first Wowser
back from Albert the Wowser to Macaulay ;
her eV AnH h V S bUt an ? cho of Jerem y Col-
w d ere mu T st have been Wowsers
Ztf w° re him ---- Ia fact, historically the
first Wowser was Jehovah, who objected
rather forcibly to Eve eating apples on Sun-
fr^i aCaUIa K,- JU , <ised tlle Res toration drama
from an ethical view-point. Of course, the
comic dramatists were extremely rude.
Macaulay stigmatised them as immoral. “His
if A s a -? rtan t conclusion about Congreve
and \\ ycberley was that their pl-ays were
m^Hrii oU ia!n UP ° n the mr,ral values of the
middie lhth century.” And since Jeremy
Collier discovered that the Restoration
dramatists were profane, wicked and sub-
versive of good manners, nearly every
printed opinion leaves the impression that
these writers have been measured by stan-
understood WoUld neither have respected nor
Palmer’s volume aims t giving the con-
demned a trial by a jury of their contempo-
thiT He finds > historically,
"Sf W e Pnghsh comedy of manners began
G , eol 'ge Etherege, was improved by
Wycherley, rose to perfection in Congreve
declined by easy stages with Vanbrough and
1 aiquhar, and was finally extinguished in
fcheridan and Goldsmith. It is with the
first five dramatists that he deals.
LJntd Jeremy Collier, in his Short Victo
ri ~e , *ro fane ness and Immorality of the
English Stage, put the moral acid upon the
Kestoration drama, nobody had thought of
xt from the Wowser viewpoint.
Neither Pepys nor Evelyn, though they
thought the Court shocking, considered the
theatre profane. After the invention of the
moral test, the drama was advised to adopt
the neck-to-knee costume. Macaulay added
ngfeaves. Palmer’s contention is that the
nation has consistently misjudged the comic
dramatists owing to an inflamed “Puritan
nervous system,” whereas our criterion of
judgment should be “What sort of men were
they in the face of their period?” We must
consider them solely from a contemporary
moral standpoint. J
First we must entirely be rid of the idea
that the comic dramatists, were susceptible
to the sentiments proper to a well-conducted
monogamous society. But in reading the
plays two important concessions must be
made to a modern reader. Every one of
these plays contains words which have long
since disappeared from ordinary conversa-
tion, though in their time such words were
commonplaces of conversation. And the
subject is usually unpleasant, as it is fre-
quently unpleasant in Shakespeare, “but,
once encountered, it need not again be men-
turned. Our point of view has merely
changed ; we can’t jest any more about sex-
ual disease. And the indecency is nowhere
sensuous—until the Restoration drama began
to decline. “The comic possibilities of
adultery are exploited to-day as thoroughly
as in the time of Wycherley; but social
hypocrisy has hitherto steadily insisted that
physical facts shall as far as possible be
slurred.”
The complete answer to Jeremy Wowser
Collier is: “The comic drama of the Resto-
ration rested upon a comic treatment of sex.
It depended for its effect upon the elimina-
tion of passion. Comic treatment is treat-
ment in a dry light. The heart of the mat-
ter lies in the epigram of Walpole that life
is a comedy to the man who thinks, a tragedy
to the. man who feels To take an ex-
treme instance, it is perfectly true that rape
and adultery are part of the action of the
majority of the plays of our comic drama-
tists. But, the treatment being purely
comic, these actions cease to be morally
significant. The sexual emotion of the spec-
tator is not involved English comedy,
after Jeremy Collier, drooped, withered and
passed finally away precisely in the propor-
tion that the ‘amorous, soft, passionate,
luscious saying’ persistently tended to in-
trude.”
The modern farce, musical play, music-
hall or revue is much more indecent than
the Restoration drama, simply because it is
so cleverly meant to be.
Collier said, in effect, “Art for morality’s
sake,” and Congreve accepted the precept.
Without cackling about “rrt for art’s sake”
—as if art could be for anything but its own
sake—-we may accept Palmer’s definition of
the aim of the artist: “His impulse is not
the impulse of a moralist to improve the
worldit is the impulse of an artist to ex-
press it.... Art is not primarily concerned
with morality, but morality is the stuff of
the poet’s art Morality is his subject,
though it is not his object... .The critics of
Collier’s generation, and incompetent critics
of every generation, invariably confuse
the subject with the object—worse, they
mistake the subject of a picture or a poem
for the work itself.”
The critical pages of John Palmer form a
valuable commentary on the chief figures
and the chief works in a shining period in
English dramatic literature. The historical
perspective is well sustained, and the figures
emerge from their niches with a fine sugges-
tion of life.

Epigrams from the Greek
HOLY GROUND.
What time the Graces sought for hallowed
ground,
To build a fane that age could not impair,
The soul of Aristophanes they found
And set it there,
—Plato.
ILL-OMENED. .
Fair may the voyage prove, hut if like me
Driven by a gale thou breach the port of
Doom,
Blame him, but not the inhospitable sea,
Who dares to loose his moorings from a
tomb.
—Leonidas.
A DEAD HERDSMAN.
The cattle wandered at eventide
Down to the byre from the mountain side,
The clinging flakes were on horn and hide
For the drifting snow was deep.
Ah for the herdsman ! slumbers he,
Lying so still on the stricken treef
Ay——and long shall his slumber be
Whom the thunder lulls to sleep.
—Diotimus.
FOR A COPY OF THEOCRITUS.
The Muses of Bucolic song were scattered
far of old,
But now they are a single flock within a
single fold.
- —Artemidorus.
AN OFFERING.
Menoctas' quiver, with his bow,
Serapis, at thy feet doth lie.
No shafts'are here —the stricken foe
Of lies peris shall tell thee why.
—Callimachus.
DROWNED IN HARBOR.
Every sea is the sea; and the Cyclades are
not to blame,
Vainly hath llelle's race, and the Needles
their evil fame-
These 1 escaped unscathed, and I deemed
that my peril was past,
For in Scarplie's haven I lay, but there she
whelmed me at last.
Pray for a safe return who will, but take
warning from me,
Lo, I cry from my grave, “The sea shall be
ever the sea.”
—Antipater of Sidon.
THE EPITAPH—AND THE READER.
My name and country were no matter
what!
Noble my race who cares though it were
not?
The fame I won in life....is all forgot!
Now here I lie... .and no one cares a jot!
—Paul.
TPIE FLIGHT OF TIME.
If glad thy mind,
Life thou shalt find
Too brief.
One single night
Is infinite
To grief.
—Lucian.
A LIFE HISTORY.
Time was I was not: I began to be,
I was, and am not ; that's the end of me;
lie lies that doth a fuller knoicledge feign,
For I, that am not, shall not be again.
-‘—Anonymous.
ON A LITTLE CHILD.
O Hades, death's inexorable king.
Wilt never turn aside from plundering?
Now is Callaeschrus of liis life bereft.
And what avails to know the child shall be
A plaything in thy halls, Persephone,
When sorrow broods above the home he
left?
—Anonymous.
TO A MISER.
You count your gold with greedy sest,
Your growing gains you tell,
Forgetting time breeds interest,
But hoary hairs as well.
Nor wine nor perfumes gladden you,
No garland crowns your head,
Love's dainty joys you never knew —
And soon you will be dead:
And then the hoarded treasures there,
Poor wretch, must you forsake;
One single coin for Charon's fare
Is all that you shall take.
—Antiphanes.
A LAWSUIT.
Deaf were the plaintiff and defendant, too,
The judge who heard the cause was deafer
still,
So to the plaint that five months' rent was
due
Defendant said, “At night I work the
mill.”
And judgment ran, “Why strive with one
another?
Both must support her; isn't she your
Mothert"
—Nicarchus.
ON THE STATUE OF A WOUNDED
FAUN.
Methinks that Dionysus heard
The wounded satyr moan
And so the god< to pity stirred)
Transformed him into stone.
But still hts pangs he cannot bear,
The miracle was vain;
l Behold him in the marble there,
Poor faun, he cries for pain!
—Leontius.
ON A NAMELESS GRAVE.
O sailor, ask not whose the tomb may be,
But Fortune grant to you a kindlier sea.
—Anonymous.
ON DIONYSIUS OF TARSUS.
For sixty years I bore an evil lot:
No child I had —and would my sire had not.
—Anonymous.
From Greek Love Songs and Epigrams,
from the Anthology, first and second series,
translated by J. A. Pott (Kegan Paul;
Is. 9d. each volume).
It is obvious that the Rhymballade needs
education. So much poetic promise must
not be allowed to go to waste. The rhym-
bellers of Australia, who have done so much
for the mother, are requested to take her
infant in hand. A guinea will be awarded
to the rhymballadist who forwards the
neatest rhymballade to this page, entries clos-
ing on May 30.
Making our own definition, we stipulate
that a rhymballade, following the model of
the ballade, must consist of three stanzas of
eight lines, followed by a verse of four lines
known as the envoy, each of the stanzas anc
the envoy closing with the same refrain
The same set of rimes, the sequence being
A B, A. B, B, 0, B, C, in the same order
they occupy in the first stanza, must repeat
throughout the whole of its verses, the se-
quence for the envoy being B, 0, B, C. No
word once used as a rime must be used again
for that purpose. Bach stanza and the envoy
must close with the unaltered refrain. (A
clever variation in the envoy refrain may
perhaps be pardoned.) Thus only three
rimes are allowed for the whole of the
rhymballade. Counsels of perfection sug-
gest that the sense of the refrain must
be supreme throughout the rhymballade: it
must come in without strain as the natural
close of each stanza and of the envoy. And
the sense of the rhymballade should not be
split into two verses of four lines in each
stanza. These rules also apply to the bal-
lade ; but the rhymballade has further techni-
calities. Each stanza and the envoy must
begin with a pun upon the last syllable or
syllables of the refrain, and each pun must
be different. Thus the rhymballadist is con-
fronted with the necessity of choosing four
words beginning with the same sound which
he must work in without the sense of effort
in a poem complete in itself —and that same
sound must close his refrain. R. G. Hender-
son’s fine rhymballade has not exhausted—
nor was it meant to exhaust —the possibili-
ties of this latest-born Australian verse-form.
Five of Hewlett’s finest novels, including
Little Novels of Italy and Richard Yea-and-
Nay, are now to be got in Macmillan’s
admirable sevenpenny editions.
E. Backhouse and J. O. P. Bland, autho
of that brilliant pageant of China, Chi
under the Empress Dowager, repeat the
success with Annals and Memoirs of
Court of Peking (Heinemann, 165.). Th
work is invaluable to an understanding o:
China, since it presents a faithful pictu
cf life at the Court of Peking from t
middle of the 16th century down to the pas
ing of the Manchus and the establishment
the present disorderly Republic. No chron
logical record is attempted; the atmosphe
of the Court is more truthfully reproducec
by this series of the most remarkable even
of Chinese history, taken directly from tl
narratives of Chinese writers, and presentee
without the intrusion of European bias. Tl
authors hope that the reader “can, and wi
approach this study of Eastern life, describee
by Orientals, in the same spirit of detac
ment, with the same recognition of fund
mental humanities and moralities, as tha
with which we approach the brutal franknes
of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel
things written for our learning... .We hav
endeavored to steer a middle course betwee
the Scylla of the Chinese chroniclers’ bruta
realism and the Charybdis of our conveu
tional and often prurient reticences.” Thes
three centuries of China’s history serve bu:
to provfe that the slow course of her evolu
tion cannot be dramatically disturbed; ant
that republicanism is not, for generation
China’s role. But the,book will not be reat
for its moral, but for its delightful glimpse
into the unchanging Chinese soul, its nai'v
account of horrors and its inhuman humo
Dip anywhere into these annals, even up t
the time of the fall of the Manchus, and yo
will find yourself back in the palace of Nero
or supping, rather diffidently, with the Boi
gias. . '
W. B. Maxwell, author of The Devil
Garden, frankly acknowledges a case o:
plagiarism. A correspondent in Th
Atiiknasum had pointed out the resemblanc
in plot between that novel and Zola’s Bet
Humaine. The situation in both books i
the same; but Maxwell justly nleads tha
the murder, by a husband, of -the man wh
had dishonored his wife is “a life-situation
belonging to the realm of ordinary facts
newspaper reports, common talk,” and tha
it had probably been used in many othe
books. The genesis of Maxwell's book i
explained, giving us an interesting light or
his methods. “For a long time I had i
jotted down in my notes as the material oi
a novel, that was to be the study of
man’s life after an undiscovered crime. Th
essential matter was that the man was to
be punished by conscience, to be brought to
expiation by internal forces, although free
from the external peril of the law. I re
turned to my note again and again, bu
could never make anything of it until, two
years hgo, it occurred to me that the logica
working-out would be to screw the murderei
round into the position of the man he hac
killed, and thus deprive him of the sense o;
justification of the deed which had hitherto
upheld him.” But how did the first jotting
down. of the situation come? Maxwell ad
mits it must have been an unconscious mem
ory of Zola’s work, which he had read more
than once.. Further similarities in the work-
ing out of this idea were “doubtless causec
by that mocking voice of memory which an-
nounces itself as imagination, which habitu-
ally simulates the hot, eager accents of an
inspired prompting, and which the mosl
original waiters have had reason to dreac
like the very devil.” But no one can say
that The Devil's Garden is the same book
as La Bete Humaine, or that, beyond the
situation, it. bears the slighest resemblance to
it. Any writer has the right, as Shakespeare
had the right, to take any situation, no mat-
ter. how often before utilised by other
writers, and make it his own. Anyhow,
there are only 19 situations in life.
There is a popular class of pseudo-critics
writing for the British dailies and weeklies:
this class gossips cheerfully about literature,
supplies conlmonplace anecdotes about popu-
lar authors, but never bores its public by
creative criticism. One of the most popu-
lar is W. Robertson Nicholl. who contri-
butes drivel to The British Weekly under
the title of “The Correspondence of Claudius
Clear,” and is regarded, by his own public,
as an authority upon literature. From his
gentlemanly contributions he has selected a
number under the title, A Bookman's Letters
(Ilodder and Stoughton, per Angus and Rob-
ertson). He supplies innocuous memories
of Meredith, discourses heavily upon dull
authors, raises questions like' “Why did
Shakespeare Retire to Stratford on-Avon?”
and - “Was Thackeray a Cynic?” without
answering them, solemnly tells us nothing of
“The Art of the Reviewer,” and supplies
an index, The book is nicely printed.
“1-em rule” is annoyed for a column and a
half because The Sheffield Independent,
The Dundee Advertiser and The Book-
man have persuaded him to buy a rotten six-
penny novel published in England.' Well,
why buy sixpenny novels published in Eng-
land when you can have good Australian
novels for a shilling, and —without any
modesty we say it—why go to The Shef-
field Independent, The Dundee Adver-
tiser and The Bookman for your literary
criticism?
The Rhymballade
It is evident that the Australian climate
is suiting the imported rymbel; for already
the freakish thing has given birth to a big
baby. She has decided to call her infant
“Rhymballade.” It. G. Henderson, who at-
tended the case, reports that both are doing
well, and that the baby, while strikingly
resembling her mother about the key-word,
shows a very close likeness to her French
father, M. Ballade, about her rimes and her
physical structure. He forwards a photo-
graph of the child.
A RHYMBALLADE.
Billowed in laces, pink and white,
Only nine pounds, yet he needs a name!
And imperious uncles and aunts would blight
His joyous youth by their choice of the same,
For each of them proffers a diverse claim —
Weird appellations of horrory-and still,
Since I, 100, have a hand in the game.
I think, in the end, he’ll be plain Bill:
Bilious Sarah, the bombazine fright,
And Maud, an early-Victorian dame,
For euphonious Percy their, votes unite.
He was primeval Maud’s first flame,
And Sarah opines that wealth and fame
Are reserved for such genteel names. My
will
Is as firmly fixed as my metre’s lame.
I think, in the end, he’ll be plain Bill.
“Billets and dollars ad infinit,
If you name him from me,” said one who
came
From a Potts Point palace. But no! not
quite!
The name’s Isaiah! Now, do you blame
My reluctant heart and shivering frame?
Let the lad work his own way up hill,
Learn on his own the shy dollars to tame —
I think, in the end, he’ll be plain Bill.
Envoy.
Billions of curses strike with shame
The shade who invented “ Bobadil,”
That load 1 bear! By my halidame,
I think, in the end, he’ll be plain Bill!
R. G. HENDEESON.

MODERN MIRACLES.
Remarkable Cures by
CLEMENTS TONIC
RECENTLY, In France, a peasant woman, whose little girl had
been bed-ridden with paralysis for years, had occasion to visit a
churchyard which contained the statue of a well-known saint.
Some of the mould from the tomb adhered to her handkerchief, which
upon touching her child cured her. This fact is vouched for upon
reliable authority.
Miracles are not all confined to the older countries, for we have
heard also of similar remarkable incidents in newer countries, and very
often in Australia, where only eighteen short months ago a certain
section of Broken Hill was electrified by the recovery from apparently
incurable St. Vitus Dance of the daughter of Mrs. R. A. Addington, of
Argent Street. It was on the 10th October, 1911, when first the
mysterious cure was reported. The mother, being seen by a special
interviewer, and overcome by gratitude that her daughter was healthy
again, told the facts at once. She said that the disorder occurred
through her daughter falling from a tram. This gave her a great
shock, and so affected her nervous system that eventually St. Vitus
Dance ensued. . She became so bad that it was impossible to hold a
knife or fork, and going out of the house was impossible. This
remarkable condition continued for some time, medical skill being
useless, Mrs. Addington declared, when suddenly, as the result of using
Clements Tonic, a wonderful change took place. She gradually
improved, and soon regained her normal condition. Nearly two years
have elapsed since her complete recovery, and all that time she has
enjoyed the best of health. “I have great faith in Clements Tonic, and
always recommend it,” added the mother, “and you can make what
use you wish of this.”
It is not strange that miracles should follow the use of this
miraculous medicine. Its cures of weak nerves, insomnia, debility,
sick headache, indigestion and constipation are really remarkable.
ALL CHEMISTS AND STORES SELL IT EVERYWHERE.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Island of the Blest.”
Mr. Glynn declined to discuss the moral aspect,
and would not say whether he agreed with Dr.
Mjoberg that missionaries should not go among'st
the blacks, or with Mr. Hall that the blacks should
be given what comfort they could obtain from Chris-
tianity.—Daily payer.
It may be said, generally speaking, that the un-
civilised native is honest, but that the so-called
civilised aboriginal who has given up his old habits
and become often a mere loafer, is not. . . .1 see only
two alternatives, either rapid degradation and ex-
termination, or the formation of large reserves, wi-h
freedom therein from intercourse with whites and
“ Asiatics. — Professor Spencer's report.
Here, in a barn-like place, bedight
With tawdry, painted glass,
Hidden away from pleasant sight
Of trees and waving grass,
We dreamers, of the purblind eyes
And blood as water thin,
Ask pardon glibly—parrotwise—
For sins we dare not sin!
The organ’s sad, asthmatic tones
Throb in the heavy air;
The white-robed preacher dully drones
A parody of prayer.
High ’gainst the bright, symbolic pane,
A stupid, ’prisoned fly
Buzzes in frantic hope to gain
The light of God’s fair sky.
But lo! At last the preacher stands,
And cries: “Dear friends, I go
To Heathen lands—dark, fearful lands !
Where winds of evil blow.
Unto the hapless, heathen folk,
At bidding of the Lord—
Their shameful nakedness to cloak—
I carry now the Word!”
“Unhappy is the heathen’s lot—
Alone ’twixt sea and sky—
Their land by Time and Chance forgot
Has slipt the ages by;
But now I bear, into their Night,
The Gosnel sword of flame....
And I shall show them Wrong and Bight,
And teach them what is shame!”
* * * #
I see the distant, heathen isle,
Caressed by slumbrous seas;
The evil, sun-kissed beaches smile,
And, through the stately trees,
A fragrant spice-wind whispers low
To bird and butterfly—
While on the rocks where orchids grow
The basking lizards lie !....
Thus do the seasons go and come,
While nations wax and wane:
And there, the heathen in his home
Knows neither loss nor gain.
Christian with brother Christian strives,
Till mighty empires fall—-
But where the Stone Age still survives
Content is known of all!
There, where the shameful Stone Age lives,
In backwash of warm seas,
To weary eyes the good God gives
One glimpse of fabled peace!
And, lest we should forget the plan
From which, by chance, we came,
He there preserves the Simple Man-
Saws holiness — sans shame !
* * * *
Night’s vanguard climbs upon the world,
The sun-god slips away ;
I see his tattered banners hurled
Athwart the fields of day.
Dark-limned against the burning west,
Low in the purple sea
The ancient Island of the Blest
Awaits.... her tragedy !. . ..
Purple and gold and green below,
Opal and rose above —
And peace ice may not ever know,
No matter where we rove ;
This is the Heathen’s home —his grave—
From castes and creeds all free;
Can any peace the preachers have
Bless one so blest as he?
* . f..
Yet now some narrow mind and cold,
Groping in visions blurred—
Where all the paths are blind and old.
And Reason’s voice unheard —
With llolv Bell and Book must go
Unto this Island Blest,
That her poor, simple folk may know
The Big World’s fierce unrest!
What has your roaring Progress brought
Men of this age of steel?
What have your countless preachers taught,
O brains that throb and reel ?
O hearts that still may pity feel,
Pause now —and understand....
Speak—o’er your widespread Commonweal—
To save this one small land!
N.S.W.
ISHMAEL.
The Angel Boy.
It was a bonny, bright-haired boy
Who stood before the poor blind man.
They’d dressed him a la Fauntleroy,
And as I looked a tear drop ran
A-down his rosy, dimpled cheek.
I took his hand, and thus did speak:
“What ails you, Edwin, tell me true?”
Then turned on me the pretty youth,
His gentle eyes of heaven’s blue —
And they were limpid wells of truth.
“The poor blind man beside the gate
Has got no pennies in his plate!”
I patted him upon the head,
And gave a penny: “There, now go
And put it in the plate,” I said.
He moved away, and answered: “No,
You see, I only hung about
To get a chance to take one out.”
Victoria.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing some
care as to methods of address.
Ilusitiess letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable. acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
feels, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
aritstic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 16, 1914.
HOW BROWN LOST HIS
INHERITANCE.
Very long ago — 848 years ago, to be exact —
England was unready for defence, even as
Australia is now. That is to say, it wasn’t
sufficiently ready. After the Germans and
Danes and Norsemen seized England—just as
a later generation of wandering adventurers
seized Australia —they gradually abandoned
their predatory habits, and ceased to hunt for
foreign adventure. They left Ireland alone.
They decided, after the appalling wreckage
of the battle of Nectansmere, that it was best
to leave Scotland alone. For a brief period
England formed part of a Northern Empire,
but that was because it was annexed by a
furious and yet snufflebusting foreigner from
Denmark —a warrior who had a habit of
weeping in church; not because it
went out to do gratuitous annexation.
When the Northern Empire fell to
pieces because the last descendant of
Gorm the Old died without issue and
full of beer, and England again set up in
business for itself, it was even more studi-
ously peaceful than usual, and its desire
was not to needlessly anger anybody. Its
white-robed king was a whale for piety till
he died. The country sent no armies to the
Soudan or South Africa or elsewhere, and
it offered back-talk to no extraneous poten-
tate whatsoever. It didn’t speak disparag-
ingly of Boers or Chinamen. England was
the land of Calm Stodge until it came abso-
lutely in sight of the catastrophe. People
with names like Beawolpii and Egfritii
held, in a general sort of way, that if the
country gave no offence nobody would take
offence. Gurtii was of the same opinion,
and Cedric agreed. In the last days be-
fore the storm England didn’t even cause
hostility, as Australia is forced to do, by
drawing the color line, for in those halcyon
times there was no color line to draw. It
didn’t annoy its neighbors by refusing the
folk of adjacent, over-crowded countries the
run of its empty spaces, for there were no
adjacent, over-crowded countries. It had
no religious differences with the peoples
round about, for all these peoples were of
one faith and hadn’t begun to religiously dif-
fer. It didn’t annoy immigrants with medi-
cal inspection. And England, 848 years ago,
had a citizen army of men—-not boys; but
unfortunately it didn’t take its army seri-
ously. It had a fleet which was a very fine
fleet —on paper or sheepskin—but the ship-
ping wasn’t taken very seriously either.
The Peace Society idea had got hold. And
that was how Brown, the typical English-
man, lost his inheritance. So, also, did his
brother, Smith.
* * * *
When two wholly unprovoked enemies fell
almost simultaneously on the contented Eng-
land of 848 years ago, the country had one
unusual advantage. The monk king, who
put his faith in God and goodwill, and
dressed in white as a sign of purity, and
refused even to occupy the same room
with his wife, had died just in time. His
place had been taken, at the last moment, by
about the best patriot and most tremendous
soldier that the English race ever produced.
He was no Joseph Cook, or Romulus
Augustulus, or Roderick, or Stanislaus
Poniatowski. Most races have done their
perish when a fool was in charge, but the
English went down with their very biggest
man at the helm. Unhappily events moved
too fast for the big man. He was crippled
by Public Stodge, by the Peace Society idea,
by the general reluctance to drill, by the
lack of efficiency, and by a Legislature
which hated to levy taxes. He was ham-
pered by Tradition. He was only in charge for
a few months, and hadn’t time to hang the
local Quaker with sufficient frequency. And
it was his curious ill-fortune to be “up
against” the greatest general that Norway
had ever produced—a magnificent ruffian who
had hacked his way from Norway to Greece
and back again; and the greatest soldier that
Normandy ever turned out.
* * * #
The last English king made an utter ruin
of the unprovoked Norwegian invader in the
ISorthern Territory—that is to say, in the
North of England. Then he made about
the swiftest tramp in history to look after
the equally unprovoked Norman invader in
the South. But, in those days, there was no
railway from Port Darwin to Adelaide be-
cause no one had had the sense to build one —
that is to say, the roads between York and
London were much neglected. The cadets,
which is another name for the ill-trained
citizen army, went to pieces on the hurried
march. The ironclads had abandoned their
job of patrolling the narrow seas through
lack of coal, because Millen and Joseph
Cook, supported by Higgs, had cut down the
estimates; or in other words, the English
fleet had retired into the Thames from lack
of wages and salt beef, and had left the road
open. If it had had a handy fortified harbor
at Bowen or Gladstone or some place
with the same name on the English Channel,
it might have refitted and got back
to its job in time, but it had to
retire on the congested city, and it
didn’t get back in time. The enemy
landed unopposed, and burned Brisbane —or
some corresponding place—and was advanc-
ing on Sydney—or what answered to Sydney
in the England of 848 years ago. The Peace
Society was still of opinion that nothing
serious would happen. The local Church
fell back on prayer—fell back so heavily that
it broke its neck, for when the Conquest hap-
pened even the humble curate was thrown
out into the back-alley to make room
for an alien. The Legislature spent too
much time discussing the loan, or the six-
hours question, or State Rights, or the
building of a dry capital at Canberra, or
something. So it was with a mere handful
of ill-trained men that the last English king
argued the point against the second enemy
who had happened along inside a year, and
had happened with no excuse save that the
country was a good one, and he wanted it.
When the day was over the army was prac-
tically extinct, and there wasn’t one leader
of any value or importance left alive. Thus
Brown, the Englishman, lost his farm, and
his house, and most of his clothes and boots,
and his victuals, and his bed, and his table,
and his chair, and the baby’s cradle—and
even his daughter, if it happened that a
stranger wanted her. And it is mentioned
casually, that “they put him in boxes filled
with nails,” and that “they hanged him up
by the feet and smoked him with foul
smoke.” Three years after the great inva-
sion an Irish army came over to attempt the
salvation of England, but England was past
saving.
* * * *
Yet the English nation had a certain
amount of good fortune. It lost its country
through lack of military training, lack of
discipline, ill-judged economy, and the
neglect to build railways to Port Darwin and
Perth, or whatever was the current equi-
valent of that oversight. Also it perished
because its voluntary service crowd got it
down, and it was obsessed by the folk who
declared that invasion was a false alarm.
Still it was conquered by a white race with
a religion and an alleged civilisation the
same as its own, and the conquerors were a
comparatively small body. So it wasn’t
blotted out as might have happened if a
brown or yellow horde, alien in color and
religion and civilisation and literature, had
descended on a community only one-tenth
its size. Brown, the Englishman, may have
lost for a long time all his goods and his
rights, but he was allowed to remain him-
self —to some extent. He didn’t become a
Mohammedan or a Buddhist or a nigger.
He had more luck than the unprepared
Hungarians had when they went to smash
at Mohacz, or the Greeks had when the
Turks arrived, or the Russians when the
Tartars dropped on them, and burned the
Czar and his family alive. There was left
to him a fair chance of national recupera-
tion.
* * * *
It was a long and dreadful experience
which fell on England by reason of its
casual optimism. The country, since that
episode of 848 years ago, has had kings of
Norman, French, Scotch and German origin,
but really English sovereigns have been few.
It was ruled for centuries by autocrats who
didn’t even pretend to speak the local lan-
guage. It got down to such a point of
subservience that no man might lawfully
defend himself against a Frenchman, no
matter what the provocation. And every
man found dead by violence was assumed to
be a Frenchman unless proved to be otherwise
—if he was proved to be otherwise he didn’t
matter much —and had to be accounted for-
on general principles by the nearest English
town or village, with fines and executions.
Centuries after the unpleasant event the
English race was still'paying for its'casual-
ness. In fact, it is paying even now. The
inheritance hasn’t been wholly recovered to
this day.
The conquerors who came over from
France had surnames, or they early acquired
them. These were largely territorial designa-
tions, and signified the pride and pomp and
state of the landowner. The Englishman,
who had lost his land, struggled along for a
while without a surname, just as Sambo did
in the slave days of Louisiana. He was an
obscure animal on whom no luxuries were
wasted. But at last there arose a habit of
giving him a family name, possibly because
it was easier for his boss to find him when
he was thus ticketed. He was called Smith
because he toiled at the forge making hard-
ware for his lord, and the exceeding
great number of Smiths suggests that
his lord used a heap of hardware. He
was Taylor because he made clothes (or
he mip'ht be Briggs because he specialised
in trousers) ; Brown because of his
complexion; Short because there wasn’t
much of him; Johnson because he was
John’s son; or Jobson because he was Job’s
son. None of these neople—not even .Tobson
—has ever recovered the throne which a set
of supine ancestors lost 848 years ago.
Surprisingly few of the Smiths and Browns
have come to the front as statesmen, for the
tradition of the “ruling families” still lin-
gers. Considering the comnaratively liberal
institutions of England during later cen-
turies, and the great numbers of the con-
quered as compared with the remarkable
fewness of the conquerors, the progress made
towards the recovery of the land hasn’t been
so great as might be expected. The English
aristocracy is still proud of its French origin
when it possesses the article, and occasionally
professes to possess the article when it
doesn’t. And, curiously enough, the old
distinctively English names are still re<-
garded as somewhat of a jest, and as a butt
for the humorist and the cartoonist. There
remain among all the English-speaking
peoples some traces of the old French deri-
sion for the Englishman who neglected to
prepare a sufficient army and navy in the
days when the world was young. Therefore
when Smith does rise to a certain social
elevation he is addicted to calling himself
Carrington, or something of the sort. He
wants to be one of the scorners —not one of
the scorned. He wants to be French and is
ashamed of the degradation of being English.
* * * *
So, even after all these years, there lingers
a suspicion that the money which Cook
and Kelly and Higgs, encouraged, perhaps,
bv Miss Rose Scott, and abetted by Harry
Holland, saved on the army estimates and
the fleet, and the Northern Territory rail-
road in a.d. 1066 was a poor investment.
For the enemy came and took all the savings.
Also he took nearly everything else. And the
Peace Society folk who wrote to the paper
which was published by hand at Westminster,
saying that unprovoked aggression was im-
possible and unthinkable, were set by their
new masters to carting dung and herding
swine. Many of their descendants are still
engaged in humble avocations of the sort.
Furthermore, the scrap of ancient history
on which this article is built suggests that
even a very great statesman and soldier
can’t organise an efficient defence at the last
moment. It was because of the impossi-
bility of doing everything at the last moment
that Brown, the Englishman, lost his inheri-
tance, as, likewise, did his brother Smith.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Australia’s Foreign Legions.
The “national regiment” foolishness was
consummated last week, when the Federal
Executive Council approved of the retention
of Scottish, English and Irish regiments as
“extra territorial” units of the Australian
army. “Uniforms distinct from the Austra-
lian khaki” are to be worn, and, in general,
a strenuous attempt is to be made to create
a distinct caste in the Commonwealth’s land
forces. Thus does Australia deliberately
saddle itself with a system which British
military experts have for long recognised
to be one of the curses of their own army.
And it does worse than that. The new ar-
rangement must tend to foster one of the
most undesirable spirits that can afflict a
young country which is still in the process
of struggling towards nationhood. The
Anglo-Indian is an easily understandable
variety of citizen. India is a colored man’s
country, whether it is the India of the frozen
hills or the India of the tropical plains.
The Englishman goes there frankly to ex-
ploit it and its inhabitants, in one way or
another, and effect his get-away as soon
thereafter as may be. But the case of Aus-
tralia is different. It is a white man’s land,
and if it is’ to have any national future
worth mentioning it wants citizens who are
whole-hearted Australians first, last and all
the time. The Anglo-Australian sentiment
is going to be as disastrous in time of inter-
national trouble to the Commonwealth, if it
is fostered-40 any extent, as the Anglo-Irish
feeling is and always has been to Ireland.
There is no surer way of fostering it than
by foisting on the Australian Army Scottish,
English and Irish regiments, whose mem-
bers have been required “to, prove that they
are substantially of English, Scottish or
Irish descent.” The greatest enemy of the Aus-
tralian defence movement has all along been
the identical spirit which is bound up in the
formation of “national regiments.” The
authors of the Dreadnought movement were
only Dreadnoughters because they imagined
that they saw in Dreadnausea a chance to
kill the Australian Navy scheme. Scratch
an anti-“conscriptionist” and you will in-
fallibly find a passionate adherent of the
principle of “national regiments.” Scratch
him some more and you will discover the
facts that he is violently anti-Australian;
that he considers it to be like Australia’s
insolence to give Britain the lead in the
matter of compulsory service; and that if
lie had his way the country would revert
speedily to the voluntary arrangement. Any-
thing that encourages this sort of calamity
is plainly detrimental to that ideal of ' unity
which every Australian should strive-after.
The “national regiment” system does it
most palpably. It not only tends to make
ordinary citizens foreigners in their own
country, but it actually makes foreigners of
Australia’s own compulsorily-trained sol-
diers. It is a reasonable contention that a
country which is good enough for a man to
settle in, and make a living in and bring up
his family in, is good enough for the same
man to adopt, and that unreservedly. The
person who is careful to announce himself
some variety or other of British-Australian
is but half a patriot at best. While inter-
national affairs run smoothly he is innocu-
ous enough. But when difficulties arise he
automatically pushes to the front, and ren-
ders them more acute. And when he is a
member of the national defence forces he
may be in a position—as that distinguished
anti-Irish soldier, General Gough, proved in
Britain the other day—to render the diffi-
culties of the moment very acute indeed.
Ruinous Reticence.
Melbourne Argus didn’t exactly publish
the following warning remarks the other
day:—
In a certain district, or bailiwick, or burgh of
Victoria (or it might be N. S. Wales) there exists a
person who is a menace to the whole of Australia.
The district need not be mentioned (on second
thoughts it may be neither in Vic. or N.S.W., but
in S.A. or Queensland or even conceivably Westralia
or Tasmania) ; nor would it quite do to indicate
the offender’s name, or age, or sex, or general
appearance. Suffice it to say that he is a standing
danger to the entire nation. He secretly murders
thousands of people. None of them deserve the
dreadful tortures to which he puts them ; no human
being ever fairly earned the agonies to which many
of these sufferers have to submit. As a matter of
fact, a considerable proportion of his victims don’t
merit any punishment at all. They are good and
innocent women, or small children. Some he drives
mad—but for him our great asylums would be half
empty—others he afflicts with appalling nervous
complaints and skin diseases. We (in common
with the rest of the daily papers) know the name
and address of this menace to the welfare and happi-
ness of the State. We could direct the attention of
the authorities to him, and presently have him
grappled with and utterly exterminated, if we felt
that way. But he is a horror of old standing.
Furthermore, convention has laid it down that his
doings are not to be discussed by really polite
people. The attitude of the Genteel is that he
doesn’t exist —though there is scarcely a solitary
Genteel home that lacks at least one of his victims.
We have known him so long, that we have almost
ceased to fear him —or at any rate „we fear him in-
comparably less than we do a mild attack of small-
pox. And, so, day by day, he contirtu.es to destroy
valuable lives and spread unspeakable misery
throughout the land, while we (in common'with the
rest of the dailies) give him the moral support of
our silence. Even if we deplore his ravages we do
it so guardedly that few can understand who or .what
it is we are rebuking. But mostly we say nothing
at all, and he pursues bis march of devastation un-
checked.
The Argus didn’t put it in . just
those words. But in Dr. B* Bubnett
Ham’s article on Brieux’s recently produced
play, “Damaged Goods,” which it printed
(5/4/’l4), it got there just the same.
Brieux’s play is in effect a sermon on syp-
hilis. Any comment on it that is less out-
spoken than the play itself must be not only
foolish but quite valueless. The Argus,
for the benefit, presumably, of its more gen-
teel readers, has so sub-edited Dr. HAM.as to
make his otherwise useful contribution quite
futile and uninformative. As his article
appears it would almost take a member of
the medical profession to appreciate what he
is driving at. In the production of which
he writes, a message from the author was
read to the audience to the effect that the
play they were about to witness was a study
of the disease of syphilis in its bearing on
marriage. The Argus makes no mention of
this most striking episode. Not once, in the
course of a column-long dissection of the
play, is the name of the disease, on which
the work is based, mentioned. It is referred
to as “the scourge,” and so on; but as to
indicating it in the same spirit of healthy
frankness that people do typhoid or influenza
—well, the Argus wouldn’t dream of it.
Syphilis is as secure from assault as the
South Pacific Ocean so long as this miser-
able spirit of reticence is abroad. The ig-
norant, who form such a huge majority, both
of the rich and the poor classes, will never
learn to fear syphilis till it i§ dragged out
into the sunlight and shown to . them. And
till a majority of the electors' know enough
about this complaint to dread it, it will never
be tackled in a serious spirit by the politi-
cians. Why can’t widely-circulated papers
such as the Argus desist from comporting
themselves in this regard, like prurient-
minded old maids? The health of a nation
is hanging in the balance.
The Man Who Couldn’t Get Work in
Orange.
Prom The Bulletin’s Homeless Contri-
butor :
A melancholy person- who puts an end to himself
with a bullet from a revolver is not always the best
witness regarding motive; yet he ought to know
something about it. At any rate, he is interested
in the matter, and has a right to be heard. Thus a cer-
tain amount of respect should be paid to the statement
of William Ellis, deceased, whose body was found
on the Spring Hill-road, near Orange (N.S.W.), a
few. days ago. The late Mr. Ellis left this message
to the world at large: “I have tried to get work in
this town, and cannot. I am now at the mercy of


this world. I camped out last night, hard up and
3 What sort of work William could do, and with
what degree of capability he did it, I do not know;
but the world is full of Williams of some sort who
want work, and work that wants some sort of
Williams, and a job that it bungles most horribly
is the job of bringing these two together. .
Labor agency is confused, formless, disorganised,
a:.d chaotic. It depends mostly on the scramble of
Private Enterprise, to understand which you should
go to the ant. From the habits of that over-boomed
insect you will perceive that 10 ants or 20 ants will
worry one small crumb of bread and jam, while a
dozen other crumbs lie round the vicinity with not
one feeler testing them. If a man is looking for a
billet in a city he pads the hoof from one registry
office to another, and has to make a search through
20 lists where one list ought to suffice for every-
body. As the shoal of work-seekers drifts along, it
often happens that a score of men are battling for the
same job at the same dingy office, while nine or 19
other jobs are lacking applicants for the time
being. Throw chopped liver into a trout pool at
the fish hatchery, and every fish in the water leaps
for the first crumb that hits the surface. The loafer
is as likely as the quickest trout to get the last and
best lump. That is what is called Looking for
Work. It is beautifully anti-Sosh, and typical of
all anti-Soshism.
There is no reason why a good, healthy instalment
of practical Socialism should not be given to the
workman at once. Apart from the Government labor
agencies which ought to exist, a central registration
of jobs and job-seekers is the easiest thing in the
world. All that is required is a telephone, and a
very few clerks in a big central office in the city.
Every employment agent should be compelled to
notify the central office, within an hour at most,
what jobs have been registered with him, or what
men and women have left their names \yith him as
seeking work. If anybody chooses to register at the
central bureau, let him do it. There is not much
consideration due to those industrial go-betweens,
the employment agents, many of whom play dirty
thief-tricks on the unfortunate devil out of work.
At present the employment system is a system with-
out headquarters, a condilion in which there can
be no efficiency.
In the country, search for employment is even
harder, for the distances are longer and even the
chaotic Private Enterprise job-middleman is often
missing. On the other hand, the distress of being
unemployed is, as a rule, not so bitter as in the city.
The normal way of finding a billet in the country
is to tramp from township to township until you
get it. Some unhappy blokes fall into the despond-
ency which afflicted the late Mr. Ellis, who shot
himself because there was no work for him in the
town of Orange. If William had been able to look
at a district register, would his end have been any
different? Nobody can say what would have hap-
pened to William, who is beyond supplementary ex-
planations. But it is certain that five-sixths of the
hungry anxiety of looking for work would he saved
by the simple device of keeping a classified register
at a central bureau of information in every town of
size. The thing has become as necessary as a post
office. Such a centre might prevent a future
William from blowing out his brains because he
couldn’t get employment just round the corner, and
beyond the corner was chaos.
Of course there are hopeless Williams who are
quite unfit for jobs. But they are largely a product
of the system.

The Horrible Discordance of Liberal
Organs.
Let us say that “Liberalism” in the Com-
monwealth is fairly represented by Mel-
bourne Age, which practically pioneered the
Protection policy hereabouts. Between the
most advanced progressives and the deep-
rooted Tories the Age moves midway, always
looking back to see that it advances no fur-
ther than midway. Therefore when the Vic.
organ of Liberalism girds at the existing
system of Party Government its contradic-
tions of itself are less humorous to reflective
readers than the clashing of its ideas with
those of the Tory press, which also works for
the “Liberal” party at election times. Thus
t lie Age, referring to the British Constitu-
tional situation: —
Under the present Party system the Cabinet is all
powerful. It controls its own followers as despoti-
cally as it controls the House of Commons. We have
it on the authority of private members of that
House, that they have no more hope of influencing
events than Mrs. Partington had of keeping back the
Atlantic tides with a broom. A private member
cannot and dare not touch the smallest detail of
Cabinet policy lest the party machine be infracted.
Professor Morgan says: “Cabinet Government as we
know it is the outcome of the Party system, which
tfcfcjiay means the caucus, and I doubt if any of the
devices of political mechanics, such as proportional
representation, will dislodge it from its supremacy.”
All this is leading up to the prediction that
Second Chambers, no matter whether Austra-
lian senators or British peers, are the occu-
pants—must inevitably be swept away by
the unwieldy broom of common sense, a pre-
diction with which Australian Laborites
would concur if the Federal Senate hap-
pened to have an anti-Labor majority. In
the individual facts, as well as in the Age’s
clumsily evolved argument, the contradic-
tions of our “Liberal” party journalism are
apparent. The old Tory side of it believes in
an Upper House or Chamber of Review as
steadfastly as it believes in Private Enter-
prise and freehold propputy, and hates the
word “Socialism.” Again the Tory side of
Liberalism declares that- the essential evil
of Labor Government is in Caucus control.
“The Cancer of the Caucus” is the disease
that afflicts the Commonwealth, according to
Tory-Liberal Senator McColl. The Fisher
Ministry was contemptible because the
Caucus shaped the policy, and no member
of the Labor party ever shouted with the
Opposition, said the Argus. But here is
the Age quoting sympathetically those
British M’s.P. who complain that “the Cabi-
net is all powerful” in British Government.
Private, members have no hope of influencing
events in the smallest degree. Clearly this
hopeless weakness of private members in
Britain must be due to their lack of Caucus
system, seeing that a Labor Caucus, com-
prising all the private members of the party,
cauld control a Labor Cabinet in Australia.
But the Age quotes approvingly the moan
of an English professor to the effect that all
Cabinet Government to-day is the outcome
of the Caucus system, and must always re-
main so as 1 ing as there are Parties in Par-
Thus we have a representative
organ of Liberalism uttering opinions and
suggestions as follows :
(1) The Cabinet is all powerful in Party politics.
Private members have no influence whatever, there-
fore they do not fairly represent their constituencies.
(2) Cabinet Government is the result of the
Party system, which means the Caucus.
(3) Second Chambers (commonly known as Upper
Houses) should be abolished.
On the other hand, the Tory press which
supports Jokook Government says that when
Labor is in power :
(1) The Caucus dictates the policy of the Cabinet
and every private member is a force in the Caucus.
(2) The Caucus is controlled by Labor organisa-
tions outside Parliament, which represent the rank
and file of the party, therefore the Caucus is an
abomination because it comprises all the private
members who do fairly represent their constituencies.
(3) Upper Houses are the soul of good Government
and the country would go to the dogs if they were
abolished. But when a Senate contains 80 p.c. of
Labor members, the position is “intolerable.”
The one sure conclusion to be drawn from
the din of conflicting anti-Labor utterances
is that party politics are to blame for it.
Party politics was the natural cause, not
the effect, of party journalism—there were
Whigs and Tories in England long before the
voters had newspapers to talk to them —and
“Liberal” party journalism in Australia is
about the most maddening product of poli-
tics that ever came to pass. Worse than the
arguments of cats on the tiles.
Still with the Enemy.
Some more of the old unpalatable news
from Sydney Sun’s Melbourne correspondent
(5/4/’l4) :
Mr. Holman not only does most of the talking [at
the Premiers’ Conference] amongst Ministers, he
leads them in their opposition to Federal encroach-
ments.
The Premiers’ Conference to-day resolved that to
terminate Federal and State Savings Bank compe-
tition the State Governments would he willing to allow
the Savings Bank deposit now held by the Common-
wealth Bank to remain on fixed deposit with the
Commonwealth Bank, and to become responsible” to
the Savings Bank or otherwise for the redemption
of such deposits, and further to undertake at their
earliest convenience and as far as possible to trans-
act all State banking business with the Common-
wealth Bank. All this to be done in consideration
of the Commonwealth Bank withdrawing from
Savings Bank business. Westralia dissented from
the resolution.
The question of the appointment of State Gov-
ernors was raised at the Premiers’ Conference to-day
by Mr. Scaddan (W.A.). He advocated that eminent
citizens of the States should be eligible for the
positions.
Scaddan pointed out that when Westralia
had requested the British Government that
Australians should be eligible as State
Governors the reply was that no action
could be taken without uniformity among
the States. Finding the Conference against
him on the general question, Scaddan moved
that there was no call for any expression of
opinion on the part of Australians gener-
ally, or by the collective State Governments
—that is, if any State desired to appoint
one of its own citizens to be its purely local
Governor it should have the right to nego-
tiate with the British Government re such
appointment without consulting other
States. And, again, Westralia was the only
State that voted in favor of so much of a
Democratic proposal. Yet, the Labor party
is distinctly in favor of locally-grown Gov-
ernors ; also, Holman is Labor’s eastern
representative on the Conference. Let’s see:
First, there was Holman’s scarcity on
Labor’s side at the first Referenda fight;
then there was the duck to England from the
second Referenda; continually, the borrow-
ing and sinking-fund plank in the platform
is being guyed; and Labor’s delegate (not
its dictator) in N.S.W. is again busy lead-
ing the Tory State Frights ; and goes after
the Australian Labor party’s national sav-
ings bank with an axe in the van of the
said Labor party’s pledged enemies; and
throws his weight behind the Snobocracy
which makes the imported Excellency a
burden to the land. Now, how long does
Labor propose to make a platform and leg-
up Holman to foul it?
The Smallest Ex. and the Size Thereof.
A personage who seems likely to shake up
this corner of the earth in his own way is
the local Governor of Tasmania —by com-
plete title and designation, the Right Hon-
orable Sir William Grey Ellison Mac-
artney, with a tail of letters streaking like
a demented comet in pursuit of his
name. Willi AM-wi th-all-the-other-names-and-
the-demented-comet recently met with the
quietest sort of a political crisis. It was
supposed that the election for the Tas-
manian House of Assembly had produced
a deadlock, and that 15 members followed
Premier Solomon, the Tory, while another
15 followed Opposition-leader Earle, the
Laborite. As it proved, there was
no deadlock, for Wiiitsitt, who was
a sort of Independent, ranged him-
self with Earle instead of Solomon.
Solomon asked for a dissolution in a formal
kind of way, and, being refused, he went
out like a gentleman, or a bloke, or some-
thing. Earle tried to come in like a gentle-
man, or a cove, or something; but found his
Excellency-—the very smallest Ex. of the
Commonwealth —in his road. Apparently
because Earle was a Labor man, or for
some other reason, the very smallest Ex. in
the Commonwealth thought fit to make
special demands on him. These are re-
ported to have been as follows: (1) That
the perfectly new Parliament should be dis-
solved immediately, though why it should be
dissolved so long as the Earle Ministry had
a majority it is hard to say, and a majority
of one is a good enough majority if the
party attends regularly; and (2) that “in
the event of the office of Attorney-General
not being filled by a fully qualified lawyer in
practice, the Governor must reserve the
right to retain legal advice, when he con-
siders it necessary, from other sources.”
This stipulation suggests that his Ex. is
the limit and the dead finish and the ulti-
mate possibility. The Earle party is
notoriously deficient in legal talent, for pro-
minent lawyers are usually Tories. But if
the party has to put up some nominal sort
of lawyer as Attorney-General, it is plainly
Premier Earle’s business, and hot the Gov-
ernor’s, to hire an outside legal expert—who
may be called Crown Solicitor or Solicitor-
General or any old thing—to advise on tech-
nical questions. The proposal that the Gov-
ernor —the smallest X in the Commonwealth
—should appoint a casual or variable or
shifting legal adviser, or fleeting Attorney-
General, who would really amount to a
transient member of the Ministry—should
appoint him on his own motion, or perhaps
with the advice of the aide-de-camp, or the
nursemaid, or the butler, or the sentry on
duty at the front gate—was beyond all
reason. And the limitations of his Smallest
Ex. appeared in this statement: —
It is important to bear in mind that the discre-
tion of the Governor with regard to the question of
dissolution, as in other instances of the exercise of
prerogative, is much wider in the COLONIES than
that upon which, by constitutional practice, the
Sovereign acts in the United Kingdom. It is im-
possible, as one of the most recent authorities on
government in the DOMINIONS has pointed out, to
maintain the position that the Governor is parallel
to the Sovereign in constitutional monarchy, and
that, therefore, he is obliged to act on the advice of
his Ministers in the same sense in which the King of
the United Kingdom acts on the advice of his Minis-
ters.
Leaving out the involved constitutional ques-
tion, which seems to be material for inter-
minable argument, it is worth while to note
that his Smallest Ex. has apparently failed
to master the simple terms employed in his
very simple business. First he talks of “the
colonies” (with a small “c”). Tasmania is
a State, not a Colony. He discourses about
“the Dominions” (with a big “D”). Tas-
mania is a State, not a Dominion (with a
big “D” or a small one). And then he
alludes to some potentate whom he calls
“the King of the United Kingdom.” It
seems rather a crude, and sudden abbrevia-
tion, but no doubt his Smallest Ex. means
Well. Still, as he has very little to do save
pour out at full length his gracious sove-
reign’s full pomp and dignity, he need not be
quite so
The Latest Income-tax Mystery.
The present N.S.W. Government’s crazy
hatred of co-operative enterprise, and its
crazy preference for the big. solitary capital-
ist, yvere evidenced once- more in its newest 5
Income-tax Bill. The company gets none of
the exemptions which apply to the lone pirate
on the high seas 'b'V'lommerce or finance. It
may be a very small affair, showing a profit
of £5OO a year, to be divided- among a num-
ber of shareholders, while the person who
cruises on his own may make a great deal
more than that single-handed. Yet, all the
same, the company doesn’t get the exemption
of £250 which is allowed to the individual
capitalist, and its rate doesn’t start at Sd. in
the f, but is Is. all round. Possibly none
of the members of the Holman Ministry
invests, much in companies—some of them
are said to be land dealers and property
owners on a big scale, but they seem to be
rather an aloof and lonesome crowd in busi-
ness matters —so they may not know much
about this particular line of industry. Still
their bitter hatred of co-operation in busi-
ness seems over the odds. They don’t aim
specially at the huge company which
threatens to develop into a trust or a com-
bine or a monopoly, and thus become a pub-
lic danger; if 10 humble Australians get
together to run an incorporated butter-box-
making enterprise in a shed they are struck
just as hard as if they were the Colonial
Sugar Refining Co. or the Bank of New
South Wales. Yet if, some day, another
Government follows up this idea with a tax
of Is. in the £ on the revenues of trades
unions, which also represent the co-operative
idea, Holman and Co. will probably become
very wild indeed. A notable feature of the
new taxation is that the special tax on the
absentee capitalist has been abolished, the
love of the Holman Ministry for the rich
absentee being apparently greater than that
of Jonathan for David. And, a serious
attempt was made to enact that profits made
from sales of N.S.W. property should be
taxable if the property-jobber lived in
N.S.W., but not otherwise. This proposi-
tion was abandoned when the Bill was before
the Upper House, annarently because the
Government couldn’t face the storm of deri-
sion which it aroused, but the fact that it
should ever have been in the Bill suggested
a strangely inverted frame of mind. Seeing
that N.S.W. is to have the highest income-
tax in Australia, it would plainly pay the
whole crowd of land-jobbers, under this
scheme, to remove their head offices to Mel-
bourne or Brisbane, and nominally job in
N.S.W. lands from there, so that they might
pay a very small tax outside N.S.W. instead
of a big one inside. If they didn’t adopt
this scheme, the old-established Melbourne or
Brisbane or Adelaide property jobbers would
have a chance to annex their business by the
simple process of under-selling them. The
income-tax propositions have been a muddled
and mixed set of devices from first to last.
The keynote to some of the anomalies seems
to be that the Holman Government is a
frenzied dealer in foreign loans, and is strug-
gling all the time to placate the outside per-
son —any and every variety of outside per-
son-—who looks as if he might have a little
money to lend. Its efforts to propitiate the
outside person who looks as if he might have
money about him ipay be clumsy and inco-
herent, but they are certainly earnest. The
war which Holman and Co. wage so per-
sistently, and wholly without explanation,
against N.S.W. co-operative enterprise, is
another thing. It can’t be imagined that all
the fuss arises because some Minister’s first
wife’s mother is married to the manager of a
wool-scouring company at Dandaloo, or any-
thing of that humble sort, so the personal
element-doesn’t come in. It may be simply
an incompetent desire to save trouble. The
Victorian Legislature did that sort of thing
once, when, being too exhausted to compile a
list of noxious weeds, it passed an Act
ordering landholders to extirpate thistles, and
added that any Government might, at its
discretion, if it had such a thing, declare
almost anything a thistle. Thus the Vic-
torian House of Rich Landlords may one day
be declared a thistle. In like manner, it
may be, the Holman Government, being un-
able to define a trust or an injurious
mononol.y, saves itself brain-fag by hitting all
companies alike.

A Lame Duck on Wheels.
Years ago, when the Melbourne land boom
was young, and overdrafts hadn’t become
bloated, certain sanguine capitalists built
what they called a tram-line from Sandring-
ham to Cheltenham, round a slab of the bay
shore, past Blaekrock, Beaumaris, Quiet
Corner, Linger Longer and other sweetly-
named spots. The contraption possibly paid
at one time, though that is doubtful, but for
long it has been a losing proposition. The
prads or mokes are dilapidated scarecrows,
the vehicles are ramshackle affairs that hang
together by the grace of God and a piece of
string, and the track is like unto the track
of the mastodon as he wandered through the
primeval forest. It is such a hopeless
wreck, as well as a danger, that Moorabbin
Council, which foolishly gave away its street
rights long ago, despairs of ever getting the
company that owns it to do anything, and has
asked the Government to investigate the case
with a view to getting the incubus shifted.
The inouiry isn’t finished yet, but it is
humorsome as far as it has gone. In many
places the rails don’t play speaks with the
sleepers, and often they are kept more or
less together by billets of wood. The gauge
varies from 4ft. BJin. to 4ft. lOin. (The ob-
vious tendency towards a broad gauge is
commendable, but somewhat insecure.)
Naturally the cars leave the tracks whenever
they think fit, and then the driver addresses
the passengers with, “All out and bear a
hand,” and the aged hearse is shoved on the
rails once again—only to fall off a chain
further on. Some of the cars have no brakes ;
in others the w’heels are almost worn away,
and nbiie have been painted since the year
Two —which is a year after the One they
started in. The concern has become, in fact,
a dilapidated joke and a nuisance, but Pri-
vate Enterprise still keeps the funeral crawl-
ing. It has a monopoly of that beach road.
Some day the citizens will demand electric
traction, and then the franchise will become
a marketable property. It will have to be
bought out, at great expense. The investi-
gation has been adjourned for a week, to
give the company a chance to “consider the
evidence,” but if it is wise it will do nothing
of the sort. Instead, it will hold an auction
sale of its threadbare horses, its dry-rotten
cars and its switchback track, and buy a
brief hour’s oblivion with the proceeds.

The Very Reverend Arch-beditor apolo-
gises :—■
In my recent notices of “Joseph and His Brothers,”
I fear, my friends, that I have unwittingly done an
injustice to the seating accommodation of the Sydney
Theatre Royal.
Owing to the fact that the management considered
that a seat in the extreme corner of the dress circle,
and not even a scat-, but a chair that does not leave
room for archbeditorial legs, was the one most suit-
able to a critic of this paper, I have perhaps unduly
dwelt upon the impossibility of any person, clerical
or even lay, seeing any more than one-third of the
stage, and the, as far as I could gather, quite in-
nocuous play-acting thereon. I have said too much
about seat No. 59a. Moreover, I notice that an
artist has even drawn a picture of the view from that
seat, though I venture in all deference to suggest
that from my memory of the successive ladies in the
box, whose back view occupied so much of my front
view, she was much more charming than the artist
has drawn her. In thus saying so much about 59a,
I have done a regrettable injustice to seat la. This
seat, situated on the prompt side, exactly oppo-
site 59a, is just as bad. In fact, dear brethren, it
is worse. For from it the occupant has no view of
the temptation scene. Yet, in fairness to the man-
agement of the Theatre Royal, I am pleased to point
out that a member of the public, by booking these
seats, 1a and 59a, on successive nights, may get a
fairly good idea of what is happening on at least
two-thirds of the stage. That is, of course, if the
back view of whatever lady occupies the box is not,
in itself, more fascinating than the brown hand-
maiden nearest the footlights on the opposite side.
For those who want a play there is only
one theatre open just now in Sydney. For
those, that is, who do not desire pantomimes,
or American farces, or American musical
comedies, or Egyptian melodramas. At
the Little Theatre Reginald Wykeham’s
production of Pinero’s famous success, “The
Gay Lord Quex,” cleverly surmounts the
apparently insuperable difficulties of the
smallness of the stage at the Doll’s House.
The second act, the scene in an Italian
garden, by the Clints, suggests space in an
ingenious way; and though the third act,
the celebrated bedroom scene —indispensable
in all modern comedies —is slightly cramped,
the new setting of this scene is admirable.
The plot shows a reformed rake, in love
with the usual “creamy English girl.” He has
hapnily disposed of his former mistresses,
and has to be good in order to win the nice
girl. But one of his many “pasts,” a ro-
mantic duchess, insists on a farewell meet-
ing and a romantic good-bye. Naturally
enough, she arranges for this meeting to
take place in her boudoir at midnight.
Sophy Fullgarney, a common, good-hearted
manicurist, intervenes to save Muriel,
Quex’s fiancee, front the reformed monster.
She is caught looking through the keyhole
of the duchess’s door. Quex sends the duch-
ess out of the room, and, summoning Sophy,
locks the doors. Either the lady is to pro-
mise silence, or to be found on Lord Quex’s
premises in the morning, and have the ruins
of her good name carried out on a shovel.
The interest of this scene, which is prac-
tically the play, is heightened by all the
brilliant stage-craft of Pinero at his best.
Having got his “curtain,” the dramatist is
left with an improbable fourth act in which
to bring about the unconvincing happy end-
ing. As Sophy Fullgarney, Violet Paget
cleverly stresses the essential commonness
and the. equally essential kindliness of the
manicurist. It is a performance which,
though on a lower level than that of Mrs.
Brough at her best, is thoroughly convincing.
Esther Mitchell, as the “creamy English girl,”
gives a highly attractive performance, which
is a promise of even better things. Lilian
Lloyd is rather overweighted in the part
of the romantic duchess ; but her conception
of a difficult part is a quite probable one.
Tempe Pigott is a charming aristocrat. In
a comedy part of Sir Chichester Fraque
Arthur Cornell scores. The various young
girls at the twitter nicely.
The Elliott Savonas, aided and abetted by
numerous electric lights, blue costumes, a gor-
geous setting, and no end of musical instru-
ments or tools whose names this, writer
knows not, provide the most elaborate musi-
cal turn the Sydney Tivoli has lassoed for
a very considerable while. Samtern, a
Hebrew-Dago-French-English comedian. YV.
G. Fields, whose silent humor is delightful;
Bosa Roma, the violinist; and Vallecita’s
jeopards all help to make up an entertaining
programme.
The Bulletin’s Wild Kitten, who is
also its Circus Professor —being a person
closely acquainted with bears, wild cats and
bulls —reports that Wirths’ Circus No-Lia-
bility Co. has struck another gold mine at
Prince Alfred Park, Sydney, and its under-
ground manager states that if the crowd
which turns up nightly develops any further
round the middle level, the tent won’t hold
them. The casli-box is panning out at 255.
to the pound.

“The Tenderfoot,” which opeued at Syd-
ney Adelphi on Saturday night, is described
as a musical comedy, but the musical part
of it almost drowns the comedy, and the
comedy, in its turn, practically smothers the
love yarn that runs through the play. In-
cluding several dances—each of which goes
off excellently—there are 25 musical num-
bers, and that, in a play of three acts,
sounds like overcrowding. Moreover,
though the choruses are distinctly good, the
solos, with one or two exceptions, are weak.
But it is quite evident that the company
includes some good people, who, with a less
musical comedy, would go splendidly. The
plot is based on the visit of a particularly
green professor to a Mexican town. There
he falls in love with an authoress ; likewise
the lady who owns the ranch falls in love
with a colonel, and by the time the show is
over everyone on the programme is in love
with everyone else. The strong point of the
whole show is the delightful way in which
Professor Zachary Pettibone' (Carrick
Major) deals out the humor. Pettibone is
the “tenderfoot” professor, and his mirth
keeps the whole thing moving; and besides
that he is one of the few exceptions to the
above remark about weak solos. Miss
“THE TENDERFOOT," AT THE
ADELPHI, SYDNEY.
The tenderfoot Professor (Carrick Major)
and the authoress (Grace Ellsworth) do the
“Dove and Crow ” dance.
Myrtle Jersey as Marion, the owner of the
ranch and the heroine of the piece, though
she would no doubt have a splendid voice
for a smaller theatre, is lost in the Adelphi,
and at times is quite inaudible. Bobby
YVoolsey, as Sergeant Barker, second in
command of. the foolery department, relies
mainly on his feet for his effects, and they
generally see him through. In addition
his faculty for tying his legs into fancy
knots helps him to score in the dances.
George Bogues, as the colonel, the hero and
the man who marries the heroine, passes a
pleasant evening making love with a rather
fine tenor voice and looking pretty in the
Texas Rangers uniform. Miss Grace Ells-
worth, as Flora Jane Fibbey, the authoress;
Miss Shirley Huxley (a local girl), as Sally
the maid ; and Miss Eva Olivotti, as Patsy,
each make a hit in their respective comedy
parts. The theatre on Saturday night
was as full as it could hold, and the assort-
ment of vegetable matter handed over the
footlights at the tail end of the show would
have stocked a flower shop.

If animals have the good sense to refuse to be
