a vigorous protest.
The management of the Imperial Dancing
Salon, or, as it chooses to label itself, the
Imperial Salon de Luxe, has drawn up its
code of ballroom ethics for the benefit of
Sydney’s Elite, etc. A few extracts
Gentlemen will on absolutely no occasion speak to,
or otherwise approach in the slightest way, any lady
to whom they have not been previously introduced.
Any lady having the slightest cause to complain
in this regard has only to inform any of our staff,
and without bringing her name or person into the
affair at all we will immediately eject the offender.
Only those dances which have received the sanc-
tion of polite society will be presented to our patrons.
Those only who are known to, or supposed by the
management to be acceptable to a majority of our
patrons, will be admitted. The most strict decorum
will be enforced at all times. The barriers which
separate one stranger from another will . be rigidly
maintained.
The Methodist Conference has given its ap-
proval of these safeguards, but has conceded
to the committee of public safety that in the
supper rooms a strange gentleman may re-
quest an unknown lady to pass the pickles,
provided two waiters are within earshot.
The chapel will be opened for service on
Monday evening, April 20 (invitation night).
Allen Doone came back to Erin, or as
much of it as could assemble in the Palace
(Sydney) on Saturday night. Talk about
McCarthy or any other parthy—they weren’t
in it with Doone when he wafted on stage
behind the smile he keeps for special occa-
sions. The play was “The Wearing of the
Green,” which —but no. Untamed jaguars
will tear no more description of that trivi-
ality from Tiie Bulletin. It was the same
piece, and, as far as the paper remembers,
the identical co.; likewise the acting was
similar and the scenery and the prices of ad-
mission were a dead beat with those in opera-
tion last time the show was in Sydney:—or
maybe it was the time before, or some other
occasion. Anyhow, everybody seemed satis-
fied, except the meagre usher who was
trampled underfoot in the scramble at the
entrance for the last vacant seat in the
house.
Sydney’s White City has been indulging
in a sort of country week, the great Bluegum
family having attended in encouraging num-
bers,. to get new thrills from the London
Door Mystery, the Fun Factory, the Aerial
Flight, the Crystal Tangle (not so good a
brand of tangle as they have at the Shearers’
Arms, Wayout, but not bad), the Scenic
Railway, the Caroussel and 50 others. xYn-
other carnival is due on the 22nd inst.
“The Poolroom,” Fred C. Hagan’s serinon-
ette from U.S. (whence the wise guys come),
is still the plaster at the National Amphi-
theatre (Sydney). Other features on the
stagescape are Carl Bentzen and Phyllis
Lawton, who cavort mightily in freak sara-
bands. The also-starters needn’t be • down-
hearted. Worse folk than them are at
large.
Adelaide’s reconstructed Royal got itself
open on Easter Saturday night, manager
Tallis being over to see the event through.
Pitt, of Sydney, was the architect, and the
contractor had three shifts of men on it
night and day for two months. Little has
been done to the stage end, but the audi-
torium is (of course) the best in Australia.
Comfort, ventilation and view-of-the : stage
are claimed to be the “limit.” “The Count
of Luxemburg” was the opening show. At
the Tivoli are tango experts and ragtime
experts, and lots of assorted dancing, and
the Homaz athletes. At the King’s are some
spectacular first-in-Australia Brennan-Fuller
turns that must be reserved for detailed
notice. The Dandies, who are in their last
open-air weeks, are announcing special pro-
grammes to compete with the Butterflies,
who, under Jack Waller, are already im-
mensely popular at the Town Hall, their
music being as good as their humor. West’s
Pictures are starring the Hodgson Burnett
tragedy, “A Lady of Quality,” but also take
care—as do Wondergraph and the rest —to
present early films of Oakbank Steeplechase.
Special chastened programmes were given
at most houses on Good Friday evening, but
the Tivoli was closed, and the King’s was
lent for a mission service.
A tender page from the history of a way-
back flapper, entitled “What Happened to
Mary,” was given a first reading at Mel-
bourne King’s last Saturday night. At the
outset of the proceedings Mary is living in
an atmosphere of slop clothes and decayed
fish on Moseses Island, where a calamity
named Peart and his comatose missus offi-
ciate as her uncle and aunt. Meanwhile an
aged mariner, by name Jogifer, acts as her
Trew Friend. Jogifer has a stiff leg and a
mass of Scriptural wisdom. In other words,
he has the imperceptiveness of a fragment
of cement. To his astonishment Mary clears
off to New York with dissolute young Henry
Craig. Henry proves a far too catholic-
minded lover for a girl who holds neither
with Mormonism nor with any of the various
nigger faiths that encourage polyandry.
Mary discovers Hen. telling the old, old tale
to a certain Miss Welch, and un-
hesitatingly hands him the bird. She thus
comes by the billet of typist to John Willis.
John proves presently that she is inno-
cent of theft and of all other charges;
also that she is a long-lost heiress.
What could any fair-minded girl do in the
circumstances but make the kindly fellow
responsible for her millinery bills? The
bellowdrama is in the hands of the Bert
Bailey co. Guv Hastings represents Willis
as one of those forceful, bustling persons
who go about banging doors and creating
needless draughts; Miss Elwyn Harvey’s
rendering of Mary is so charmingly modu-
lated that one doubts whether to accept it
as an impersonation or a piece of\music.
Bert Bailey seizes the role of JogifeXas a
pretext for humorous work, while Mary’s
deep-sea admirer, Tuck Wintergreen, ' is
merely Fred Macdonald’s Dave in “On Our
Selection,” republished, as it were, for the
Amurkan market. Edmund Duggan is
Craig senior, and sufficient unto the char-
acter is the Edmund thereof; Arthur Ber-
tram portrays a Yankee clerk ; while Craig
junior is played by It. Bellairs. Miss Bevan
as Mrs. Winthrop suffers somewhat from
colorlessness, but the other ladies are either
good or passable.

Full details of the latest “Tragedy in the
Air” were published at Melbourne Olympia
last Saturday. It tells how a beauteous
dancer went up in a balloon and got in the
way of a flash of lightning. “The Uprising
of Ann” explains how a little harmless phil-
andering came to a horrible end. “The
Reward” is a mild Amurkan story full of
bandits bind and bullets. Parts of this
gentle Sunday-school yarn suggest an abat-
toirs on a busy day.
Melbourne had a new experience at the
Princess’s last Saturday. A vaudeville show
rather more effectively staged and orches-
trated than the ordinary, but still a mere
vaudeville show, was produced as an enter-
tainment calling for rare occasional prices.
A programme of spven turns —a company of
10 people in all —and a tariff of admission
ranging from 3s. to 10s. Qd. The Lauder
attraction filled the house, of course, but
Lauder was the last item on the bill, there-
fore the co. was practically required to keep
the audience satisfied with its investment
until the star came along to “cow the
cuddy” or “tak the scone.” With one ex-
ception these previous turns were fairly
equal to their awful responsibility. The
exception was a nice-looking lady who sang
a doleful Irish ballad concerning “ochone”
or something to that effect. She was a
striking non-success in comparison with the
three Hassons (whose clever performance
on the old, old wire seemed somewhat wire
drawn), and Irene Bercsenys, described as
a Hungarian Gypsy Cymbalist. Irene’s
cymbal is a mysterious instrument of the
harpsichord pattern, and she smites it with
toy drumsticks what time an eccentric gent,
in false saveloy ringlets, ambles to and fro
the wings with a violin accompaniment.
Three earlier turns had shown a larger pro-
portion of unquestionable merit. Jack Ark’s
dexterity with a diabolo outfit explained
why diabolo had the shortest life of any
parlor-game craze. It was really a business
for experts rather than a foolishness for
amateurs. Selwyn Driver, talker, singer
and pianist, was briskly entertaining, like-
wise Ernest Sewell, who, after rushing
through a series of sleight-of-hand tricks,
puts liis own face through the back cloth
of a marionette stage, and supplements his
smile with a pair of active toy limbs. Both
Driver and Sewell claim to have made mon-
archs laugh under royal command circum-
stances, and the writer is glad to hear it.
Himself was much amused by their efforts.
Given a throne to sit upon, he also' would
command them royally.
Harry Lauder lasted for about an hour,
including short intervals for changing liis
garments; and, apart from the time spent
in asking the audience to sit for a flashlight
photo., the hour passed quickly enough.
The quaint, abbreviated comedian changes
his manner and facial expression along with
his clothes, and even his violently pro-
nounced nationality is not allowed to grow
monotonous. There is'no Caledonian color
to speak of in Lauder’s cleverest creation —
“The Saftest of the Family.” His ridicu-
lously funny burlesque of a soldier in a kilt
and top-boots is built on Cockney humor
lines. Lauder has several sides, though
only one pair of legs, to his broad, artistic
character. Other comedians have been more
cannily Scawtch, but none of them won
the hairt o’ the whole Mac Sandy clan as
he did, because none of them had the per-
sonality, the homely humanity that appeals

(STUART
GARNER).
The Ada Reeve season at Melbourne
Tivoli began on Saturday afternoon, when
bunches of flags were fastened to the
theatre’s waistband — i.e., the front of the
d.c.—to mark the event. Melbourne, especi-
ally the less unintelligent half of it, is going
to be very fond of Ada Reeve once again.
The dainty girl of a while ago has developed
into a charming woman. She retains all the
original freshness of her voice, sings a good
assortment of nice, clean songs without
changing her costume, and patters with re-
fined art. The new co. that leads up to
the monologues of Ada is headed by Tucker,
a “singing violinist,” who also dances whilst
he fiddles, and introduces female impersona-
tion into his unduly long turn. Tucker is
a versatile young man, but not remarkably
clever in any department of his universal
emporium. Gilday and Fox supply what
the advt. calls the last word in Jewish
comedy, which is not necessarily the best
word. Russell Carr brings the military
haw-haw note into ventriloquism, and feeds
his poor little dummy figure with obvious
opportunities for easy repartee; the Creos
are acrobats, and the Golden Atlilones
differ from all previous plastic poseurs in
being gilded. Their most effective attitudes
are struck on either side of a clock, and
their performance leaves one in doubt as
to whether they got themselves gilded for
life, or are in the habit of washing it off.

Melbourne Repertory Theatre will have
four seasons of Australian plays this year,
and these, as a matter of business necessity,
will be staged at St. Peter’s Hall, opposite
St. Pat’s Cathedral, on the Eastern Hill.
Gregan McMahon has learned from experi-
ence that the patriotism of the public in
general doesn’t yet run to the encouragement
of native dramatic literature, neither does
the patronage of the Very Best People tend
that way. The small literary circle which
will pay its half-crown for a night with
Australian authorship in Culture’s local
cause can be accommodated at St. Peter’s
Hall, a bright little building with a neat
staging equipment. The first performance,
on Saturday (25th), will introduce a curtain-
raiser—“La Femme Riante” —by Philip Ray
(Mrs, M. M. Philips) and a three-act play
by Alfred Buchanan, of the Age. Gregan
McMahon reckons that the double bill should
go very well, judging from rehearsal results.
* * * a*
“0.K.”: Melbourne’s resident vaudeville
managements, being confronted by the pre-
sence of a rival Revue at Her Majesty’s
and the prospect of the Lauder Variety
Co. at the Princess’s, seemed to lose
their heads in their anxiety to make a splash.
Firstly, the Fuller-Brennan management
started twice-nightly performances with a
bright new co. at the Bijou. This experiment
(which had been imported by an unsuccess-
ful Yankee exploiter some years previously)
lasted until Easter Saturday, when a daily
matinfie' was substituted for the early evening
show. Meanwhile the Tivoli had advertised
a forthcoming introduction of the two-shows-
a-night system as soon as Ada Reeve and a
new co. got to work. Handbills distributed
the Week before Easter repeated the allega-
tion that Ada Reeve, “the highest salaried
lady performer in the universe” (this
was- stated quite regardless of the high sal-
aries paid in Jupiter and the reckless scale
of wages-on some of the asteroids), would
play to two audiences a night “in conse-
quence of the fabulous expenditure entailed,
etc., etc.” The Bulletin took the news-
paper advts. and the handbill at their word
and stated in last issue that the Tivoli was
to be a two-shows-a-night theatre for the
present. But next day the Tivoli advt.
stated with due emphasis that Only One
Performance Nightly would be given. Pro-
bably Mclntosh and Fuller were unneces-
sarily concerned about the recent invasion
of their vaudeville domain. The unusual
variety shows serve as advts. for the old
established ditto. Lauder and the Revue
are introducing disconnected “music-hall”
entertainment to lots of people who have
never gone to either of the local halls, and
are making that sort of show more familiar
to a public that has very rarely gone to a
hall. There was no occasion for the Tiv.
and the Bijou to make those Announcements
Extraordinary concerning their goods. The
fact is their sort of goods was never before
so fashionable as the recent competition has
made it.

Merivel of the Confi-

Newspaper interviews im-
ported mummers carry their mßmrdity on the
face of them, as a rule. But when the artist
talks of a previous visit to the country she
is about to “star” in, the yarn has historical
interest for many readers. Listen to Ada
Reeve, telling a Melbourne Herald reporter
of how she came to this country 14 years ago :
“Oh” (laying an impulsive hand on the arm of
the interviewer’s chair), “I have much, very much,
to thank Australia for. I will never, never forget
the first time I played in this country. It was in
Melbourne that I made my debut. I was but a
girl in my ’teens. As I walked on the stage they
gave me a splendid reception. Tears filled my eyes.
I said to myself, ‘You dear people, I like you, I will
try to deserve your praise.’
“And the more I played the more I liked the
people, and I honestly think they liked me. I left
Australia a favorite. I owe much of my success to
Australia. I had not been back in England six
months before I was earning more pounds than I
had previously earned shillings. I must have learned
something in Australia. I cannot say what it exactly
was, but it was something.”
The reference to pounds and shillings in
these reminiscences seems rather rough on
the Williamson and Musgrove management,
and the Rickards management of yore. The
“Firm” imported Miss Reeve, and when she
appeared at the Melbourne Princess’s in “The
Gay Parisienne” (November, 1897) she was
billed as a London actress who had played
the name part for “upwards of 300 nights
at the Duke of York Theatre.” Subsequently
she appeared in “The French Maid”- and
the Christmas panto., and in September, ’OB,
she was a principal turn at Rickards’s Opera
House, the rival principal being Peggy Pryde.
The contrast between the dainty, chic style
of Ada and the broad “what-ho” manner of
Peggy was very marked. After the engage-
ment with Rickards the little lady went back
to England, where, within six months, she
was earning more than 20 times as much
salary as she was paid in Australia, she
says. Query—what salaries did Ada Reeve
draw in Australia?
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Melb”: Howlers ever increased Show prices.
Williamson Ltd. state that the new log rates for
working staffs issued three years ago increased the
wage list over £15,000 a year, further increases
under two recent agreements will add £6OOO or
£7OOO, making total increase of over £21,000. I
expect the public to rise, with the baits, and justify
the stiffening. Lauder is pitched high, by the laits,
at 3s. for the cheapest seat. In London the ten-
dency is to go down. Lauder could be heard, at
South London Music Hall, for 5d., with cushioned
seat, and for 6d. at the Colosseum. Some halls give
cushioned seats for 2d., with good artists. Melba at
Covent Garden for 2s. 6d„ and in concerts for Is.
Some of these up-country showmen are not too
slow. T’other day the manager of the new Goulburn
Majestic hurried out 34 miles to meet Miss Waugh,
the girl who rode from Melbourne to Sydney bare-
back, and persuaded her to appear at his theatre the
same night. That was her only appearance on a stage
during the journey.
The allegations of Catts, M.P., re the influence of
his mother-in-law in his domestic affairs, remind one
of an American farcical comedy wherein George
Willoughby of yore played the troubled husband.
Said his young wife tearfully: “When we married*
vou promised that I should be your Queen. Cei-
tainly,” replied Willoughby, in his heartiest commer-
cial tone. “You were to be the Queen, and I was to
be the King; but we never agreed that your mother
was to come along and be the Ace.”
According to the U.S. press General Villa is having
flicker films made depicting his march from the Rio
Grande to the City of Mexico. He has a big interest
in the co. which is running the venture, and ex-
pects to do well, as two of the reels are already being
shown in N’York to vast houses. This gives Villa
the distinction of being the first man to realise the
possibilities of war from the legitimate business
standpoint. After all, the Mexican mix-up was
engineered merely to make a Noo Yark holiday!
From London: This city prides itself on being
immensely up to date, and for the revival of “The
Tyranny of Tears” (doing well under Ethel Irving, at
the Comedy), Haddon Chambers was bidden to look
through it and cut out any approach to a soliloquy or
an “aside.” Yet the artificial old “Diplomacy” is
having the success of its life —literally, for when
first produced in 1878 it ran only 325 times, which
number was passed weeks ago by its present run at
Wyndham’s. King George has sent for it to come to
Windsor Castle ; he has old-fashioned tastes, and has
personally selected “The Silver King” for this year’s
show in aid of the Actors’ Pension Fund, named
after himself. “Diplomacy” is now being toured in
the country, and in the big suburban theatres which
are such a feature of modern London. Marie Tem-
pest, also, is revivaling with her old success, “The
Marriage of Kitty.” So is Tree with “The Darling of
the Gods,” which he cynically told an interviewer
he had chosen because it was the nearest thing to a
revue he had in stock. The successful new plavs—
barring the musical—are Arnold Bennett’s “Great
Adventure,” and the American “Within the Law” —
foolishly translated into English. Both are well on
towards the 400-nights mark.
“Norbell”: I am willing to wager that fully a
ton and a half of the two tons of whiskerings
which disfigure Joseph’s brethren and friends were
imported. Foreign producers doubt Australia’s
ability to evolve even bird’s-nest effects. When Nat
Goodwin came here, he imported all his junk, and
the stage properties he paid freight on included
several bags of scrao-iron required for scattering
round the stage as vivid local color in a black-
smith’s shop scene.
The return of Dorothy Vane to the stage is seri-
ously predicted. It is said that when the Combine
revives “The Mikado” in the winter, the revival
will include the little lady who was imported to
Australia with “La Poupee” of yore. She left the
stage to become Mrs. Norman Menzies, of Melbourne’s
tony hostelry, and except for a very occasional
appearance at a charity mat. she has been off the
stage ever since.
Seems that the young man (Bellbridge) who played
the part of Floriot junior on the first night only of
“Madame X,” at Melbourne Royal, occupied the
claim of young man (Ward) who stood ready to go
en. Both of them were given the part to study •
after the original selection (Pearce) had fallen sick.
But Ward was number 1 understudy. Bellbridge being
expected to wait patiently for the other fellow to get
sick. The management had no suspicion of B.’s
splendid enthusiasm, or he would have been chained
up, no doubt. As it was, he dissembled his deep
intent until the moment for Ward’s entrance was
almost due, and then he rushed on, hurled himself
into the character and remained there to the bitter
end. It might not be usual but it was magnificent.
“0.K.”: A last week’s Poverty Pointer speaks of
Sarah Bernhardt having been as thin as a lath and
meatless as a bone 20 years ago. > Say 45 to 35
years ago. It was in 1891 that the Divine Sarah
came to Australia, and she was then pleasingly
plump, though not at all fat. The arms which she
stretched out like semaphores in acknowledgment of
her audience’s greeting were shapely winsome limbs,
quite capable of tender embrace. The days when it
was dangerous to be folded to her breast on account
of her sharpness all round had gone for ever in 1891.
Sarah Bernhardt, remember, is a Jewess with a
wonderful appetite for food. It used to be said
that she made nothing of eating 12, hard-boiled eggs
during a rehearsal at the theatre. Even her abnormal
nervous energy couldn’t keep her skinny after she
had turned five and thirty. -'*■
Are any pipers’ bands and receptions being pre-
pared for Ellen Terry, now en route to Australia?
Thusfar no glowing managerial announcements of
the imminent advent of Ellen have appeared
in Melbourne’s press, although it is understood
that her tour will be started in that city.
No resident representatives of showmanship
are concerned in it, seemingly. Hence the bursts of
silence up to now. But when Ellen Terr3' comes to
hand her name, doubtless, will make good. She
was a celebrity before Lauder was born, or any
other visiting “star” of the moment was thought of,
and she has kept her celebrity going all the time.
Playing in comedy of late years her memory was
apt to play tricks. The horn of the prompter was
heard on the wing. But as a platform artist, lec-
turing and reciting and witching the audience with
her manner, Ellen will have notes to refer to, and if
the charming old comedy queen fails to grab Aus-
tralian womanhood by the wool—well, the sex’s
fondness for Lily Brayton in Shakspearian
plays will seem unaccountable. Presumably the
Women’s League and the Immortal Bard societies
will lead the way in fussing over Ellen Terry. She
stands for classic drama, art culture and unmilitant
suffragettery. Her theatrical record dates back to
the days of Charles Kean, for it was under his man-
agement she made her stage d6but as the child
Mamilius, in “A Winter’s Tale.” That was in 1856,
and she was then an eight-year old kiddie. As a 10-
year old she played Prince Arthur in “King John,”
with the Keans. Her famous impersonation of Portia
is just 39 years young this month. Her first appear-
ance in an ingenue heroine part happened 51 years
ago, when she was a girl of 15, and Edward Sothern
was the leading man of the co. Ellen Terry has no
sordid, shabby theatrical recollections. From her
childhood upwards she was always a shining light
among the Best People in mummery—the spoilt
darling of the stage, like her sister Kate before her.
No stagewoman has been more unaffectedly artistic
than this charming exponent of theatrical natural-
ness. The charm is still hers. Age has not withered
it. Age has merely harmonised it with the artist’s
years. If the long sea voyage refreshes her, people
will wonder at the vivacity of Ellen Terry, and
whatever she does for an entertainment will be a
success. But where is she going to do it?
“Harrison O.”: “Bookware” (B. 9/4/’l4) protests
with amusing seriousness that the impishness of G. B.
Pshaw “spoilt” the mock trial, held in London, of
John Jasper, for the murder of Edwin Drood. “Book-
ware” is angry, because, he says, it was meant that
the trial should be a “solemn and genuine attempt
to decide what Dickens had intended his unfinished
story to be.” Seems to me that it is somewhat late
in the day to discuss the intention of C. Dickens in
the matter, especially since it is of no importance.
Shaw, as foreman of the jury, transformed a piece of
solemn humbug into a matter for joy and mirth.
F’rinstance, his remark, made when it was proposed by
counsel to call certain evidence: “If the learned
gentleman thinks that the minds of a British jury are
going to be influenced by evidence he little knows his
countrymen,” was worth several hours of debating
society tomfoolery such as those present were threat-
ened with.
“Melb”: Goodbye to “The Forty Thieves,” but
we have the doctors of Collins-street East still left.
This was the third “Forty Thieves” panto, in Mel-
bourne. The first was with Greville as Ali Baba at
the Royal. The second had Lonnen as Ali Baba, at
the Princess’s. The sale just effected of Fawkner’s
original property is a reminder of the best panto,
ever seen here: “The House that Jack Built, or the
Loves of Jack Melbourne and Little Victoria.” This
was in 1869, after Fawkner’s funeral. Jack was
Fawkner, and his house was Melbourne. What a
happy idea. Can such enter the wooden brains of
Hickory Wood and such as furnish us with printed
goods from England? In the 1869 panto., written
by Akhurst, Harwood was Jack, and Maggie Stewart
took Victoria, Nelly being then a chicken. A fea-
ture was Hennings’s magnificent panorama of the
history of Melbourne.
Lauder has been doing some theatre-haunting.
One night he went to “On Our Selection,” but did
not grasp the humor until some time during the
second act. Then he laughed and kept on laughing.
Better late than never. On Thursday night he
taxied round to the Tivoli and appeared unexpect-
edly in a box. The various turns began to play
spasmodically and every performer’s eye was turned
hopefully upwards. Once “the highest salaried”
laughed outright, and the artist thus honored be-
came boisterous with joy. If Harry had laughed
again the performer would probably have gone dotty
with joy and thrown himself into the piano.
“Haresfoot”: Before me is a small book of rather
melancholy interest. It is the version of Hugo’s
“Ruy Bias,” written by the unfortunate suicide, John
Davidson, for Lewis Waller, and acted by Waller in
London. It is compact and vivid to read, by those
who cannot struggle through Hugo’s interminable
length. This magnificent play had Lemaitre for the
original Ruy Bias—a tremendous success, although
Lemaitre had only been regarded as a melodramatic
actor, in “Robert Macaire,” “Ragpicker of Paris”
and the like. Dickens wrote in the most enthusiastic
terms of Fechter’s Ruy Bias. The play has only been
produced in Melbourne twice. First by George Faw-
cett Rowe at the old wooden matchbox Princess’s,
when he acted Ruy Bias. The second occasion was
when Darrell presented a version of his own at the
Theatre Royal. Both Rowe and Darrell cut the piece
short. Anyhow, it is much finer than anything Waller
has acted, as leading man, except “Henry V.” One
of my grumbles against Sarah Bernhardt’s Legion of
Honor is that, in her starring time, she acted nothing
of Corneille, Racine, Moliere, or Hugo, except
Racine’s “Phedre ” very occasionally. At the theatre
Franqais she had been resplendent in the Dona Sol
of Hugo’s “Hernani” and the Queen in “Ruy Bias.”
Don Cssar de Bazan appeared first in Ruy Bias.
Then Dennery made him the hero of his brisk comedy,
adapted for Wallace’s “Maritana.”
“C. Ockney”: Oswald Stoll, the Melbourne-born
entrepeneur, who for so long has been at the head
of vaudeville in Britain, has a useful sort of mother.
She takes, and has for years taken, the money at the
door of the London Coliseum—the Stoll palace in
Charing Cross-road. She takes pride in the fact
that at most of the theatres which her son had
opened she had taken the first money at the doors. No
wonder Stoll has succeeded. The opening of Life’s
oyster would be a comparatively easy job if we
could all, in a manner of speaking, get really reliable
mothers to take the money at the door.
LINES TO ELIZABETH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lizzie, the blood-sweating hippopotamus, is in town
with Wirths’ Circus —Social news item.
Sweat no more, my pretty dear!
Ah, sweat no more, my sweet!
The Autumn days are fresh, and here
Are lots of things to eat!
Ah, vex yourself r.o more, my dear,
And banish vain regret;
I’d have your dear complexion clear —
Unless you want to sweat.
Sweat no more, my gracious queen!
Perspire no more, my dear.
For oh! it dims thy hide’s bright sheen,
And I would have it clear.
What sorrow wrings thy hippo heart
Come, break the dreadful news!
(I would thy heart’s blood would not start
In purple beads of ooze!)
Sweat if thou must, but inwardly,
For blood I can’t abide!
Alas! the people pay to see
Her heart’s blood on her hide!
N.S.W.
RICHARD LOVELACE.

The suburban passenger bound for Sydney
has developed a new habit. After much
lame delay and famine and backwardness,
and many of the labors of Sisyphus, his
train is finally bailed up in the old bottleneck
tunnel just outside Sydney station. Then
to fill in time he gets out and writes inscrip-
tions on the wall, saying that he is Pharaoh,
and that the train started from Memphis
1000 b.c. To this he adds a sketch of a
pyramid and a cry for the blood and bell-
topper and boots of Johnson. Having done
thus he either gets back into the train, or
walks along the line in defiance of the regu-
lations. Sydney station is being amended
and enlarged, so part of the premises are in
hospital. If it belonged to a railway which
went right through the city, so that the sub-
urban train ran from, say, Homebush to
Wahroongah, it would only be necessary to
hustle a little, and push the trains through
faster. But when a station is a dead end,
and every train that goes in has to back out
again, the fact that three or four platforms
out of the usual 14 are laid up for amputa-
tion makes chaos. It is possible to habitu-
ally drive 14 tame elephants into a cave
without confusion if there is an outlet at the
far end of the cave and the animals can go
straight on, and sort themselves out in the
open country for the return journey. If the
cave comes to a dead stop, and the elephants
have to back out tail first, careful manage-
ment is required to prevent annoyance and
dislocation. But if half the cave is closed
for repairs, and the elephants get all mixed
up inside, and back out anyhow, there is
sure to be a very bad mixture of elephant.
The A.N.A. in Adelaide, at its coming con-
ference, undertakes to discuss—sandwiched
in with encouragement to local industries,
afforestation, swimming, a uniform railway
gauge, and locking the Torrens —about tlie
vaguest motion on record: “That this Associ-
ation is in favor of greater encouragement of
‘real sport’ among young Australians, as it is
the best means of developing a healthy
national character.” Now unreal sport con-
sists naturally of a Hebrew chasing a pig in
a dream, but the real article is hard to define.
“0.K.” speaks—as in a whisper darkly :
Melbourne’s most Pankhurstian associated women
made a bid for notoriety last week by attempting, in
a mildly turbulent way, to introduce the question
of “White Slave Traffic” to the State Premiers. They
had no chance of getting serious consideration, and
they knew it. The W.S.T. question had previously
been referred to the local police, and the police
had said there was no such thing to be found here-
abouts. Melbourne was the worst possible place for
the angry females to make a demonstration in.
They were wasting time and energy, as is the habit
of indignant feminines, with the result that they
have brought their cause into disrepute. What
they should now set about is the collection of evi-
dence in support of their cause. If they keep their
eyes and ears, open and their dear little mouths
shut; if they get into touch with non-political
women who go around and gather hews in their or-
dinary way of business, these unpractical Pank-
hurstiennes might presently be in a position to prove
that a number of unsuspecting girls are lured to
brothels by nice kind ladies in Melbourne, although the
police are quite sure that nothing of the sort takes
place. This is hardly what the world understands
by the term “White Slave Traffic,” which suggests
the capture and shipment of girls to distant places,
or the entrapping of them by fraudulent advts.
But it is presumably what the Melbourne Pank-
hurstiennes meant when they were getting their
hair off last week. Their meaning, however, is a
matter of no consequence just now, seeing that
nobody is paying any attention to them. They must
do something to compel attention if they mean busi-
ness, and they must do it darned quietly. If they
don’t know what they ought to do let them seek
instruction from a wicked man. The writer would
just love the job of making them wise, over a cup of
tea and a muffin.
* * * #
As a result of argument between certain
Melbourne poulterers and poultry exporters
and their organised “pluckers,” the debilitated
chooky-hen and the young spring chicken
destined to adorn the dinner-table at Easter
time enjoyed a few more days’ life and
another bushel or two of shell-grit—or what-
ever it is the beasties eat. According to
the evidence available, the gentlemen who live
by tearing the clothing off poultry placed
before their employers a log, claiming in-
creased wages and better working conditions,
and requested a conference, failing which
they would strike at the critical moment,
just before Easter. The employers con-
ferred, refused to concede the demands as
per log, and offered the employees their
united co-operation to secure a Board to
rectify trade grievances, fix wages, etc. —
which offer was to be accepted instanter or
it would be withdrawn. Both parties being
thus evidently in the humor for a fight, the
offer - was declined and war began. One
party—beside the above-mentioned chooky-
hen—gained by this “industrial upheaval,”
the small suburban poulterer, who does his
own plucking. When he attended the Easter
poultry market he found that prices had
naturally dropped to zero, so he gathered in
as large a harvest as his pocket would
allow. But, except for him, nobody seemed
to gain anything, or anything that a Wages
Board couldn’t have given. Still, the hen-
strippers may have wanted a rest. Pulling
feathers is a tedious, mechanical, unintellec-
tual job; and to keep at it year after year
would naturally make a man cantankerous
and hasty-tempered.

By the recent arrest of a youth ’o the act
of scaling a warehouse wall the Melbourne
police ninoed in the bud a career that was
full of promise. An inventory of the scaler’s
personal possessions panned out: One six-
chambered revolver, fully loaded; one revol-
ver case bearing the stamp of the Kilmore
police; one large butcher’s knife; one steel
for sharpening same; one electric torch;
one onium pipe; a watch and chain,
the latter bearing a ’Varsity Sports
Club medal; several foreign coins; some
brummy jewellery, and a numbgf of
door-keys. The axe and the, keg of dyna-
mite, and the bottle of prussic acid, and the
bag of lime for incinerating the remains, are
believed to have been concealed by the enter-
prising young fellow just before his capture.
“Albatross”: Regarded as a hoodoo ship
the Dunmore is a mere child by comparison
with the Mintaro (one time Larnaca), late
of the Adelaide Steamship Co. When
“Archie” Paul commanded this vessel it
camped for a night on the south breakwater
at Newcastle. Under “Billy” Butcher it
sped on to Stony Point —also off Newcastle.
It next collided with the Nymboida, and
caused Captain Ville’s certificate to be sus-
pended. Later it sank the Falcon tug, in
the Port Adelaide River, causing Captain
“Bill” Leiper a ton of trouble. Captain
Barnes had his “bit of parchment” smudged
through its assault upon the Spitfire bank in
Moreton Bay. And, finally, the writer of
this par. was in charge when it argued the
point with the Wairuna in Sydney Harbor.
Add to the above a whole host of casual
bumps into piers, wharves, etc., and you get
a very remarkable record. There are but
two things I care to recall nowadays in
connection with the wretched old craft. (1)
It never caused a death ; (2) it was recently
destroyed.

Paris Nesbit, K.C., brought off a coup last
week, of the kind that gets into books about
“Good Stories of Bench and Bar.” He was
defending another Adelaide lawyer charged
with “converting” £lO. Nesbit’s cross-exam-
ination took this form :
Before Lunch:
Is it a fact that you have always looked on the
£lO as a debt due from him to you?—Yes.
And have always been willing to accept it? —Yes.
Would you accept it now? —Yes.
Is it by your desire that this prosecution was
brought ? —No.
After Lunch:
Since giving your evidence has the debt been paid
to you?—Yes.
Who paid it to you?—You did!
Judge Murray interposed with a hope that
Mr. Nesbit had not committed the “unprofes-
sional act” of paying the money out of his
own pocket, and was assured that he hadn’t.
After which the puzzled jury said it couldn’t
agree on a verdict if it “stayed there for a
week.”
Tbe Oldest Inhabitant: One of the most
notable drinkeries in Melbourne —-Young and
Jackson’s, on the corner of Flinders and
Swanston streets —has just changed hands.
It was originally a butchery, kept by James
Graham, who subsequently established him-
self in Sydney. Early in the ’6o’s it became
a pub, under the name of Princes Bridge
Hotel, and the habit grew upon it. Young
and Jackson have kept the pumps going for
80 years. John Batman originally owned
the site.

The Cassaba is to be the melon of the future. It
carries a flavor which is indescribable. It suggests
pineapple, guava, water and rock melon. One lady
taster whistled and almost shouted; it reminded
her of champagne.— Sydney Agricultural Show item.

The Oldest Inhabitant makes no mention
of a pub:—
Last week a bit of historic Sydney changed hands
when the building owned and occupied by Batt, Rodd
and Purves, in Pitt-street, was sold to Messrs. W. C.
Penfold, stationers, of the same street. The site was
originally the property of Sam Terry, and the five
or six houses on and adjoining the site were known
as Terry’s Building. In the most northern one the
Public Library, then a private subscription concern,
had its genesis. The Batt-Rodd Purves building,
which occupies 22 feet, was valued at £4OOO and the
land at £16,000, which works out at about £727
per foot.
THE PEOPLE OF PEACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Professor Starr Jordan said that the belief in an
invasion by Japs which had begun the White Aus-
tralia policy was the work of scoundrels.
—Melbourne Age report.
Miss Gibbs, a Quakeress, was fined £lO or two
months imprisonment, for having thrashed a police-
man with a dog-whip as a protest against the latest
incarceration of Mrs. Pankhurst.— Cablegram.
Sons of Quaker members of the Peace Society are
pleading immunity from compulsory service for reasons
of conscience.— Melbourne Age.
From the land where they dare not refuse the Jap
Starr comes to teach us how—
From the land where the ’Murkan maiden sits in
school with the slant-eyed Chow —
He comes to teach us that knuckling down is the
way to perfect peace,
And that he who possesses a willing arm is speeding
his own decease.
He comes to say that a people rich and few, if they
remain but so,
Are sure of peace with a people poor and strong from
Tokio.
From the Hell that is called Chicago he comes with
his drone and his sappy head,
Full of schemes to make the world a heaven —when
all now alive are dead.
And he and the shirkers of drill are one —-and their
mother is Our Miss Gibbs,
. The Quaker maiden who thrashed the cop and was
charged before his Nibs.
And the Christians who beat one another sore and
fight for the love of God,
And are brothers most when they tear the turf and
ensanguine the poor Ould Sod.
He fights with his mouth so bravely—this same bar-
racker for “Peace,”
His munitions of war are words like “Fools” and
“Scoundrels” ; his shot is grease.
Professor Jordan who starred and burst when he asked
our land to show
By its long, drugged sleep that it loves and trusts the
strangers of Tokio.
If the Quaker shirk his drill because of his conscience
white and pure,
And the Quakeress for her conscience beats the cop who
must aye endure,
And the man who defends the world’s best land is a
scoundrel and a fool,
Then the padded cell’s a Professor short—and they’ll
find him at the school.
The Californian school-girl learns cheek by jowl
with the small, brown Jap,
And Columbia bows to the alien man and takes the
Chow in her lap.
But, though scoundrels or fools or utter rogues, who’d
fight for their own, we be,
Australia’s legions shall not decrease while Jordan
runs to the sea.
VICTORIA.
MARTIN LUTHER.


THE FORM OF ILL-HEALTH THAT MOST
PREVAILS IN AUSTRALIA.
We are told that the consumption of medi-
cine is greater in Australia than in any other
country, and this must be attributed to an
unsatisfactory condition of health affecting a
large proportion of our people. What then
is the prevalent form of ill-health ? All indi-
cations point one way—to the fact that the
average person suffers most frequently from
some disorder of the digestive functions, which
might be easily corrected by the right kind of
treatment. Modern medical authorities teach
us that most of our common ailments have
their origin in the digestive area. In a word,
we poison ourselves (autointoxication) by
absorption from the intestinal tract. To over-
come this condition, the best remedy is
petroleum, because unlike other remedies, it
reaches the intestines unchanged, where it
exercises its lubricating, anti-fermentative,
healing effects to the fullest extent. The
original and standard preparation of petroleum
for internal use is Angier's Emulsion, which
has been largely employed by the medical
profession ever since 1892. Apart from its
soothing, healing properties, Angier’s Emulsion
(because of the hypophosphites which it con-
tains) exerts a marked tonic, invigorating
influence which makes it of value in a wide
variety of disorders. Doctors prescribe it
largely, not alone for lung troub'es and wasting
diseases, but even more for digestive and bowel
disorders. Very good results have followed
its use in the treatment of, gastric catarrh,
gastric ulcer, mucous colitis, nervous dys-
pepsia, chronic constipation, and in many
obscure digestive and bowel disorders that
have resisted other treatment. Of all chemists

An eerie tale of human credulity was re-
counted at the Central Court (Sydney) by
one Margaret Slack the other day. Three
months ago she met a young man named
Moloney. The fact that his name was
Moloney was all she knew about him, and
she only had his word for that. He wanted
to know the address o.f a good hospital, and
she, being a nurse, was able to oblige him.
Then, according to her story, he began to
lie. And she, like Desdemona, believed every
word he said. He told her —if she is to be
believed —that he was the owner of a big
racing establishment in the West; that he
owned the Belmont stables; that Mr. Con-
nelly was his trainer and Silver Lad
and Plastine his property. He also
mentioned that he owned the Palace
Hotel in Perth (W.A.), and about
a dozen other big pubs and businesses. Inci-
dentally, he is, alleged to have remarked
lightly that he was worth three or four
hundred thousand sterling. And during all
this time, if the lady’s story is correct, he
was borrowing from her sums such as 10s.,
fl and £2 12s. 6d. She claims that, in the
end, she lent him not only all the money
she had in her banking account but every
farthing she could raise on her home. The
episode constitutes a dreadful reflection bn
her intelligence, assuming her account of
it is true. It presents her as the ultimate
limit in the way of that faith which can
throw away mountains. On the other
hand, the whole affair may be a hallucina-
tion on the part of the lady, and Moloney,
who now stands committed for trial, may
be one of the most ill-used men in N. S.
Wales. You can never know in these matters
till the legal numbers go up.

Melbourne had a goodly crop of breach-of-
promise actions at the recent sittings. One
distracted female got £4OO and another £225
out of gentlemen who had gone cold on their
matrimonial bargains. Also, a refrigerated
lover, who had fallen in for £lOO but had
paid nothing, was ordered to part up 10s.
weekly out of a wage of £126 per annum, or
go to quod. The £4OO victim told the jury
that though the plaintiff had kissed him fre-
quently and fervently he had never done the
same kind office by her. The jury’s opinion
of this story may be estimated by the dam-
ages awarded —a cryptic remark which the
reader may take whichever way he likes.

The £225 plaintiff provided the phenomenon

The Bulletin’s Court correspondent dis-
courses :
Lady Randolph Churchill has got her divorce
from Mr. George Cornwallis West. Their marriage,
13 years ago, exasperated the members of the
Churchill and West families in about an equal
degree. West’s sisters —one is the Duchess of
Westminster, the other the Princess Pless —ex-
hausted all the arts of persuasion, badinage, ridi-
cule, chaff, scorn, reproach and downright rage
and fury in the hope of averting the calamity.
The bridegroom’s mother, who, up to this event
had been reckoned the most capable matchmaker
in London, retired in despair to Wales. Her
medical advisers opined that had she stayed in
London while the matrimonial sacrifice was being
accomplished she would have burst. Mr. West was
in his early twenties at the time, and in
appearance younger than his years. Like all his
family, he is astonishingly good-looking. Lady
Randolph had a couple of grown-up sons, one of
whom —Winston—was already forging ahead in the
political world.' She was close on 50, and looked
every minute of it. The young man’s second choice
is no immature girl, either. She owns up to 50,
but such things, in the case of actors, actresses and
pugilists, mean little. It must be 25 years since I
first saw her in melodrama at the London Adelphi,
and to my boyish eyes she looked an elderly woman
then. Like Lady Randolph, she has been beautiful
in her 'time ■ and, also like the Churchill lady, she
is exceptionally clever and original. Clement Scott,
the dramatic critic, who was so scandalised at Ib-
sen’s plays, loved her with one of those wild, hope-
less passions that occasionally afflict aggressively
respectable persons of middle age. However, she
spurned him. There is no clear reason why two
distinguished women should make fools of them-
selves over Mr. West. He is tallish, slight, the
possessor of small, regular features and beautiful,
wavy hair. And that is all there is to him. If
he had not made a couple of sensational marriages
no one, outside London Society, would know that
he was alive.

“T.F.M.” writes: —
“Melb” (in B. 2/4/’l4, page 11, col. 1) errs when
he states that “Batman’s first house was on
the Collins and William streets corner now occupied
by the Australian Mutual Provident building.” That
is, if he means that Bat’s first dwelling-place was
on this site. • On the A.M.P. edifice are the words,
“John Batman, founder of Melbourne, built on this
spot.”. So he did, but he never lived there. The
inscription must have been drafted by a lawyer—
or perhaps by one of our great statesmen. Why,
even one of Melbourne’s great dailies was so misled
by that inscription as to declare not long since that
the Collins and William streets corner was
identical with the historic Batman’s Hill. Bat-
man bought the land in question in June, 1837, for
£6O, and on it built a two-storeyed structure which,
after his decease, was occupied for a time by his
family. He himself lived while in Melbourne in
the house he erected on Batman’s Hill. Also he died
there. He' didn’t make a hobby of dying, but he did
it once.

“Sherlock Holmes”: Heaven only knows
why they are called “detectives,” but that is
how the pathetic Victorian plain-clothes
policemen are described whenever there is a
mystery to be deepened and a criminal to be
totally lost. When they are not posing
before newspaper cameras they are going
about the country mak-
ing a noise like a cir-
cus, and publicly de-
manding to be supplied
with clues. People
can’t work for the up-
roar they create, and
valuable advertise-
ments are crowded out
of the newspapers be-
cause the fact has to
be announced that they
are going to Duck-
waddle Swamp by the
5.45 train from Spen-
cer-street, and are
proceeding with the
utmost secrecy. It’s
all rank bellowdrama,
and the acting is of a
very inferior sort.
Then, of course, they
never discover any-
thing—not even the
fact that they are pub-
lic nuisances and dis-
turbers of the peace.
Victorians just hate to
have a quiet visit from
criminals, because that
brings down on the
house a tribe of actors
who proceed with such
deafening secrecy that
the victim can’t think
or attend to his job.
The Victorian sleuth,
reminds me of the offi-
cial bloodhound in one
of Yankee actor Col-
lier’s productions of
years ago. He rushes
into the room where
the “wanted” man is lounging and roars,
“I’m a secret service man! Don’t tell any-
one, will you ?” And the pursued one re-
plies calmly, “No, you tell them!”

HOW HE FELL IN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We will sav where we will work, how we will
work, and when we will work, just when we like, and
we will receive what we say we like if we think it
adequate to cover all our needs. — A Flinders-lane ware-
house free labor man defying trades unionism and
the Trades Hall. —Melbourne Age report.
A “fag” alight betwen his lips, his cady on his head,
The young man flung his yardstick down, put on his
coat, and said:
“Enough o’ this for me—l’m off to do the blessed
Block!
A lot o’ goats you are to work right on till six
o’clock!”
And to the boss, who said: “We’re working back until
eleven
To-night,” he snapped: “Oh, work be blowed —and
you can go to Heaven 1”
The young man, passing onward, spoke the bald and
bent cashier:
“I want a bit of cash, to spend on girlie’s tea, and
beer!
(Let’s help meself!) I want a pair of new and
sparkling socks,”
He plunged his ready hand into the nearest bullion-
box,
And grabbed five quid. “Fair doos,” he quoth, in
quite a careless way—
“An adequate return, I think, for what I’ve done
to-day.”
Gaily he went, quite confident, big piles of work
among.
“I should be toiling here, I know,” he grinned;
“but, while I’m young,
I mean to work where, when and how I wish, or not
at all;
And when the billiard-table waits, or moving-pictures
call,
Or to a sweet, secluded hour at tea twin souls
aspire,
Why should I toil and moil when such is not my
heart’s desire ?”
The boss, pursuing, blocked his way before he
reached the door,
And asked why that free-labor youth would toil that
day no more?
“Out o’ me blanky way at once!” the bluff young
fellow cried,
“Why, I’m a pledged free-laborite —my work-hours I
decide!”
The boss breathed deep and swallowed hard, and
smiled a dreadful smile,
And then he screamed and fell on him, and kicked
him half a mile.

LEVER BROTHERS
UMITEO. SYDNEY.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
Died at Summer Hill, Sydney, on April 1,
John Baton, aged 83. Baton served with the
93rd Foot (Sutherland Highlanders) at Bala-
clava and in the Indian Mutiny. He won
the V.C. before Lucknow when Havelock
was relieved. Unarmed, he made a survey
of the Sepoys’ position under heavy fire, and
finding a breach in the walls, led the British
to victory after Colin Campbell had reluct-
antly decided that the fort was impregnable.
Arriving at Sydney in 1861, the warlike
Highlander became a warder at Darlinghurst
Gaol, and gradually rose until he was made
governor at Berrima and afterwards at Goul-
burn. He retired on a pension in 1896.
JOSEPH AND HIS BRETHREN.
One of the oldest residents of Victoria
died recently at St. Helena, Greensborough,
near Melbourne. Miss Margaret Beale ar-
rived in Watt’s State in 1839, with her
father, Major Anthony Beale, retired from
the East India service. He represented
“John Company” on the island of St. Helena
during the term of Napoleon’s exile there,
and was one of the pall-bearers at his
funeral. Miss Beale was 86 years of age
when her funeral fell due.
“Mere Cove” speaks :
James Mitchell, of Table Top (N.S.W.), who died
the other day, owed his capacity to his remarkable
mother. She came, a widow with a young family
(James was at the time, then aged two years) to
Albury in the year 1837, and took Mungabreena.
James had been about the place, in a sentient state,
for 77 years till the other day. He never forgot
what he owed to her of whom the music-hall offender
delighteth to wail. It was in memory of his mother
that he parted up £2OOO to the Albury Hospital.
Nothing less potent could Wring such a sum out of
James, whose carefulness was Lauder-like in its
intensity. J. Mitchell, and brothers, with typical
family thoroughness, learnt the language of the
Woradgery tribe, and thus wormed themselves into
the members’ affections at a time when Binghi was
still a power in the land. James founded the Table
fop . st ud. it produced many good winners from
Glorious, sire of the Australian Cremorne, which, at
33 to 1 beat Straightftre in the Caulfield Cup. He
also owned Grand Canary, dam of Trafalgar. One of
his blackest memories surrounded the fact that he
sold this fair animal for something like £5O or
7s. 6d., or some equally paltry sum.
“0.K.” writes: —
When General lan Hamilton went to Canberra the
other week to witness performances of the Duntroon
military collegians in ceremonial drill, physical
drill, light horse and artillery drill, bridge building
and skirmishing, the unconscious funny man of the
circus-show was one of Hamilton’s staff-officers.
This typical representative of British militarism
wears a monocle and talks like the futile Augustus of
imported melodrama. The Duntroon youths couldn’t
for the life of them understand what the Johnnie was
saying, and every time he readjusted his eveglass
they roared inwardly. By the way, General Hamil-
ton’s parting address to the future officahs was not
quite correctly reported in the Sydney press. A
Peace Society has reproved the hearty warrior for
wishing the boys “plenty of wars” (vide newspaper
reports). What he actually said was “Good luck,
rapid promotion and bloody wars.” In its ex-
clamatory sense “Bloody wars!” means the same as
“O Jerusalem!” “Goodness gracious!” or many other
sociable terms. In this instance, coming at the
end of the sentence, it could be vaguely understood
to mean “So long!”
Maoriland has more than a passing inter-
est in the ill-fated Stefansson Arctic Expedi-
tion, as the anthropologist with the party is
Diamond Jenness, who hails from Welling-
ton. A letter was received from him the
other day (it had taken about five months
to wander down from the North), saying
that he was putting in the winter with an
Eskimo family within hailing distance of
the North Pole, and didn’t know when he’d
be able to get back. Anyhow, it wouldn’t be
for a day or two. He was one of the party
that went ashore to hunt caribou with flie
leader, and came hack to find that the ship,
with all the tucker and outfit and the balance
of the expedition, as well as the ship’s cat
and both anchors, had disappeared off the
bosom of the deep. It hasn’t been seen
since. The marooned party, filled with
hope (which is about all it had to fill itself
with), contrived to struggle along, and at
latest reports was still struggling.
Winckler, the last white skipper of the
Nippon Yusen Ivaisha line trading between
Japan and Australia, has left his familiar
job and become a Torres Strait pilot, for
which industry he should be eminently
fitted. The T. S. pilot runs, probably, the
longest stage of single-handed pilotage in
the world. On the Irrawaddy and Brahma-
pootra and upper Amazon boats the pilots
relieve each other at short intervals, and
each is expected to know his own stage only.
On the Irrawaddy a fresh nigger is taken
on in the morning, with his sub-nigger or
deputy-blot to help him, and both are dis-
charged at night. Then the vessel anchors
and waits for a bright, fresh nigger, who
comes with the sunrise like a black radiance
rising from the waters. As for the Missis-
sippi pilot he is practically dead and gone,
if he ever existed. Some people think that
Mark Twain invented him. If he was a
reality the railways killed him. But on that
immensely long, rocky stretch or graveyard
of ships inside the Barrier Reef there is a
grand chance for expert pilotage, and Cap-
tain Winckler is the kind of man who will
save many an old ark’s bones from
burial thereabouts. The Jap. ser-
vice is a good thing to get out of.
At first only the junior deck officers
were brown, and the white man
was supreme. Then they steadily
browned into the higher ranks, and
the white chief mates and captains
went out one by one to make room
for them. Winckler was retained
till the last because he w_as the
best of the crowd, but he felt lonely
as the final survivor of his race on
the Jap-Australia run. It was like
being the Last Man on the day of
the Deluge. Also he saw hundreds
of brown men famishing for his
job. And perhaps the Company
became too beastly polite, as if it
was talking to an Oldest Sea In-
habitant or a Survival. Being still
a young man and not feeling at all like an
Oldest Sea Inhabitant he slammed the door
of his engagement and quitted.
By “0.K.”: —
Train up a boy in the way of cheap notoriety and
when he is a man he will stick to it. George Corn-
wallis West is the son of the Mrs. Cornwallis Wrst
who was contemporary with young Mrs. Langtrv. and
her nearest rival in the society beauty business.
Mrs. Langtry’s photos had not long been on sale at a
hundred London shops when portraits of Mrs. Corn-
wallis West came into the market. Lily Langtry
was photoed always on her own, as girlishlv as pos-
sible, therefore Mrs. West, having several pretty
children to her credit, brought them into some of
the pictures. Practically the public was, asked to
give its society beauty vote to Mrs. Cornwallis West,
the lovely mother with a quite large family. The
juvenile Wests of the period saw themselves in shop
windows all over the place, and the craving for
notoriety was bred in them. It was born in the
subsequent members of the family. George Corn-
wallis West was one of the subsequent achievements
of his mother. He must have come to pass after
the craze for society-beauty photographs had died
out, and Lily Langtry was about to be born again as
a professional actress. His only chance of bringing
his features before the world at large was to
marry a notable woman, so he captured the widow
of Lord Randolph Churchill in 1900. He was then
25, and she was more than old enough to be his
mother. Last year the foolish matron got a decree
nisi of divorce from her darling George, after
having sued him for restitution of conjugal rights.
Last week the decree was made absolute, and on
the same day George Cornwallis West, now nearly
40, married Mrs. Pat Campbell, who is only ten
years his senior. So his photos are on view once
more. He’s happy.

Edgar Waite took over control of Adelaide
Museum on the Ist of April. He had five
years of the work at Leeds long ago* but since
1892 has been a good Australasian, first
as Zoologist to the “Australian MuseUm” in
Sydney, and latterly as director of the
museum at Christchurch (M.L.), whete he
specialised in fish—not the stinking variety.
Professor Stirling, of the University, has had
control of the Adelaide Museum up till now.
“Albatross”: “Sammy” Toombs, master
mariner, who cut Varney Farkes’ cable at
last N.S.W. general elections, is one of the
most tireless orators in the universe. He is
distinguished not so much for what he says
as for the interminable length at which he
savs it Hence his net name “Tnw”
says it. nence, ms pet name, Jaw
Toombs. In his sea days when he got really
going his fellow mariners were unable to
even hear the interjections of the
engines. He was engaged by the A.U.S.N.
Co. to help salve the Mildura, which was
wrecked on the N.W. Cape (W.A.). There
was a vague idea in the minds of the directo-
rate that he might talk the unhappy vessel
clean off the rocks. He failed in this ; how-
ever he talked Varney out of Parlia-
ment. He is hard at work at present
talking himself into the Ministry. He has
come nearer, perhaps, to solving the problem
of perpetual motion than any other male
citizen of this great land of ours.

From a Victorian artist in Bull’s Run:—
You will be surprised to hear that George Reid’s
absence from London has not been noticed. Even in
officious circles they seem to be unaware of his non-
existence. At the office they are telling of a caller
who asked after the Wide Commissioner, and was
told he had gone to Australia. “Has he, indeed?”
said the visitor. “Could you give me his telephone
number? I want to ask him to bring me an emu.”
“Islander” writes
Your contributor’s character sketch (26/3/’l4) of
C. E. Jaeomb was uncannily correct. The author
of “The Horrible Australian,” or whatever it is
called, is not in England, however, but in the New
Hebrides; or, to be explicit, he was in those parts
when I left the other day. I never heard that he
wanted to get into the Melbourne Club and failed,
but I know that he was anxious to become a mem-
ber of one of the Sydney clubs. He could get no one to
put him up—-not that there was anything against
his character, but on the general grounds that he
was a tiresome young ass of the blatantly boring
type—and the project fell through. His people
are well-to-do English folks of the bourgeois class.
They set him up in the raisin industry; came out
to visit him and his brother (who lives at Vailima)
periodically; and used to send them both cases of
wine, pianos and other home comforts from time to
time. The brother, who is older than Australia’s
assailant, is a barrister and solicitor, both English
and French, and a good sort. Young Jaeomb —he of
the book —made no great millions at the raisin
business. The local view was that he spent so much
time telling total strangers about the persons of
birth whom he had met at Harrow that he did not
get a chance to attend to the raisins. Mildura heard
more about (1) Harrow and (2) the titled people of
Britain who had been educated there, during the
period when Jaeomb was endeavoring to grow raisins,
than it ever had before, or, in all human likelihood,
ever will again. His continued failure to make any
money irritated his people, and ultimately his brother
wrote and advised him to chuck the raisin business
and come to the Islands, where an easy and fairly
lucrative billet awaited hihl. And that is IiOW
Jaeomb came to abandon Australia. He is not a
bad lad at heart. A queer sort of Snobbishness that
one Only finds in suburban Britishers; and the touchi-
ness and Self-sufficiency which are the heritage of
the young and dull the world over, are his Chief
troubles. In estimating his book—which, by the
way, I haven’t read mvseif—yOu want to remember
that he had a tt.ying time Socially irt Australia, for
various reasons; and that to this particular type,
Social neglect is an intolerable experience;
ROBBING THE DOG OF HIS BONE.
Mr. Flowers proposed the second reading of the
State Debt and Sinking Fund (Amendment) Bill.
N.S.W Parliamentary item.
“Hamer”: The Premiers’ Conference
offered an example of the chances that Aus-
tralia provides for the poor man, if it did
nothing else. There were only two men of
inherited wealth at it—Mackinnon and
Willie Kelly. Of the others, two started life
selling newspapers, Joe Cook was a miner,
Forrest a surveyor, Holman a French
polisher, Watt a clerk, Peake a carpenter,
Schddan an eUginedtiver, and so on. That
several of these men are hide-bound Tories
merelv illustrates the fact that Toryism, like
measles, occurs unexpectedly.
Opposition-leader Wade, of N.S.W., sug-
gests that the Liberal voter should be caught
young. It is a good idea, but a slow man
like Wade is much more likely to catch him
dead, or crippled with rheumatism.
“It is only a question of a few years,”
according to N.S.W. Minister Hoyle, “when
the whole wool produce of N.S.W. and
Queensland will be sold in Sydney.*’ Yet
Hoyle belongs to a Government Which pro-
fesses to believe in decentralisation !
Hagelthorn, who represents Public Works
in Victoria, was waited on last week by
“representatives of the Liberal Workers’ In-
stitute,” who wanted him to do something
“for the prevention of unemployment.” If
these representatives would get busy at find-
ing out why the greater part of Victoria is
losing population, they might begin to get on
the track of their job. The Victorian theory
is that it is essential, by strange railway
deviations and wild devices in railway
charges, to rush all business to Melbourne,
and if the business can’t go to Melbourne by
reason of the remoteness, to kill it dead
rather than let it go somewhere else. For
instance, there is plenty of work for 100,000
toilers around Portland if it were not for the
insane Congestion policy. The Liberal
Workers’ Institute might consider the point.
REVEREND EVERARD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Better Japanese rule than Home Rule. —Utterance
of the Reverend Everard Digges La Touche (Syd-
ney), in recent newspaper controversy.
The Reverend Everard Digges La Touche
Joined the debate with a frightful whoosh;
Glared like a Gorgon at gods and men,
Into the vitriol dipped his pen,
Wrote for a minute, wrote for an hour,
Wonderful tropes of eloquent power ;
Thus he wrote, with his mouth in foam,
“Gimme the Monkey, but spare me Rome.”
The Reverend Everard Digges La Touche
Had no doubt in his mind—not much!
Suppose that over the seas there crept
The Monkey host while the watchers slept?
Where, oh where, in that case would be
The Reverend Everard Digges La T. ?
I see a sad and stipendless man
Whose church is a house for Ayusha San.
The Reverend Everard Digges La Touche
Is an Orange hero who deals out stoush—
“Lo, from Italy comes the Beast!
Embrace the Monkey and spurn the Priest!”
Won’t they be a bee-utiful pair,
Japanese morals and Anglican prayer?
The world will grin when in church it twigs
Koko San and Everard Digges.
Reverend Everard Digges La Touche
(To put it in French) please close vot’ bouche:
Preferring the Jap to the Irish gods,
(Begging your pardon) is past the odds.
That state of mind in an old-time dunce
Was called “hysterica passio” once;
And hard-head medicoes treat it still,
With a cooling draught and a good big pill.
N.S.W.
L.
A QUESTION OF MEASUREMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London Thursday.—Under the heading, “A Great
Imperialist,” the Daily Telegraph to-day devotes its
first leading article to references to Mr. George
Reid. — Cable.
They call him “great”—that is old London's latest.
But have his great hosts found
Where the “great” guest from overseas is greatest ?
Is it straight through or round?
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Independent Order of Foresters life
assurance concern, a Canadian outfit which came to
Australia a number of years ago with pomp and
blowing of brass trumpets, and departed with no
pomp at all, has been recently heard from. It was
of the assessment order; that is to say it promised
absurdly large benefits for very little, and
professed that it could always make things
straight, if funds ran short, by putting up
the charge on its membeis. It took the
client by the hand, and gave him a policy for much
in return for something that could only be seen
through a microscope, but reserved the right to in-
crease the premium when the bottom fell out of the
wonderful device, and to forfeit the policy if the
extra money wasn’t forthcoming. And if Smith and
Brown and Jones had all died before the great annoy-
ance came and the heirs had got their money,
Robinson, who was alive and still held his policy,
would naturally have the shortage left on his hands.
This 1.0. F. contraption had a huge, fat, unwise Red
Indian as Chief Manager, or Principal Ranger, or
Boss Elk, or something of the kind, and it advertised
him greatly. His name was Oronhyatekha, or
Ornithorhynchus, or something of the sort, and he was
described as “Dr.” The enterprise sent to Australia
a large, short-tempered manager who brushed aside
all criticism on the ground that he wasn’t bound by
the theories of “old line” companies. He apparently
reckoned that when he called the A.M.P. Society and
the National Mutual “old line” he had blown them
quite away. Also there came along a short, plausible
man, wider in the waist than George Reid, who was
supposed to have no immediate connection with the
1.0. F. He was just a Canadian politician who had
happened along, and couldn’t help mentioning the
tremendous advantages of the show, and its solvency,
and the fact that to meet its Chief Elk was, in
itself, a liberal education. A certain amount of ill-
feeling arose in Victoria through this broad-beamed
citizen giving to a prominent politician a donation
for the party funds. The liabilities of the 1.0. F.
under its policies looked enormous. Its assets looked
very small indeed, and a great proportion of them
seemed to be shares in a trust concern about the
solvency of which nothing was known. But the finan-
cial creature was amazingly bumptious, amazingly
patronising, very sorry for this country’s ignorance
of real life assurance principles and its devotion to
poor little concerns like the A.M.P., and very proud
of the advantages it was going to confer upon a
primitive race of sheep-rearers.
'There was no law in Australia to prevent the
garish bag of tricks collecting good Australian
money, and nobody made any law to prevent it. In
fact it persuaded men of the standing of George
Turner and Alexander Peacock to give it a certain
amount of countenance. It held meetings. It ad-
vertised largely. It got sundry dailies to publish
the portrait of the Chief Red Elk, or whatever he
was called, with remarks about his geniality, his up-
standing figure, the lightning that shone in his eye,
the thunder on his brow, and the beneficence of his
coat-tails. The Bulletin is pleased to remember
that it was the first paper —and almost the only one
—to publish a wholesale and retail denunciation of
the 1.0. F. concern from Canada, that it kept on de-
nouncing and that it was on thoroughly bad terms
with all parties concerned from the beginning to the
end of their stay here. When the foolish old Chief
Red Elk died—apparently he was only a Choctaw or
Cherokee figurehead—and the clouds of financial
stench gathered heavily round the show, the hostility
was still in good working order.
The S. M. Herald described the 1.0. F. the other
day as a concern which had been “happily driven
out of Australia.” (It is a pity that the Herald
didn’t do some more driving on its own account
when a strong unanimous shove was badly needed.)
Also it mentioned casually that, according to the
latest British Board of Trade returns the liabilities
under policies are £9,297,248, and the assets, assum-
ing them to be sound, a little over £4,000,000. A
fair amount of good Australian money is probably
still buried in the bowels of that shortage, but as
the 1.0. F. has left for its old home far away there
isn’t much chance for any Australian to argue on
the point. And as the old Chief Red Elk is now
understood to be grazing in Heaven, no questions can
be put to him. The only reason for all this diffusive-
ness is that, despite the many years that have flowed
past since this garish jest came to our country, Aus-
tralia hasn’t devised such a thing as a rigid and
strenuous life assurance law, with Government audit
and Government valuation. And if some Independent
Order of- Armadillos Life Assurance Company, with
its head oftfSejn a tree beside the upper waters of
the Amazon, comes here this year, with plentiful
cheek, a prospectus and table of rates, some ad-
vertising money, and large portraits of the Chief
Sloth or Principal Alligator who runs the show, there
is still room to do business.

Wunderlich, Ltd., of Sydney and Mel-
bourne, manufacturers of ceiling and roofing materi-
als, etc. (the co. began with a disclosed deficit of
£1737, representing preliminary expenses) :

Feb., 11..22,916 2800 9 „ „ 11,033 9,083 26,649
Feb., 12..30,405 2800 9£ „ „ 11,646 15,959 42,608
Feb., 13..34,071 2800 10 „ „ 12,259 19,012 61,620
Feb., 14..32,877 3821 10 „ „ 13,599 15,457 77,077
During the year a further £60,000 of capital was
got in, £40,000 of it in second prefs., 7 p.c. cumu-
lative, the balance in ordinaries. That brings the
total up to £222,591, of which £BO,OOO carries a
7 p.c. pref. Apparently this extra capital went into
freeholds and towards forming a subsidiary co. in
Brisbane —Wunderlich and Steelart Ltd. It will be
noticed that in the table the reserves are described
as “nominal.” When the co. was formed the goodwill
items were bunched with tangible assets. Hence it
was impossible to ascertain their exact amount.
Since then, however, the items have been separated,
and no less than £65,702 was represented by in-
tangible assets. Of this £38,500 was for the good-
will of the Sydney business. Besides being able to
retain the name, the co. is in the fortunate position
of having the services of the three Wunderlichs as
permanent directors. Their unusual combination of
art and business capabilities has practically made
the business. Whether their successors will be able
to carry on the show as they have done is a matter
for time to settle. Hence the necessity for rapidly
wiping off the goodwill entry. But the co. has had
such an exceedingly prosperous existence that it has
been able to accumulate a Reserve Against Goodwill
equal to the amount of that item, besides an ordi-
nary reserve of £10,068. In other words, the
capital of the co. is now wholly represented by tan-
gible assets. It is a wonderful performance. During
the six years that the public has been interested in
the concern, the total net profits have been £159,856,
and the capital, up to last year, had ranged from
£144,507 to £162,591. Of course it must be re-
membered that Sydney has been enjoying an unpre-
cedented building boom of late, and most of the
buildings are of the better class, with the fine
finishing touches that help to swell Wunderlich’s
profits. However, the profits have been so gaudy as
compared with the capital that there could be a
very considerable shrinkage before the dividend rate
would be endangered. The co.’s financial position
is particularly sound. Liabilities amount to
£53,606. Assets include: Freeholds, £73,201 ;
machinery, etc., £38,939; investments (no doubt
mainly the Brisbane subsidiary co.), £51,118;
stocks, £80,458; book debts, £39,927; sundries,
£5357. So that assets are as much as £235,395
in excess of liabilities. Five years ago liabilities
were £41,399, and assets £134,450, a surplus of
£93,051. Thus, in five years, besides paying a very
respectable dividend and making repairs, etc., the
co. has put no less than £142,344 back into the
business.

F. W. Hughes, Ltd. (Sydney), manufac-
turers of wool-tops, who commenced business (so far
as this co. is concerned) with a deficit of £11,940,
representing goodwill (£11,566) and preliminary
exes.:—
Written
Profits. Dividends. Off. Deficit.
May, 1909 .. £11,940
Dec., 1909 .. £17,411 7 p.c. = £4,998 £4OOO 3,527
_ Reserves.
Dec., 1910 .. 18,587 10 „ „ 13,357 1,703
Dec., 1911 . . 12,506 6 „ „ 9,329 4000 880
Dec., 1912 . . 17,047 6 „ „ 9,899 6000 2,028
Dec., 1913 . . 10,835 5„ „ 8,350 4000 613
That looks very bad. Six years back the Govern-
ment offered a bonus of not exceeding £lO,OOO a
year for the production of wool-tops for export, and
this co. took advantage of the offer, and formed
itself into a public concern. The original offer
expired on Dec. 31 last, but the term was extended
for another two years. During 1913 the co. pro-
duced 2,615,5211 b. of wool-tops, on which the bonus,
equal to Id. per lb., would have amounted to £10,896,
but as £lO,OOO is the limit, presumably it did not
get the £896. Assuming that it received the
£lO,OOO, and there is no reason to suspect that it
did not, it will be seen by the table that only £835
of profit was made exclusive of the Government gift.
What will happen in, say, 1916 when the bonus will
be non-existent no one can tell. The term may, of
course, be again extended, but to date there is no
mention of it; and to buy shares on the off chance
that the Government will go on renewing the bonus
for two- or three-year periods is out of the question.
It will be noticed in the table (1) that the profits
are the smallest since the present co. took the busi-
ness over, (2) that after allowing for depreciation
and amortization of leases, two very necessary things,
the reserves have to be cut into to pay the dividend,
(3) that even then the rate of dividend is reduced,
and (4) that the reserves are only a miserable £513.
When this co. started there was a deficit of £11,940,
representing goodwill ( £11,566) and preliminary
exes., but the whole of this item has by this time
been wiped out. Now, no doubt, the shareholders
are beginning to wonder whether the goodwill was
worth what they paid for it. The directors enclose
a type-written note to the effect that “in view of the
fact that the company must hold heavy stocks for a
time against contracts made, for which the whole of
the resources are needed,” they have decided to
“defer for a few months the distribution of the divi-
dend.” Last year the same thing was done, only
then it was “on account of the stringent financial situ-
ation and of the commitments of the co. in the
future.” Whether even that dividend has been
paid yet, The Bulletin knows not. The co.’s
liabilities amount to £38,641, and the capital
now stands at £167,152. Of this, no less
than £164,272 is represented by “Plant, premises,
machinery, lease and water rights Stock
stands at £44,726, shipments in suspense £5661,
sundries £3997. Which, taken all round, is not a
particularlv cheerful situation, though it is not pos-
sible to tell exactly how the co. stands on account of
the lease and water rights being thrown into the gen-
eral heap. Expenses are showing up a little of late, too,
and in two years have moved from £61,227 to
£69.057. Another conglomeration that needs shat-
tering is “sales, shipments, and stocks of wool-tops,
basils, etc., and bonus on tons exported,” £473,472.
It amounted to £412,625 last year, but it is hopeless
to attempt to discover what progress the co. is mak-
ing. Of course, when the export bonus ceases to
come in the local market may expand, but it will
take a long time, and unless something very unex-
pected hapnens The Bulletin regrets that it cannot
be very optimistic.

for the goodwill of the business. In the table this
item is not regarded as an asset: —
