A full history of this co.’s early career was given in
the last post-mortem. The co. was founded in Ade-
laide, and had up to the time when the public be-
came interested branched out into Perth and Sydney.
During the past year it has done a good deal of
building and extension. New showrooms are being
built in Sydney and a branch has been opened in
Newcastle. The co. has also acquired the business
of the Foreman Foundry and Enamelling Co. in
Hobart. To pay for all these extensions 10,000
shares were issued at a small premium and 8679
were issued as payment for the Hobart business.
This extension campaign and the fact that the co.
has had more than its fair share of labor troubles,
especially in Perth and Adelaide, have com-
bined to make the year’s profit rather smaller than
usual. Yet though the profit was large enough to
stand a reduced dividend the directors have shown
considerable forethought in foregoing one for the
year. The whole of the net profit, £10,723, goes
to reserve, so that now all but £2707 of the capital
is represented by tangible assets, notwithstanding
that no less than £29,656 was paid for goodwill.
Gross profits for 1913-14 were £61,723, as compared
with £58,160 for 1912-13: but, the co. being a
very large employer of labor, expenses have increased
out of all proportion—from £36,496 to £46,004.
This comparison shows, among other things, the
expansion of the business etc., within 12 months: —

Land and buildings show a big increase, but as
most of this was paid for out of new capital, the
liabilities do not show a particularly big increase.
Sundry debtors are still on the large side, though
there is a slight decrease since last year. The growth
of the “other assets” item is, of course, made up of
shares in the Hobart acquisition. Taken all round,
however. The Bulletin isn’t alarmed at the sudden
drop in profits. Labor troubles are sure to be pretty
frequent for a year or two, but apart from that
there appears to be no cause for worry.

The capital is £lOO,OOO, of which £20,000 has been
handed out in bonus shares within the past four
years. In 1907 it was only £70,000 and the profits
were £8826. Now, sad to say, though it is £lOO,OOO
the profits are only £8532. Next year they may be
£5OOO or they may be back to the £lO,OOO mark;
it all depends on the season, both for wool and
wheat. This co. deals largely in fodder and station
necessaries, and the cocky buys just as much during
the year if it turns out to be a poor crop as he does
if it is a record one, and he pays for it with a P.N.
due in February. While the present run of good
seasons continues, Wright, Heaton’s profits will
boom. But when the run is broken the co. is liable
to find a heap of dishonored P.N.’s on hand, and a
consequent slump in profits; though if the W.-H.
management is as wise as The Bulletin gives it
credit for being, it has made some provision for
this. The co. is on a very solid position financially,
as this comparison will show
Liabilities.

Thus in five years the co.’s liabilities have increased
by only £17,979, and during- the same period assets
have shown a growth of no less than £78,492. It
is a wonderful showing, considering that the co. has
during the period paid a dividend of never less than
10 p.c. Book debts are certainly heavy, especially
on a capital of £lOO,OOO, but the reserves are so
considerable that the co. is able to keep going with
only £55,627 of outside money. The co. used to
carry on business in Queensland as well as N.S.W.,
but sold out the Northern undertaking. Besides
that it holds all the shares in the Victorian branch
of the McCulloch Carrying Company, which pays
a dividend of 124 p.c., and no doubt has consider-
able reserves of its own. These, of course, really
belong to Wright, Heaton and Co., though they are
not shown in the accounts.
* * * *-
Just two years ago (on April 25, 1912)
The Bulletin had a word or two to say about the
Williams-Endicott Banking Corporation, which,
through newspaper advertisements, invited applica-
tions for a batch of first mortgage 7 p.c. debentures,
to be secured by “one of the finest buildings to be
erected in Sydney.” Since then The Bulletin has
heard nothing of the co., and although it is still
mentioned in the financial year books it is crowded
right out of the directory. At the time of the co.’s
first mortgage 7 p.c. appearance this paper asked a
few questions. Here are some of them: (1) Where the
Williams-Endicott Banking Corporation hails from;
(2) Where the Banking Corporation was established ;
(3) When and where the £160,000 capital was paid
up, and how it is invested pending the commence-
ment of the proposed building operations; (4) What
business, if any, the Banking Corporation has done in
the past, and where; (5) The location of the build-
ings to be erected. It got no replies to the ques-
tions at the time. Now it digs up the dead body of
the problem, repeats the questions, and adds some
more. (1) Where is the Williams-15ndicott Banking
Corporation; (2) When is it going to begin building
operations on a large scale; (3) Did anyone in Sydney
put up any cash, and if so how did the speculation
flourish ? The Bulletin also added: “A censor of
public companies, who would pass judgment on a
new institution before it began to take the public’s
money and assure the community that it was a
feasible affair, and a State auditor who would in-
spect it regularly in later years are two officials
whose appointment is worth consideration.”

The item in the table situated just about where Fat
wears his watch chain takes some of the polish- off
the 8 p.c. dividend. Still it was better that the
accumulated wear should be written off out of
capital in one lump than that dividends should be
starved for years in the hope of making good the
loss out of earnings. The co. shows quite a respect-
able increase all round for this year. It runs four
lines of business: (1) it hawks its own goods up the
Murray and other back country rivers; (2) it
brings the farmers' produce down on the return
trip; (3) it is now sole owner of a sawmill in which
it had formerly held only a share; and (4) it does a
pretty extensive carrying business on land. The river
department, say the directors, still shows a small
profit ; last year it showed “disappointing results.”
But it is a risky business; when the rivers are low,
as they often are, the boats are off duty, and when
there is plenty water there is generally a corres-
ponding supply of snags. That means a big repair
bill. Last year repairs to river plant ran into
£lO4B, and for the previous year £1347 ; besides
which, of course, there is pretty extensive deprecia-
tion to provide for. Like other up-country trading
cos. Permewan, Wrights have a goodly stock of book-
debts ; but with this class of concern big book
debts mean big business, and that is the main thing,
given, of course, good management. That and other
matters are shown in this comparison, extending
over a fair period:—
1901. 1910. 1912. 1913. 1914.
Liabilities . .£28,014 £30,781 £47,501 £40,325 £40,184
Plant .. .. 47,358 38,870 45,824 45,842 49,682
Book debts .. 48,981 34,990 46,080 44,912 41,413
Merchandise .. 9,270 10,537 14,704 13,835 17,828
Mi icellaneous.. 2,107 3,044 3,208 2,740 3,106
Thus it will be seen that on a capital of £50,000
debts amount to £41,413. The result is that a
large amount of borrowed money is needed in the
business. But the debts are no doubt mainly far-
mers’ P.N.’s due, as usual, in February. Under the
circumstances, it might not be a bad scheme if cos.
who do a big country trade, made up their accounts
to the end of March. However, the table shows that
Permewan, Wrights are quite safe financially, and
provided they keep their plant well written down,
which no doubt they will do after past experiences,
the profits should show a steady increase, though
The Bulletin will be better pleased when the re-
serves are double what they are now.

This is, without a doubt, the black sheep of the Aus-
tralian family of co-operative stores. In the first
place it spent too much money op premises; then
it found that these premises, its chief store, were in
a very out-of-the-way place, and business began to
be desperately bad. Last year the chief stores were
sold at a loss, on the book value,. of about £3OO.
The co. then moved into temporary premises, and
finally into its new buildings. All this moving
about meant that the “central” store made a fat loss
of £4490 for the six months, and that the cake
manufactory lost £B5; however, the bakery—one of
the co.’s side-lines —made a profit of £Bl2, and
the Williamstown branch was £.B to the good, so
that the loss for the half-year was reduced to £3755.
The “capital forfeited” line which drops in regularly
every half-year needs a little explanation. About five
years ago the directors suddenly woke up to the
fact that shareholders weren’t taking any interest in
the concern, wherefore they decided that, unless a
shareholder spent at least £l2 a year at the store,
he or she must pay a forfeit, such forfeit to be
deducted from the shareholder’s capital holding.
The result is that while the capital in Jan., 1909,
was £63,340, distributed among 13,283 members, it
is now only £37,067, and there are 7823 members.
Sales during the same period have fallen from
£80,895 to £59,524. The co. has not paid a
dividend since Jan., 1908, when it handed out
£l2OB, and it has not distributed a bonus since
July, 1907. The total bonus distributions amount
to £8351, as compared with £251,716 for the Sydney
co. During the past half-year the directors have been
making a raid on the expense item, with a view to
bringing it down somewhere near the income; if
that is the case it plight be as well if they started
on themselves first. There is a whole army of them —
nine in all, when five would be quite enough to run
the show. Liabilities amount to £54,791, and
capital is £37,067. Against that, stock stands at
only £22,675. Sales are by no means small, although
there is a constant shrinkage, and a loss of £3755
when sales are £59,525 simply shrieks that some-
where the management is, to say the least of it,
rotten,

The Bulletin hates to remind these building socie-
ties of their past, especially when they are ob-
viously trying to go straight. But when a co. with
a history as dazzling as this one’s pays a dividend
far in excess of what it should, not once, but year
after year, it is time its past performances were
hurled at it. When the smash came, just on 21
years ago, this co. was left with liabilities of no less
than £254,000, and its assets consisted mainly of
a heap of suburban allotments worth nothing like
what they stood for in the books, and an absurdly
long name. The result was that its £5 shares—
there were 28,092 of them—were written down to
305., and the debenture holders were asked to forego
3 p.c. of their interest, which they did, having not
much choice. This table will show how things
have moved of late years :
Liabilities.
1903. 1908. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Capital ..£42,099 £42.138 £42,138 £42,138 £45,000
Debentures .. 49,144 Nil Nil Nil Nil
Other debts.. 47,337 28,547 1,217 624 753
Assets.
Advances .. 29,009 16,780 11,575 10,440 19,003
Estates, etc... 123,766 75,489 45,462 43,300 41,275
Cash, etc. .. 9,835 4,349 7,587 9,441 7,551
Now that the co. has almost cleaned up its slate—
there are only £753 of liabilities left—it lets itself
off the chain and hands out fat dividends again.
Lately it has resumed the loan business and hopes
thereby to make enough to induce the shareholders
to forget and possibly forgive. But on a capital of
£45,000 and reserves of £16,126, a very extensive
loan business is out of the question; and until the
brake is put on dividends the reserves will never be
much help. Good progress has been made as re-
gards getting rid of the suburban holdings, though
most of the sales have been made at below even
written-down values. Of the £41,275 now on hand
the Glebe Estate accounts for £34,500, the Waterloo
Estates for £6725, and the balance is in suburban
property, the total rent for 1913 being £5208.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.; ■
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

London Stores, Ltd., which bought a
men’s outfitting business in Melbourne, and started
with various goodwill and other intangible items
representing £26,368 of the capital:—
Towards
Reduction
Written of De- De-
Feb., Profits. Dividends. Off. ficit. fait-
jgjj. ——•— £2oiooo
1912 ..£7,293 Bp.c.= £4200 £3093 23,275
£1666 added to goodwill items . . 24.941
1913 .. 9,702 8 p.c.= 5600 £1596 2506 22,435
1914 .. 11,003 8 ~ „ 5600 704 4699 17,736
Perhaps a better view of the co.’s position is afforded
bv this comparison:—
1912. 1913. 1914.
Sundry creditors .. £7,310 £5,368 £5,526
Goodwill items .. 29,459 25,025 24,325
Sundrj' debtors .. 3,257 3,095 3,230
Stock .. .. 31,237 39,952 37,250
Plant and fittings .. 5,624 6,807 6,332
Cash, etc. .. .. 14,728 6,184 14,086
Thus it will be noticed that intangible assets still
stand at a fairly large amount, £24,325. But
this needs a little explanation. In accordance with
the co.’s articles of association, which stipulate that
each year at least 10 p.c. of the profits available for
dividends must go towards a reserve for replacing
the goodwill items with something more inflammable,
a pretty solid reserve is now being built up. With
the £3OOO to be added out of the past year’s profits,
this reserve will amount to £5245. This is of
course equivalent to writing that amount off the
goodwill, etc. When that item disappears entirely
The Bulletin will be able to look more kindly on
the co.’s accounts. A very bright feature is the
diminutive size of the liabilities ( £5526), and the
handsome amount of cash and more or less liquid
assets, amounting in all to £17,000 odd. When a
co. works almost entirely on its own money like this
it is in the favorable position of being able to pay
cash for its own purchases. And the co., especially
in the retail line, that confines itself to cash pur-
chases, can always give customers a better deal than
the one in which both customers and co. do their
business on long credit. Naturally the co. is begin-
ning to have its share of increased expenses, and
shows a growth in that item from £24,809 in 1911-12
and £35,587 in 1912-13 to £36,539 for the past 12
months. However, gross profits have been moving
along too, and to Feb., 1914, were £47,532, as
compared with £45,289 to Feb., 1913, and £29,991
for the previous 12 months. On a capital of £70,000,
of which a large amount is in goodwill assets, this
is a splendid showing. When the whole of the
capital is represented by solid assets and it is no
longer necessary to take that 10 p.c. bite out of
profits, shareholders should be on a good wicket.
The Bulletin can’t swear as to the quality of the
shirts the London Stores supplies, but it certainly
turns out a very respectable balance-sheet.
* * * *
Broken Hill Proprietary expects to pro-
duce over 40,000 tons for the half-vear ending
May 31. A run of four weeks to April 8 gave 2184
tons lead from its own, and 4482 tons from pur-
chased ore, a total of 6666 tons. The silver output
was 299,9580 z. In 16 weeks this co. has produced
9401 tons of lead on its own account, and 14,629
tons from purchased ore. The smaller quantity of
domestic lead contained 632,9270 z. of silver, while
the purchased ore gave only 571,8230 z. Last month’s
lot of 2184 tons of domestic lead contained
157,7910 z. silver, over 70oz. per ton, and 4482 tons
of lead from purchased ore gave 172,1670 z., an aver-
age of less than 390 z. per ton. The Proprietary also
produced 23,545 tons of zinc concentrates in 16
weeks, containing over 11,000 tons of zinc.
*■*-**
For a run of four weeks the Amalgamated
Zinc (Broken Hill) treated 40,696 tons of tailings
for 11,991 tons of concentrates, assaying 48.6 p.c.
That would mean 4827 tons of zinc, which at
£2l 10s. is worth £103,880. Yet the profit esti-
mated by the co. is only £10,225. Silver values
and 88 tons of lead concentrates are thrown in. If
spelter falls the profit on zinc concentrates will
whittle down some more. But lower prices will do
good if they force the Australian producer—now
taking a miserable profit of 425, per ton of zinc—
to smelt the ore on the Australian seaboard on top
of one of our magnificent coal seams.
* * * *
The Grafton copper mine (N.S.W.) has
a new lens of ore in F level. The ore is high-
grade sulphide for a width of from 3ft. to 4ft. A
winze below this level is down 16ft., with sft. of
ore showing in the eastern end. Smelting has been
started again, so evidently the Grafton isn’t fright-
ened at the low price of copper. The chairman at
the annual meeting remarked that “metal” can be
produced for £37 ss. per ton. Taking “metal” (a
technical term) to mean 62 p.c. matte, the working
cost is £6O per tori of fine copper.
The Iron Duke copper mine in the Dan-
daloo district (N.S.W.) reports a development of
dense sulphide ore 7ft. wide in its new shaft at
the southern end of the lease. Basic ore is required
to flux the silicious ore met with at the 90ft. and
113 ft. levels in the northern section. The 180 ft.
level is regarded as the sulphide zone, but the sul-
phides in the northern section are silicious. So far
no large body of basic ore has been proved. This
mine will be eventually served by the Tullamore-
Tottenham railway, which will cheapen the supply
of coke.

From a Queensland oil enthusiast: —
Now that the Queensland Government has nation-
alised the oil industry, it has had a pretty exhaustive
report made by the Government geologist on the
Roma fields. N.S.W. Geologist Carne is alleged to
have put the chances of finding oil at Roma at nil;
however, in W. E. Cameron’s report, on which the
Roma Co. puts a lot of trust, there is more optimism.
It is of course known for certain that large quan-
tities of gas exist underground at Roma, and Cameron
looks hopefully at this. He says: “The fact that at
Roma a second bore, sunk only 234 feet from a bore
which had already flowed gas at the rate of from
40,000 to 70,000 cubic feet annually for 5J years,
itself struck a much more violent outburst of gas at
about the same denth, seems to me to carry great
possibilities for this district in this respect....
This analysis shows that Roma gas is similar in
composition to many of the gases found associated
with oil in die oil fields of America and Baku....
The gas from the Roma Gas Bore had a distinct
‘paraffin’ odor on the occasion of my visit to this
bore in 1904, when it was flowing freely with the
water from the sin. casing... .The discovery of the
Bremen Oil Field (U.S.A.) is a result of the close
association of oil and gas. As is well known, every
oil well is to a greater or less extent a gas well, and
large gas wells are seldom remote from 0i1.... There
seems no geological reason why oil in large quantities
should not be found in the Roma district, and at
much shallower depths than those at which gas was
struck in the Roma bore. The occurrence of this gas
in quantity is a hopeful Indication that deposits of
oil. occur.”
* # * *
For the first three months of this year.
Mount Elliott (Q.) produced 1158 tons of copper,
1840 oz. gold and 23150 z. silver from 11,428 tons of
ore. How long it can maintain a 10 p.c. extraction
is uncertain, because it is drawing its ore from the
parent mine, which is heavily depleted and from out-
side mines, the most important of which, the Hamp-
don Consols, has been only partly developed. With
the MacGregor and Mount Cuthbert lines open for
traffic a decided impetus will be given to mine de-
velopment and customs smelting. The tendency is to
centralise smelting, and it may be to the advantage
of Mount Cuthbert, which has a large quantity of
ore developed, to send it to a central smeltery. The
Corrella Co., with its Rosebud mine, is also con-
sidering a tramway to connect with the MacGregor
line. With regard to development, Mount Cuthbert
is now crosscutting at its Warwick Castle (150 ft.
level) and its Kalkadoon leases with a view to open-
ing up ore at depth. The Kalkadoon has a strong
ore body down to 100 ft., so it will be interesting to
watch development work at the 250 ft. level.
The prospectus of the Young Australia
Copper Mine (Queensland) is about as benevolent a
proposition as The Bulletin has met for
some time. The lease (40 acres) was taken over
20 months ago by the present holders, and “various
parcels returned a percentage as high as 52 p.c., one
parcel going as high as 55 p.c.” Also “there is
about 200 tons of saleable ore at grass averaging
about 15 p.c. to 20 p.c., and 50 tons suitable for
screening of 10 p.c.” Total, 35 tons of copper, at,
say, £65 per ton = £2275. And presumably there
is any amount of untouched ore. The mine can be
worked for £2O to £25 a week, it is “fairly well
equipped,” and there is a plentiful supply of water.
There are to be 50 shares of £lO each, the vendors
to take four of them as well as the cash realised
from the other 46. In other words, they are asking
only £460 in cash and four £lO shares for a pro-
perty like that 1 This paper is always ready to take
off its hat to real philanthropy when it meets the
article.
* * * •
Quite the brightest thing in Gympie
mining at present is the success of McLeod’s New
Wilmot, which has been plugging away quietly for
many months past, and developing effectively on
sound mining principles. During this period of its
existence much caustic criticism was heaved at
McLeod’s by the large army of Gympie experts—and
there are more amateur mining geologists to the
square foot in Gympie than in any other mining
camp on earth. But McLeod’s has survived it all,
and during the past six months gold worth £4600
has been won from stone averaging 15dwt. (including
one crushing under 9dwt.). It is now proposed to
erect another 30 head of stamps, with a view to
more economical working. The reports generally are
refreshing compared with the usual Gympie stuff.
* * * *
Lode Hill (Bloomfield River, N.Q.)
shareholders are very optimistic, and on the strength
of the first run which gave 21-J tons, expect an annual
yield of 250 tons of black tin or thereabouts. Lode
Hill is rather an out of the way camp, so it seems a
risky thing to value black tin (72 p.c.) at more than
£lOO per ton, at which the 250 tons would be worth
£25,000. On 100,000 shares, the much talked of
Is. per quarter will cost £20,000 a year, so that the
mine would need to show a profit of £20,000 from
tin worth £25,000. The co. calculates working
costs at 3d. per cubic yard, and its yield at lib.,
and on that basis one ton can be produced for £2B.
But £BO per ton profit is required to bring in
£20,000 from 250 tons of stream tin, so the Is. a
quarter doesn’t look very safe. The latest report is
that there is a 70ft. face, plenty of water, and all
looking well. It would require to look well to stand
a capitalised value of £85,000.
* * * *
New Langi Logan, Ararat (Vic.), mainly
on account of cheap expert opinion, went into Easter
vacation mournfully. No. 1 rise workings haven’t
swung right into 2oz. dirt like the Cathcart and the
Central Cathcart, and drives are not properly into
the wash yet. A change was expected when No. 2
rise got up into the high reef, but “Bill” Toole
so far hasn’t reported bags of gold, as he isn’t
in the wash far enough to scrape up a decent
bucket-full off the bottom.
* * * *
Tom Birkbeck’s barge at Toora, South
Giopsland (Vic.), is at work groping in the tailings
left there a decade ago. But as the men then knew
as much about tin sluicing as the new-comers there
can’t be much profit till new ground can be reached.
When that will happen isn’t clear, for no one appears
to know where the old-timers knocked off. The de-
lay has used up most of the available cash, so
London has had to pass the hat round among hope-
ful shareholders in order to furnish another £15,000,
which means adding to an already overloaded share
register. It is rumored that there is an offer from
an influential quarter to underwrite the issue; but
at what cost The Bulletin can’t say just yet.
* *■ * *
Victorian dredging cos. are up against fur-
ther worries. Drysdale Brown, who runs the Mines
Department, has evidently been frightened by the re-
port of the Sludge Commission. So he pastes up a
notice that in future every covenant in a dredging
lease must be fulfilled. That wouldn’t have been so
bad if Brown hadn’t been the leading light of the
Charlotte Plains and other Bull alluvial shows
that didn’t observe all the conditions of the leases
during the long years they had to struggle with
adversity.
i * » *
For March Tongkah Harbor (Siam) pro-
duced 107 tons 7cwt. of tin concentrates. This
brings the half-year to a close under conditions
slightly less favorable for profit making than the 12
months to Sept. 30. The output for that period
was 1339 tons of concentrates, averaging
£llB 7s. Id. per ton. Royalty has to be paid to the
Siamese Government, so that tin is sold under less
favorable conditions than in Australia. An average
profit of £5O is required on 300 tons of concen-
trates per quarter to pay 2s. per share ( £15,000).
Tongkah may maintain an average of 100 tons of
oxide per month, but it is too much to expect high
yields and high prices all the time. In spite of the
heavy slump in the metal during the last 12
months shares have risen from 365. to 475. since
January. Future payments include the redemption
of debentures in June, 1915, the purchase of No. 6
dredge‘for about £26,000, and a balance of £10,500
due to the Siam Government. Dividends declared
to date total 295. per £1 share.
* * * *
Law, jam and leather stand sponsors for
the Trong Tin No-Liability in the persons of J. S.
Meagher, A. W. Palfreyman, and D. T. Bedggood.
The promotors are the Perak Rivers Tin Co., whoever
they might be, though it is known that those three
are interested. The Perak Rivers Co., like the
promotors of the Debook and the Austral Malay, are
not over-modest in their requests, and of 60,000
shares take 30,000, besides £2500 in cash. This is
in exchange for 720 acres, of which 150 acres are
reported by R. T. Hagger to average 2.361 b. of tin
oxide per cubic yard, and 180 acres to go lib. per
cubic yard. The ground is all virgin, which makes
the proposition more attractive. Judging by Tom
Horton’s check boring it doesn’t look as though there
will be the slightest difficulty in bringing off a
float.
To the end of March Radium Hill (S.A.)
had completed one half of the English order for
£l4OO worth of radium. For 14 days to March 28,
10 tons 16cwt. of concentrates were produced, but
no mention is made of results. The No. 2 shaft is
down 150 ft., and at 142 ft. there was an improve-
ment in the ore. The lode is now 4ft. wide, with
well defined walls.
Taking everything into consideration,
Mount Lyell did well to produce 3433 tons of blister
copper last half-year, and the dispatches, totalling
3517 tons to Port Kembla, will give the half-year’s
profit a lift. Still, it requires to increase the out-
put of ore and metal for there are so many shaves
that the Is. 3d. distribution calls for over £Bl,OOO.
The directors cannot screw that amount out of 3433
tons of blister; but there has been a considerable
profit, and Lyell’s superphosphate works may do a
good deal towards paying the next dividend. Ap-
proximately 165,594 tons of ore were treated last
half-year, of which 59.981 tons came from North
Lyell.
* * * *
The post-mortem of the Tasmania mine,
Beaconsfield, has been placed by the Tasmanian
Government in the hands of W. H. Cuhdy, of Bendigo,
and J. K. Fawcett, of Sydney. But whatever they
say isn’t likely to count for anything in the face of
the advice of Manager Heathcote and of John
Taylor’s man to draw the pumps. Of course if
Launceston has the balance of power it may be able
to deal with the balance of surplus assets as it likes.
But that won’t keep Bull in hand, and Bull comes in
useful when there’s a bill to pay.
* * * *
Edna May Central’s recent development is
expected to add considerably to the ore reserves; or,
rather, its local supporters say so. The ore struck
appears to be a continuation of that worked in the
Edna May East, where it is declared to be worth
15dwt. over a width of 14ft. In view of the doubts
that are being expressed as to the ability of the new
mill to keep going for any length of time when
crushing starts, it would be interesting to know how
much developed ore is available in the Central for
treatment. An independent report would best settle
this point.
* * * *
Bar One (W.A.) : I have come to the fol-
lowing conclusions in respect to the Edna May: (1)
That the developed ore in sight is equivalent to
about 30s. per share in dividends and to eight or nine
months’ mill supply; (2) that the co.’s policy of
development has been a dawdling one, and, if per-
sisted in, may land it in a fix sooner than its worst
detractor predicts; (3) that the granite menace isn’t
at present serious; (4) that it is nearly time the
Board thought about installing a rock-drilling plant.
At present there is not an air-drill on the mine, and
if hard ground was unexpectedly struck the show
would soon be up a tree as regards development. It
is simply juggling with fortune to buy into a mine
whose shares are quoted at over 300 p.c. premium,
but whose actual reserves are only some few months
ahead of the mill. Of course the good developments
that are now taking place in the bottom must not be
forgotten when reckoning market valuation, but at
the present price of shares the value of these seems
to have been more than anticipated by the market.
* * * *
Mararoa (W.A.) has gone to Holden’s
Find in search of something new. Some money is
to be spent on boring shows now held under option
by the Ruby Well Options syndicate, and if any-
thing worth dangling before the eyes of the Adelaide
and Melbourne public is met with, a new co. is to be
formed to work it. A good deal of optimism is still
being indulged in regarding Holden’s, but the opinion
expressed about it in back numbers of this paper
is still adhered to. If Holden’s is the only place
left in the West in which moneyed cos. like the
Mararoa can look for new shows, the State, as a
field for mining speculation, must be crumbling very
fast.
* * * *
Mary Mac. (W.A.) fancies itself for hav-
ing dodged reconstruction, but wait awhile. The
working account for the last six months shows a
loss of £527; £1360 is owing to the bank, £205
to sundry creditors, and all the co. had in
the cupboard when the books closed was £4OO.
Tribute royalties are being relied on to pull it out
of the mud, but with a patchy show and a reef, more
likely than not to cut out at a shallow depth, as a
standby, its chances appear slender.
*** . *
Westralia’s gold output for March tumbled
10,000 oz. compared" with February of this year, and
5000 oz. compared with March of 1913. Locally the
fall is regarded as being “disappointing and inex-
plicable,” but it can be accounted for by the stop-
page of returns at the Associated Northern’s Ora
Banda show, the three weeks’ breakdown of the
Queen of the Hills plant, and reduced yields from the
Ivanhoe and Bullfinch —a fire being the cause of
the former and a creep of the latter.
The Melbourne-controlled Speakmans
Hampton Plains and Melbourne Hampton Plains syn-
dicates are joining forces with a view to floating a
co. to work their W.A. shows on a comprehensive
basis. Both were reported on a few months ago
bv Miller, late of the Long Tunnel, and, according
to him, a reasonable estimate of the probable ore
that would be opened up if certain work was carried
out was 250,000 to 300,000 tons, and the probable
value of two-thirds of this tonnage was given as
7dwt. A little later Miller supplemented this state-
ment bv a remark that the deposit was huge, and its
size could not be grasped by figures. An influential
crowd is at the back of both concerns.
* ♦ * *
Golden Horseshoe Board recently invited
geologist Larcombe, of the Kalgoorlie School of Mines,
to say something about the lower levels of the mine.
Broadly speaking, Larcombe is hopeful about the lode
at depth, and regards the property as the most
favored one on the field. He believes that the felsite
slates which have had such a deleterious influence on
some of the best ore bodies in the district, will not
reach the No. 4 lode opposite the main shaft before
a depth of 3500 ft. is reached, and in any case they
will only be met in Horseshoe ground at much greater
depths than in adjoining mines. As regards the calc
schist which Dr. Maclaren believes will engulf the
productive Kalgoorlie mines, Larcombe assigns a
minimum depth in the Horseshoe main shaft of
5700 ft., at which the rock is likely to be encountered.
All the geological factors, in his opinion, place the
mine, as regards longevity, in the best position on
the Golden Mile, and, as the general pitch of the
shoots is south great hopes are entertained by him
for the southern and middle portions of the property
along the eastern side. _

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 13, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
Governor Strickland opened the big
Easter parade of fat st-ock, as soon as the
band had blared forth the National Anthem.
The ancient stand, which cannot be called
“grand” except in the same sense that the
Holy Roman Empire was so described be-
cause it wasn’t holy or Roman or an empire,
was packed with people, who stubbornly kept
their seats for hours before the event in
the hope of hearing something new. In a
grandstand you are exceedingly pleased to
get a humble seat. But the official brain
had poured forth most of its treasures at
the huge feed which always opens the Show
with a corkscrew. President Francis Suttor,
about the best-preserved specimen of 70
summers hereabouts, was in his usual spot
adjacent to the vice-regal elbow.
The Stricklands’ victoria, with Lady Ede-
line, accompanied by various official dames
who hopped in and out of the seat of honor,
trotted into the middle of the Show ground.
Beef, in a magnificent bellowing and belli-
cose circle garnished the outer circle of the
emerald green ring. It stood more or less at
ease, being held by an unsympathetic ring
in its nose, and butted the air, or main-
tained a haughty bovine calm, according to
its _ temper. Satin-skinned draught horses
whisked a two-inch tail apiece, tortured into
a ribbon bob, and sidled round their keepers,
while the Strickland kindergarten ambled all
over the place to see things.
Admiral Patey brought his wife along to
see the Show officially torn open. She is a
slender, dark-eyed woman, with a smile like
a small benediction, and was draped like an
attractive fashion plate in bright pink—the
plain, uncompromising pink the Futurist
hurls at a yard of canvas. Flag-Lieut. Poe,
a much larger man than the average official
shadow to a naval frill, wore his silk hat at
the same saucy angle as the tile that was
perched on the W. G. Layton brow.
Prime Minister Joe Cook and his lady
came over here for the great Horse carnival.
He is thinner than of yore, and wears the
hurried, anxious expression of a politician
who is for ever in pursuit of his majority of
one, lest it should fall ill by the way or trip
over a moving tram. Mrs. Joe is a tall,
strong-minded-looking dame, given to no-
nonsense millinery. Something that might
have been mistaken for a yard and a half
of greenish-blue Liberal plank was set side-
ways on to Mrs. Prime Minister’s determined
black hat the other afternoon. This
is the general effect of the new autumn
headgear, but it has to be worn with dis-
cretion.
The John Madden family, of Victoria, is
so distractingly alike in its sleek, well-fed
contour that when it makes its biennial
descent on Sydney it has to be carefully
sorted out to avoid wrong labels. Mrs. Pat
Osborne, one of the flock, was promptly
recognised at the Show the other day by a
certain heaven-born dimple in one cheek.
With her was her cousin, Mrs. Tony Hor-
dern, in a light, summery frock, top-dressed
by a bewitching little hat of the soft,
squashy species.
Miss Alice McCulloch, mounted on her
magnificent grey gelding, “Wait-and-See,”
was the star woman rider at the Show in
spite of the fine horsemanship of Mrs. Stace,
who is still faithful to the side-saddle. If a
lady swallow could ride astride in a per-
fectly-tailored divided coat, put her toes into
riding boots, and her head into a bowler
hat shaped like a rather flat pudding basin,
she would probably take the fences in the
same flight as this girl with the bird-like
habit of skimming timber. Sydney can
spare three cheers for a Victorian, who was
acclaimed by two very horsey Englishwomen
the other day as “the finest woman rider
astride they had ever seen either in Britain
or on the Continent.”
The pace at which most of the jumps were
taken in the show ring was too slow for
cross-country riding. Possibly it was the
comparatively leisurely attack of “Wait-and-
See” on the fences that lost Alice McCulloch
the blue ribbon and sent her off with second
prize in the Lindeman Cup event. She was
a winner in other contests, and a long way
ahead of competition in her style and velvet
hands on her horse’s mouth.
Even a city woman can lift up her pen
and make it kick against the greed which
has exported most of the best horseflesh in
this State, and left the strain so deteriorated
that importations get a majority of the show
prizes. Many of the gee-gees in some of the
ring events were a weedy-looking lot, and,
besides, bore external proofs of mighty littl?
grooming in their shabby coats. The Welsh
cob got its muscle up long ago climbing hills
like the sides of steeples with Llewellyn, and
his harp, and his family Bible, and his wife
on its back. Sometimes it is even driven to
walk head-downwards on the roof of things,
so inverted is the country. This animal
from St. David’s land is coming into fashion
here, and is a handsome, sturdy, little beast
suited to rough country. Bachden-Dewr, the
cob from Gwent, owned by S. E. Roberts, of
Barwang, got away with a first prize in
last week’s clatter of hoofs at Moore Park.
A Master of Horse to the King of Siam
was one of the non-official exhibits at the
Show. The King of Siam is not a tremen-
dous personage as royalties go, outside his
mahogany-colored realm. But he keeps up a
certain amount of state round his dusky per-
son, and a Master of Horse is a fine up-
standing title to help things along. When I
become really affluent I intend to hire one to
cook, and water the pot plants and do odd
jobs about the house. The Siamese visitor,
clad in the garb that accompanies a black
silk hat, made a dignified progress round the
opening fuss at the Show. He was shadowed
by a European secretary—the King’s shadow
and the King’s shadow’s shadow. Again I
make a good Easter-time resolution. When
great wealth comes my way I will have a
dignified secretary, who will take in the meat
at the back gate in three languages.
Having unfortunately mislaid the name of
our recent visitor —the King of Siam’s
Master of Horse —I am forced to take refuge
in his royal employer’s nomenclature. This
is Chowfa Maha Vajirawah, whose decease
will not be unduly mourned by his heir-pre-
sumptive, Chowfa Chakrapongse Poowanarth.
These words have to be kept “right side up
with care,” and when they are unloaded from
the train the trunk containing them must not
be dashed down recklessly on the platform.
For if the trunk should burst, and the cog-
nomens escape and spread in a thin blue
haze over the city, so that the inhabitants
would be wading knee-deep in Siamese desig-
nation, not even the best dentists, surgeons,
physicians and herbalists can foretell the
consequences.
Race Week opened on a day of blue and
gold, an Easter Saturday which even the
grandfather of grumblers couldn’t have found
fault with. The clothes which paraded up
and down the lawn—the wearers don’t seem
to count on these occasions—were not ad-
dicted to many frenzies. A bright purple-
clad woman hung with ropes of beads about
her skirt was out for notice, and got it.
Plaid in subdued tartans jerked itself into
frivolous bows, as on pretty Mrs. Jim Gor-
don’s hat, or toned down to a mere sugges-
tion as in the dark blue silk worn by Mrs.
Steer Bowker with a chic black velvet hat
that was a kind of millinery Napoleon
junior. Mrs. Tom Rolin, who has a liberal
share of the Bowker good looks, being a
daughter of the late medico of that name,
swathed in filmy black, showed us how to
wear a striped silk waistcoat without undue
bulginess. Mrs. Phipps Waugh, who is a
handsome blonde, was frocked in the vivid
shade of blue that is like a small, deep pool
of sea water. She was with her sister, Mrs.
E. Waters, of Melbourne. One of the Philip
Morton girls, in black, had her head firmly
squeezed into the new flyaway toque species.
Sydney’s Mayoress, Mrs. Richards, and Mr.
Mayor were a smiling contribution to the
officials present.
McPhillamys, Campbells (of Inverell),
McMasters and the innumerable Osbornes
can be mentioned by the hundredweight
or ton this Easter. The woolly Mcßeans
from somewhere on the Deniliquin sheep
plains are in town for the horse
melee. The Bucklands, from Pine Ridge
and the Jim Allisons, of Coomoo-
Coomoo station, have been here for quite a
while. And several carefully-assorted
samples of the Ryrie family who are mostly
in the sheep business, and the usual sprinkle
of naval frills, may be gazed on free of
charge by anyone who paces the Randwick
turf this week.
A curious feature of the average man’s
attitude towards a modern race meeting in
this settlement is his go-as-you-please
clothes. The top-hat and frock-coat, pepper
and salt etceteras, white spats, and other
trimmings of the old-time dandy are by no
means extinct. They are infrequent, but they
still linger on the face of the earth.
Opposition-leader Wade, who has inad-
vertently caught the new figure—the ingenue
slump—goes to a race meeting in the same
kind of loose grey drapery which he wears
when he peers into the empty Government
Treasury. Ex-M.L.A. Wood has also taken
to the Anyhow garb of daily toil. His pretty
missus (a daughter of the General
George French who never did any-
thing of any startling importance worth
mentioning in this State), on the con-
trary, is always decked in graceful
finery. Solicitor Alfred Nathan was about
the best-groomed male in the legal fratern-
ity who stamped round the betting trees.
Barrister Arthur Kelynack trusted to a hur-
ried looking serge to see him safely through. It
is part of the arduous occupation of being a
woman to go forth and spend all that she
has, or that her husband has, or that both of
them have not, on race-week rags. There-
fore a full and detailed account of the un-
brushed unimportant duds in which Man
leads forth his gorgeously-attired seraglio,
would make a restful break in the yards of
ink devoted to female clothes.
The Downes family, from Brov -low Hill,
near Cobbitty, are in town for the madding
Easter whirl. Mrs. Downes, junior, the new
bride, tripped round the members’ stand at
Randwick the other afternoon with her good-
looking sister-in-law, Ethelwyn. She was
decked in an attractive frock of oyster-col-
ored skimmer, dashed with Bulgarian em-
broidery.
The cheeky-looking Sports’ coat, which
reminds one of the old illustrations in some
of Anthony Trollope’s stories —surely Lily
Dale ate syllabub on the lawns at Allington
with a garment of this kind about her slo-
ping shoulders —made several appearances at
Randwick this meeting. On Saturday Mrs.
Ivenna wore one of these autumn frivolities
in periwinkle blue, over a light frock, and
top-dressed the lot with a small hat of gold-
plaited straw. This 1914 species of milli-
nery is generally fashioned rather like
the early Victorian cottage bonnet, and is
first cousin twice removed to the coal-scuttle
of the same period. The latter made a meek
appearance on the first race day, being about
half the size of its great-great-grandmother.
One hat of this persuasion bristled in front
with a fierce display of floriculture. Mrs.
J. J. Rouse had popped her dark hair into a
less aggressive scuttle, and lynx-eyed spec-
tators saw no reason to weep at the result.
Fur, which used to be a serious affair
solemnly affixed to solid matter, is now a
light and fluffy adjunct to ninon and similar
frothy materials. Race-week finery lingers
in the alluring realm of the floating trans-
parencies that marked the summer fashions,
in spite of a sub-layer, so to speak, of
winter goods. The leopard, however, lent
his spots to the Randwick lawn in limited
supplies. Two or three long, furry leopard
drapes, attended by large muffs and accom-
panied by partially-submerged females,
trailed across the sward on the first race day.
But they gave the wearers (who were sup-
ported by the knowledge that no one ques-
tioned the fiendish expense of the goods)
the hurried look of having seized a few fur
mats and dragged them out for an airing.
The female head which has supported
everything, from a bang with a club to a
Paris hat, is now indulging in a small mil-
linery entrde —a mere snack of the tottering,
feathery monuments of October. The latest
indiscretion is not so tame as it looks at a
first glance. Sideways on, it supports a
huge silk or velvet bow, about the size of a
semaphore arm. One of these last yelps of
fashion —a dark-brown affair —snuggled
down on the Mrs. Alec Hay’s trim head the
other day. It outclassed anything in the
way of a bow that came near it, and was
followed bv the respectful gaze of less hatted
dames.
Miss Alice Franklin, who has been diving
deep into social and educational work in
London, is here for a while. She is a niece
of Postmaster-General Herb. Samuel, in the
Asquith team, but is otherwise not specially
interested in the domestic habits of stamps.
Mrs. Pritchard Bassett and her small
daughter are out of town dabbling in the
toy waves that lap a quiet cove on the South
Coast.
The Women’s Industrial Section at the
Easter Show was better housed than at any
previous exhibition in the Agricultural
Grounds. It is never ready on the opening
day. No one who has any experience of the
history of this female effort at the autumn
Hide and Hoofs display expects anything
but fancy-work chaos till the Show has
settled down to business. Well housed and
carefully arranged, the Women’s 1914 ex-
hibits had every chance of extracting the ad-
miration of the multitude who poured them-
selves in to hang over shiny glass cases.
But the generally uninteresting specimens
of feminine industry, and the dull plod along
the same old useless rut, only proved for the
millionth time that Woman is a crusted
conservative. Year after year she wears out
her eyesight and toils to turn out work that
nobody—outside a church bazaar—really
wants.
The wild and woolly gauds of a year or
two ago have been lopped out of this sea-
son’s milder nuisances. The old-time terrify-
ing adventures into “Art” are also missing,
though the small and feeble section entered
as “fashion plate drawing” deserves a brick
to itself. Paper flowers, my sisters, are not
buried (as one believed) in the hecatombs of
horsehair sofas and waxwork fruit. They
reared their mid-Victorian petals in the
Women’s Work pavilion at the Show. Only
one patchwork quilt—folded up to conceal its
inside —put in a meek appearance.
The crazy-work cushion is still
extant. It showed its fierce nobs to
the public gaze as another proof that
Woman considers life too short to 101 l
in comfort. The knitted or ' crochet
bedspread first prize went to Mrs. Susan
Palser, who certainly earned it by her ant-
like industry. Her exhibit vaguely suggested
to me the coral creature which spends thou-
sands of years and thousands of generations
in making an inch of coral bedspread at
the sea-bottom, and dies happy with its
fraction of the work accomplished, and is
drowned with its fathers.
Miss M. Davies snatched a reward
also for some eye-aching cross-stitch
fancy work. Mrs. G. W. Mitchell
was one of the most satisfactory ex-
hibitors. and a piece of Chinese appliquti
lace, which must have cost the worker much
patient graft, was a prize winner. Rug
wool mats were perpetrated by Miss God-
dard. Miss Ivy Mobbs got away with a first
for a valiant attack on fine lingerie stitchery
with a machine. Mrs. J. J. Malony used
her fingers for a similar garment, and
emerged with a first-prize placard.
The new gilt-edged ballroom in the ex-
Imperial Rink (William-street) is to open-
by invitation —on April 20, under the awe-
inspiring name, “Imperial Salon de Luxe.”
In spite of unreliable rumors to the con-
trary, Mrs. Cecil Youl says she is not pack-
ing up for the British rainfall —not just yet,
anyhow. Tassy’s Isle is her present destina-
tion. The brisk lady who has been a vital
spark at the Women’s Chatriotic Club, left
for Hobart on Saturday.
A meeting of the infant Australian Opera
League is going to cut its teeth on the pro-
ject of a women’s committee, on April 22.
Mrs. Mayoress Richards will watch over
the preliminary struggle in her official den on
that afternoon. The baby Opera League is
almost bursting its sides with rosy visions
of future greatness. It is likely to want
about £20,000 to make a decent start, I
imagine, if half the fearsome tales of for-
tunes lost in grand opera be true. In the
meantime it has £lOO or so to go on with. Pos-
sibly it feels like the humble toiler who has
already paid 2s. 6d. on the £6O piano which
his daughter, now aged two, is to play on
when she grows up.
Engineer Fred Gibbes has persuaded
pretty Marjory Henderson —the only
daughter of the Louis Hendersons, of Edge-
cliff-road —to say “yes.” The wedding will
take place in June, and the moon may be
spent at Kosciusko. The groom-to-be is a
son of the late squatter Gibbes, of Yarra-
lumla, in the Canberra area, where there are
rabbits in the water supply.
Sky-pilot G. McDonald, formerly attached
to the Commonwealth Forces, is now chap-
lain in charge of souls on 11.M.A.5. Tingira.
Artist Phillips Fox and his wife are going
to turn their palettes this way in June. In
that month of short days the artistic duet
will give another show here before going to
Tahiti. There they can paint nature adorned
by a hibiscus flower behind its ear.
Contralto Eva Champion, a one-time pet
lamb of the Girls’ Realm Guild, is appar-
ently going to settle down again in the tents
of Sydney. (Eva is Mrs. Wilson in non-
professional hours.) She ought to under-
stand the business she is getting into with
another vocal instructor — i.e., teaching
singing—for the Guild handed out a good
deal of cash to pay for her training in
London. There, the Girls’ Realm prot€g6e
had her vocal aspirations carefully tended
by a Greek gentleman with a name like an
insurrection of all the fishermen in Thessaly.
This is a busy week, and I haven’t time to
discover whether they do fish in Thessaly.
The Billyard-Leake pair, who used to be-
long to this slice of the map, have been head-
lined at various Eastpr frivols, with the
appendix (England) let in as a postscript.
I forget where the Leake comes from —per-
haps it sprung in the lady’s family—but he
belongs to a well-known clan named Billyard
which used to keep the hospitable latch on
the click at Elizabeth Farm on the Parra-
matta River. This roomy old place had
something of a history. Didn’t John Mac-
arthur walk over there one fine morning to
show a friend some of the first baa lambs in
the then baby colony?
Mrs. Douglas Mackenzie’s impressive pro-
file loomed through a white lace on the
opening day at Randwick. This veteran
society campaigner, who is just back from a
pleasant jaunt on the other side, is mother-
in-law to various eligibles, the most glaringly
rich being Russell Clarke, of the Victorian
millionaire family.
Doiipthea Spinney, an English reciter of
the Modern Emotional cult (this is in no
way related to the Higher Criticism), has
just blown into this port, after giving a few
samples of her prowess in Melbourne.
Archibald Strong the Uni. lecturer on litera-
ture over there, says that Dorothea’s acting
moved him, possibly more than anything of
the kind he has yet seen in Australia.
Strong, who is not given to hysterics, has a
Voice which counts when he hurls honeyed
praise at ladies who tackle Greek drama,
mercifully translated by Gilbert Murray.
Dorothea’s version of “The Trojan Women,”
without any assistance from the scene-
shifter, is said to take warm flesh on its
classic bones.
What is now called in Paris the “one
blotch” woman is quite likely to become a
habit here this winter. A cherry-colored lady
with hair to match her dress, hat, shoes and
hose, is, for instance, “one blotch.” (I sup-
pose this is Futurist language, and ought to
be framed.) She’s going to liven up the
landscape during the short days that fall
into the night before the homing trains
leave the city.
Miss Bud Neill, a daughter of the late
banker William Neill, of this town, went off
to the land of the early rising Jap, the other
day.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 13, 1914.
My Dear Tarrie, —
The ease with which the Denman Loan
Exhibition stuff was gathered shows what
a fine hunting-ground is waiting for the
skilled collector. Perfect treasures were
carted up to Government House from all
sorts of odd corners, and offered to the
secretary apologetically. “It doesn’t seem
to be much good, you know, but I know it’s
old.” Sbme was rubbish, of course, but the
bulk of it was very valuable. Where there
are half a dozen who would cling to their
goods, half a dozen would willingly sell or
even give away. The Exhibition may have
been an awful bother to arrange, but it
brought to light things calculated to make
the heart of a collector jump. Some were
dragged out, covered with grime and candle
grease and ink stains. If the Denman lady
does not carry away to England a fine addi-
tion to her collections, I’ll eat my hat.
(Not my best one with the feather.) ' After
the show opened heaps of articles were
offered. But they are too late to be in-
cluded.
Lady Denman’s Loan Exhibition has been
a draw. The hundreds of people who skir-
mish hopelessly on the outer edge of the
social flock gladly paid their shilling to get
inside the doors and rustle round in full
garden-party rig. The grounds (suggests
the dregs, doesn’t it?) were open to the
aspiring bobites, also the terrace outside the
ballroom and the dustbin at the back door.
A temporary green fence was erected to
block strays from getting down the terraced
steps or climbing through the drawing-room
windows.
A blind woman had a special “view” of
the show, and, of course, was allowed to
ignore the “Must Not Touch” placards.
She got hold of a precious Dutch doll be-
longing to Lady Denman and discovered
small details about its exquisite old dress
with her far-sighted fingers that the owner
had never noticed. The crowd swarmed
all day and every day, and sight-seeing was
always difficult. Tea was served on the
terrace—at a price—and some garden seats
were available. In the show room the
crowd moved round uneasily, in a slow
circle. The only chairs in sight besides
the. precious antiques were the royal-blue
satin, gold-legged things on the dais. How-
ever, these last were tantalisingly corded
up in case anyone might dare to roost in
the sacred place.
Comedienne Daisy Jerome sold pro-
grammes in a business-like way, and smil-
ingly took orders for tea-tickets.
State Governor Stanley added to his list
of vice-regal achievements by opening the
Druids’ Gala and Austin Hospital Bazaar
on Saturday afternoon. The job took
35min. by his Excellency’s repeater, and
then there was the official pilgrimage and
sundry side shows to visit. A dozen gold
and black Druids made escape impossible.
Lady Stanley, in a pale fawn dress with
a low collar ruffle, looked peaky and pale;
perhaps she was worn out with the mad
rush of Premiers’ parties. Mrs. Hennessy,
wearing a blue collar to her dress that
might have been a Druid charm, sat along-
side ; and Mrs. Brenan, garbed in purple,
was also of the circle. When his Ex. had
shouted himself hoarse (he had to shout to
make himself heard), the Chief Something-
or-Other had his say—and it was a long
one.
As an antidote, the Opening Party were
given free seats at an elaborate side show.
After this reprieve the party started on
the futile round of inspection.
A wary crew of stall managers, made wise
by long experience, put little out on its
stalls except hardy kitchenware or dinky
little silk and woollen goods that could make
a respectable re-appearance at any old
bazaar of the future. A Shed de Danse
has been fixed up in one of the long corri-
dors, and the floor has been waxed.
Captain Hughes-Onslow, the imported
naval officer who came to loggerheads with
Naval Board officialdom, did not steam
back to England by the Orama, as he
had originally intended. He means to linger
and see Maoriland before he goes home. The
cheery little man has become a well-known
figure at Menzies’, where he has made a lot
of friends —principally by arguing with
them. It is an effective way of getting to
know people.
Marjorie Raphael is turning her wit
to art and literature these times. Recently
she hostessed a big Sunday lunch gathering
of pen-and-ink personages in the London
world, to celebrate her hubby’s first appear-
ance as a publisher. Norman Raphael, it
seems, has taken up publishing as a hobby—
“not for coin but for the glory of English
literature” —and his young wife is keenly in
sympathy with the enterprise. But his time
is not wholly devoted to the cares of business.
He and Marjorie were about to motor to
Monte Carlo at the invitation of Raphael
pere when last she wrote to her Melbourne
friends.
Dr. Fred Bird’s daughter, Dorrie, entered
into the usual partnership on April 6, Harold
Brookes being the other party. And as
Harold is one of the Brookwood Brookes,
and Dorrie one of the vanguard of South
Yarra, the affair was within an ace of being
arrested for obstructing the traffic in Toorak
and St. Kilda roads. Then, you see, a Fair-
bairn, a Grey-Smith and a Hamilton fluffed
along in cream satin and white fur as brides-
maids, and each brought a different crowd of
friends to watch the show. The presents!
Well really, you know, what with Easter
bazaars and Easter weddings, the Best
People are on the edge of ruin, and have
taken to turning last year’s feather. They
are even wearing their boots a disgraceful
number of times.
Engagements will never cease. The
Gerald Bucklands’ only daughter, Helen, has
caught the craze now. Leslie Rigney, of
Avoca, Tasmania, is the fortunate man. The
girl will have money to roll in and throw
into the air with a shovel, and the wedding
extravagances will be on a suitably arrogant
scale. Before Leslie is a completely mar-
ried man he will think he has joined a cir-
cus.
The widow Galbraith has returned to Mel-
bourne, tired of life in highly-colored Colom-
bo. Perhaps she is also tired of playing a
mother-in-law role in the house of planter
Maddox. Daughter Rhyll Maddox (who got
into her wedding dress last year straight out
of her flapper frocks) is along with mother
for a brief spell of six weeks or so.
Female friends are buzzing and gyrating
round the lately returned Mrs. Donald
McKinnon. They are just crazy to know
how she managed to reduce her figure; but
the lady, who used to be excessively plump,
preserves an egg-like silence and smiles a
Mona Lisa smile as she arranges the new
filmy gowns over her now willowy form. Her
fattest friends burst into tears as they leave
her and call down fat upon her. But the “re-
duced” lady sits unmoved and keeps on with
the Mona Lisa smile.
Leila Cowan, of the Gilmour Cowans, who
squat near Hamilton, is to marry Rudolph
Uren, of the Ballarat family. The Urens
have been associated so long with Ballarat
that some people assert they got there before
the blacks.
Aviator Hawker’s sister is back in these
parts from her honeymooning. The dimpled
young person is now Mrs. Edward (or
Teddy) Smith, and is showing some pretty
trousseau frocks to women callers.
Women from the Western District who
visit Warrnambool every year for the May
horse races have often complained that
their evenings are hard to kill. The men go
off to the local Club and entertain one
another with bottles, while the women have
to sit in the hotel and listen to the potatoes
growing. Sometimes a game of bridge helps
the limping night off the premises, but there
is no certainty about it. This year the old-
time race dance is to be resuscitated on a
liberal scale, with a turkey and champagne
supper to cheer the revellers who don’t
tango.
The few select females who have met
Adela Pankhurst (is her name Adela or
Adelaide?) complain that she is “so quiet!”
Apparently they expected the daughter of
England’s chief she-martyr to give exhibi-
tions of hammer-throwing and be led round
on a chain. Vida Goldstein, her hostess, is
keeping the overseas guest snug and close.
“Perhaps she is getting ready to jar the
new Governor-General,” suggested one
frightened young matron, adding confidenti-
ally, “Vida Goldstein doesn’t approve of
him, you know.”
Ada Reeve held a reception soon after her
arrival last week. The red carpet of cere-
mony poured out of the Tivoli to the kerb in
the most approved manner, while representa-
tives of the rich unemployed, laden with
feathers and flowers crowded into the entry
to wait for H.R.H. (Her Royal Highkicker).
The loungers who own that corner of Bourke-
street expressed their opinion of the invasion
in neat explosive phrases. Ada eventually
arrived wearing grey velvet, furs and a
dazzling smile. Then the reception commit-
tee got them to their boxes while Ada made
a theatrical entry to a red plush draped
stage, and friends were invited to come up
out of the audience and shake hands. It was
diverting to watch the large fat backs of the
Best Fed mounting the Tango-girls’ steps.
Most of the Best have not practised a
stage parade, and they scuttled like sheep in
their efforts to descend again. A tally-man
stood at one corner to prevent them dashing
behind the scenes. The Gaudy Mayoress,
wearing a vestment of black and red brocaded
velvet, emerged from her box and gave Ada
some flowers. Theatrical identities climbed
up and held up the procession while they
made long speeches. One man with the iron-
grey side hair of the melodrama hero got a
double handshake. “She knows ’im!” bel-
lowed an interested onlooker somewhere
near the roof. Lesly Madden pranced
along skittishly, surrounded by a pink
sash and followed by her second, Mrs.
Davidson. That promising boy, R. S.
Smythe, sat in the orchestra stalls and
beamed. Essie Perrin-Kcrouse was among
those present, also the Godfreys, just back
from Fogland. Elsewhere Mrs. Hogan, wife
of one of our naval officers, exhibited her
fine Tasmanian complexion above a grey
cloth dress, and Mrs. Major James was neatly
boxed in some clothes which I have forgotten.
Whatever they were they were adequate.
Subsequently Ada made a speech about some-
thing or other; then she took the managerial
pen with husband Colton, and, much to my
astonishment, the house settled down to en-
joy a vaudeville programme. I had prepared
myself for a prayer-meeting and sermon.
Harry Lauder settled himself in a chair
and said “Umph-umph!” Therefore you
knew he was either Scotch or a camel.
He was wearing a short kilt over very fat,
short legs. The kilt pattern showed only
one tartan, but he told that he has three
—the Macleod, the McLennan and the
Urquhart. (When he says Urquhart he is
so deeply Scotch that you have to guess.
Perhaps after all he said McFlanagan.)
He was also wearing tango shoes of the
newest fashion—the sort that have four
peaks on either side for the lace holes. He
explained that they are the real Highland
footwear and not a copy. Mrs. Lauder is
a dimpled little body named Nancy, who is
just an admiring echo of Lauder. In 10
years or so she has not had time to get
used to the terrific height of her husband’s
popularity and the noise of his press-agents.
About that time ago, Lauder was singing
two sentimental Scotch songs a night at a
London vaudeville show. Then he added a
comic song, and the management soon had
to cut out the sentimental items. Mrs.
Lauder found American manners bewilder-
ing. She said: “You know in London Harry
had an audience with the King” (here a
pretty little bridling of pride), “and when
he appeared first in New York the Ameri-
cans yelled out, ‘Hi, Harry! What sort
of a guy is King George?’ It was no’ a
prupper way to speak of a king!” Then
Harry said “Umph-umph!” again, like
another camel. They suggest a pair of
Barrie characters —very domesticated and
deliberate in all their doings. They would
prefer “lodgin’s,” snug and quiet, instead
of the hotels, if it were not for the adver-r-r-
-tisement. He loves soda scones, and when-
ever his wife gets a chance on tour she
bakes a batch. Then they find a quiet
corner for a happy supper time. The star
adds to his stolid, deliberate look off duty
by wearing glasses. He doesn’t smile (he
has enough of that in “business,” ye ken),
but sucks thoughtfully at his pipe and occa-
sionally makes a dry speech. But mostly
he says “Umph-umph !”
The attraction of weekly Pops for
musical people is in the possibility of a
new voice being Discovered. Pops, such as
the Taits started at Melbourne Auditorium
last winter and are repeating this season,
give the undiscovered voice a chance. The
concerts, apart from one or two well-known
names that guarantee fair value in the pro-
gramme, are rather in the nature of a com-
petition. Any unknown aspirant able to
sing to a certain standard can hope for a
hearing sooner or later. Then it is up to
the aspirant to make good. The other
Saturday an unpretentiously pleasing little
lady—Miss Rosa Alba —made very good at
the Auditorium. An amateurish tenor, Percy
Blundell, also showed promise of doing well
enough on the concert platform; but the
quality of the lady’s voice in trillsome, birdy
ballads was a sweet surprise. Rosa Alba’s
light soprano warble will be talked about on
its present merits, and is certainly
capable of being trained into one of the best.
She sings with a lisp, as naturally as a bird.
The notes are fully musical. The songs she
tried at the first concert were no test of her
temperament—and possibly she has no emo-
tional force —but she will get further oppor-
tunities to prove herself. The new find was
promptly re-engaged and featured in the
next advt.
Professor Orme Masson is to open an
Antarctic picture show at the Guild Hall
after Easter. The show will consist of
photographs of the Scott Expedition, 1910-
1913, taken by scientist Herbert Ponting.
The special private view will happen at
three o’clock on Thursday, April 16.
The Mawson dogs, which panted for a time
at the Delprat house in South Yarra, have
gone to Kosciusko. Personally, I think it
is a pity they did not go to Buffalo —it is so
much easier to spell.
Melbourne Shakspeare Society, which is
bounded on the north by Archie Strong,
M.A., and on the east by Frank Russell,
LL.B., is issuing tickets for a “Birthday
Celebration in aid of the Tercentenary Memo-
rial Fund.” The Tercentenary Fund is un-
derstood, of course, to be a matter con-
nected with the Bard, but tickets don’t state
whose birthday it will be on Wednesday,
April 22. The doubt is divided among the
Society, Strong, M.A., The Immortal Bard
and Russell, LL.B. One or other will have a
dramatic and musical celebration on the
date aforementioned at the Masonic Hall.
Horace Gleeson has put some of E. J.
Brady’s brilliant verses to music. The title
is “Brown Eyes,” but several other varieties
of eye get honorable mention. Brady says
he has a bundle of song-words in his pocket
waiting to be winged with music.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Harry Duttons suddenly left for London last
week. They took with them naught but two small
sons, a brace of maids, a mass of luggage and some
money. Mr. F. A. Wyatt caught the same boat—a cir-
cumstance which caused much gnashing of teeth, imi-
tation teeth, and even gums. He is one of our
few eligible bachelors.
Oak Bank is crammed, not to say packed. Every
second brick one throws hits a polo-player—which is
one of the many reasons why I have dropped brick-
throwing for the present. Nor is the multitude
confined to mere merrymakers. On Saturday arrove
or transpired that distinguished builder of foreign
railways, Sir William Johns. He is visiting his
sister, Mrs. Pooler, and his niece, Mrs. Cuthbert
Wells. The roads in this neighborhood are dreadful
just now—but then they always are.
Miss Ermyntrude Senden returned from her violin
J® ss ° n , s in Germany °, n Saturday. However, the boat
t“ at brought her took back to Sydney Miss Dorodiy
Luscombe, who has been staying with the Harry
Duttons. So we don’t know whether to laud or
vituperate the wretched vessel.

Talking of trouble, invitations for the McEwan-
Gardiner, Moncrieff-Wakelin, Marshall-Hack and
Bakewell-Henderson knot-tyings are all out.
Mr Franeis Belt) a o ne-time Adelaidian, is on a
visit here. He is a chronic traveller and big-game
shooter. The close season for big game being on
in S.A. just now, he is staying quietly with Dr. and
Mra - O’Leary, at Mount Lofty.
There 18 a military encampment at Gawler, and the
resultant excitement and gratification in and around
that hamlet is something terrific. If a Quaker dared
to mar the militarist harmony by his Peace Society
croakings, I verily believe he would be stoned, or, at
anr rate > severely chidden,
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“.Esculapius”: In ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. We moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water —only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to the
Earl of Hopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
1 igaro . Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
hut close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of ’’Helidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, ’’Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom is always to
SPLIT IT WITH
HELIDON.
ABORIGIN-
