“Pick”: “Leo Lear” (B. 19/3/’l4) has
misgivings regarding the wall-of-water yarns that
we read about. Well, I have seen it—not water,
perhaps, but a wall of mud and debris pushed for-
ward by the flooded MacLaughlan River (N.S.W.)
across the Bibbenluke Plains. When the heaving,
rolling mass soughed by my camp at Shanty Hill it
was a quarter of a mile wide, which was all the
width the adjacent hills allowed. I learned later
that a heavy thunderstorm had flooded the country at
the river’s source, early the day before. Likewise
during 18 months of sparring for a crust at Bayliss
Glen, on the Snowy, I have often, after an un-
usually hot day, seen the water wall created by
the melting snows on the peaks beyond. The fos-
sickers of that rugged valley know when to expect
the coming of the wall, and generally heave their
paraphernalia and themselves above high sludge mark
in good time.
John P.”: In the Marlborough (M.L.)
Sounds the universal means of communication is the
motor-boat. In the Pelorus and Queen Charlotte
Sounds alone there are over 300 of these modern
craft. Every farmer has one. Practically every farm
there has water frontage, and the homesteads are all
close to the shore; so, with a telephone and a motor-
boat, the once isolated cocky is in close touch with
civilisation. The motor-launch takes the kids to
school, the milk to the factory, the boys to the
darnce, the family to church and dad to the pub;
and it brings back the mail, the groceries, and some-
times the bailiff and the intoxicated parent. This
means of getting from place to place is always ready,
is quick, and, now that Taranaki benzine is coming
on the market, will be cheap. The very latest devef-
opment is a big, Socialistic sort of launch, to be run
on co-operative lines by the farmers, that will scoot
round to all the farms, morning and evening, to col-
lect the milk and take it to the big dairy factory at
Picton.
' * * *
“Leo Lear”: “Marloo’s” farmers (B.
12/3/’l4) can’t have had much experience with burn-
ing swamps, or they would be able to block the
outbreak at the beginning. In S.W. Victoria peaty
swamps of this kind are common, and if the con-
flagration is not checked when the dry decayed
vegetation gets on fire the whole floor would be
burnt out of the swamp, and the depression left an
ash-hole. The receipt for fire-fighting in this class of
land is draining. Dig a deep ditch round the
smouldering part and isolate it. This can easily be
done, as most soils of this nature are more peaty
on top than anywhere else, and a couple of feet
down run into heavy black ground or sand.
BILLYGOAT FLAT.-THE MAIN
STREET. (As a stranger sees it.)
Billy T.’ : Re the local leaning habit, so
much stressed by George Reid and other strangers
within our gates. I remember, as a youth, an old
pub on the road from Ballarat to Haddon in front
of which stood a well-polished tree-trunk on which
was nailed the notice: “Lean Here!” There is a
somewhat similar notice on a mountain track near
Bright. It is on a post on the edge of the road,
where the push of a foot might hurl it into the tree
tops 100 ft. below. On it is inscribed, “Do not lean
on this Post, it ain’t safe.”
HUMILITY
CUTHBERT: “Madam, ye fes' asked me to split that wood."
SERVANT: “Yes"
CUTHBERT : “Wouldn't y' like me to carry it into Ike house and make a fire fur ye?"
SERVANT : “If you wish to, my good man."
CUTHBERT: "Well, I don 't wish to. I was merely showin' ye how, after having some-
body smile me on one cheek , I could turn the other. Good day!"
“Ginger”: Down in the Lower Murray
district a pair of German girls (sisters) earn the
elusive pin-money and square their dressmakers’
bills by swinging “douglas” for the Mildura firewood
supply. They have put up the respectable tally of
from a cord to a cord and a half (six-foot) a day.
The spectacle of two girleens whirling “Kelly” under
a blinding shower of chips and big father slogging
away placidly a good ton behind is something in-
spiring in these days of tango tarts.
* * * *
From “Bad Australian” (whose engineer-
ing job keeps him wandering over the Andes, S.
America) : I don’t quite know whether
the Salvation Army should be kicked
hard or handed a laurel wreath for
its latest advertising dodge. A few
days ago I was navigating across the
Andes by the Uspallata Pass, and
when I reached the summit, at
13,000 ft., I became aware of a curi-
ous, bony, white-whiskered old frowser
singing, in a shrill “come-all-ye”
kind of voice, “Are you washed in
the blood of the Ker-rucified One?”
as he painted his Army advt. on the
shelter hut which tops the pass. He
was busy oh a statement, in English,
to the effect that “The blood of the
Lamb cleanseth from all sin,” when I
hailed him as we hail men of our
own tongue in a far country. He
took no notice, but got busy on his
statement in German; and as my
mule slid down out of Argentina into
Chili, he had already started to
affirm the same in French. On my
way down the mountains, with my
half-breed arrieros, I found his re-
mark repeated at intervals in Spanish,
Italian and almost every civilised
lingo. This poor old disciple is wan-
dering over South America skirmish-
ing for the Army, which, as usual,
gets a fairly effective advt. for darned
few pesos. The tough old patriarch
hopes to work up the West Coast,
amble through Bolivia, cast his bread
upon the waters of Titicaca (which
by die way, is a much over-rated pond), paste his
little offering on Illamani and the remains of the
Temple of the Sun at Cuzco, and deliver the goods
at Panama about the time the Great White Fleet
gets through. After that, if he has time, he will
blazon it on Popocatepetl, to the astonishment of
General Huerta’s descendant of the 19th generation.
* * * *
An amazing number of relies have been
disinterred in Maoriland at different times. The
latest, dug up at Akaroa, is a helmet, which some
kind of an expert declares to be French and to date
back to the time of Louis X.—Louis the Quarrel-
some. Seeing that that angry crowned cove only
reigned for a pair of years, and those years were
very long ago (1314-1316), the identification niav
be rather a matter of guesswork. A real expert may
tell from a dog’s noise whether the animal comes
from a family which barked in Scotland in the
days of King Kenneth McAlpin, or in Persia under
Darius Tlystaspes, but the public would like some
evidence. Anyhow the thing has been discovered.
It is somewhat in the shape of a low-crowned, mush-
room hat, and is composed of a single piece of raw
hide, evidently moulded on a wooden block while
green. How the hide lasted since the days of Louis
the Cantankerous no one knows. Over the top of
the crown a finely woven canvas is stretched. The
canvas ought to be dead by now. This is covered
by a heavy coat of black lacquer, which probablv
wasn’t known in France at that time. There is very
little decoration, but on the crown an emblem (a
rose and leaves) is worked in gold lacquer. The
brim also shows signs of something or other. The
expert to whom it was submitted found illustrations
in a French history corresponding with the helmet
in every detail, but says he has never seen one in
any museum and does not think another exists.
Consequently he reckons its value should be con-
siderable. The ancient hat was evidently brought to
Akaroa by one of the early French settlers—pro-
bably a man named Smith, from England. Some
people say it is a billy-cock which had dropped into
varnish.
* *
So the poor Papuan is of some use to liis
white planter neighbor after all. Somebody with
patience and consideration has imparted to him the
rotten secret of how to work for his living, and he
is doing it. Consequently, the clamor for Javanese
and other colored labor with rubber-tapping experi-
ence has faded away like a receding dog-fight, and
that involved nightmare, a wholesale importation of
strange, far-travelled niggers, has been averted. It
js remarkable how some people clamor for remote
niggers in any national emergency. Even when there are
herds of raw, docile aborigines at hand, just waiting
to be imposed on, the staunch believer can’t wait to
teach them the job, but bellows to be allowed to
bring a mob of strange savages from somewhere else
and pour them into the plantations, regardless of how
they may complicate the politics and morals of the
rightful naked heathen who. are already there. In
this case thousands of pounds might have been
wasted in a great shuffle of our murky brother, if
some overseers and managers hadn’t been possessed
of the blessed virtue of patience. The Papuan has
apparently given the lie to his numerous traducers
who said he couldn’t work. The madness was just
a bit strange to him, that’s all. And if you dragged
any six company directors out of their Sydney
offices and told them they could boil the billy as
soon as they succeeded in making fire bv rubbing
two sticks together, it would probably be months
before they ground out a spark. The silly secret of
toil is just as hard to acquire as the aboriginal art of
making fire with sticks.

THE HYMN OF FUTILITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
• / ' lc s ®Wlement of the Territory is Australia’s own
job, and Australia’s own salvation depends on the
efficiency and expedition with which it is done.
—The Bulletin (12/3/’l4).
Lord, Thou hast given unto us a land.
r In Thy beneficence Thou hast ordained
'lhat we should hold a country great and grand,
Such as no race of old has ever gained.
A favored people, basking in Thy smile:
So dost Thou leave us to work out our fate
But, Lord, be patient yet a little while.
The shade is pleasing and our task is great.
Lo. Thou hast said: “This land I give to you
r lo be the cradle of a mighty race,
A\ ho shall take up the White Man’s task anew,
And all the nations of the world outpace.
No heritage for cowards or for slaves,
Here is a mission for the brave, the strong.
Then see ye to it, lest dishonored graves
Bear witness that ye tarried over long.’’
Lo, Thou hast said: “When ye have toiled and tilled,
t\ hen ye have born the heat, and wisely sown,
And ev’ry corner of the vineyard filled
’ With goodly growth, the land shall be your own.
then shall your sons and your sons’ sons rejoice.
Then shall the race speak with a conqueror’s
mouth ;
And all the world shall hearken to its voice,
And heed the great White Nation of the South.”
And Thou hast said: “This, striving, shall ye do I
Be diligent to tend and guard the soil.
If this great heritage I trust to you
r Be worth the purchase of a meed of toil,
then shall ye not, at call of game or mart,
Forego the labor of a single day.
lliey spurn the gift who treasure but a part.
Guard ye the whole, lest all be cast away I
“Say, is My bounty worth the winning?” (Lord,
(< Thou spoken. Humbly have we heard.)
“No son of man is born who can afford
Lo pay Me tribute with an empty word.
Guard ye the treasure if the gift be meet.
Win ye to strength and wisdom while ye may.
For he who fears the burden and the heat
Shall gain the wages of a squandered day!”
Lord, we have heard.... Loud our Hosannas rang!
Voices of glad thanksgiving did we lift.
I l lom out the fullness of our hearts we sang
Sweet hymns of praise for this Thy gracious gift.
Here, in one corner of the land, we found
A goodly garden, where abundant food
We won, with scanty labor, from the ground.
Heie did we rest. And, Lord, we found it good 1
Great cities have we builded here, O Lord ;
And corn and kine full plenty for our need
We have ; and doth the wondrous land afford
Lieasuie beyond the wildest dreams of greed.
Even this tiny portion of Thy gift,
One corner of our mighty continent,
L’oth please us well. A voice in pray'r we lift: -
"Lord, give its peace! For we are well content .
“Lord, give us peace; for Thou has sent a sign:
Smoke of a raider’s ships athwart the skv !
Nay, suffer us to hold this gift of Thine!
The burden ! Lord ! The burden—-by-and-bve I
The sun is hot, Lord, and the way is long I
’Tis pleasant in this corner Thou hast blest.
Leave us to tarry here with wine and song.
Our little corner, Lord! Guard Thou the "rest!
“Why must we toil ? Here is enough for all!
To-morrow shall our sons take up the task.
But, lest the cities of our people fall,
Guard Thou our continent! ’Tis all we ask.
To-morrow shall our sons go bravely forth,
With banners, and in goodly armor decked.
To-morrow shall they journey to the North,
And do them penance for their sire’s neglect.
“But yesterday our fathers hither came,
ltovers and strangers on a foreign strand.
Must we, for their neglect, bear all the blame?
Nay, Master, we have come to love our land!
But see, the task Thou givest us is great!
The load is heavy and the way is long!
Hold Thou our enemy without the gate!
When we have rested then shall we be strong.
“Lord, Thou hast spoken And, with hands to
ears,
We would shut out the thunder of Thy voice,
That in the night-watch wakes our sudden fears
‘The day is here, and yours must be the choice!
Shall ye be slaves and shun the task of men ?
Shall ye be weak who may be brave and strong?’
We wave our banners boastfully, and then,
Weakly we answer, ‘Lord, the way is long!’
1 ime tarries not, but here ye tarry yet,
Llie futile masters of a continent,
Guard ye the gift I gave? Do ye forget?”
_ And st iH we answer, “Lord, we are content.
Fat have we grown upon this goodly soil.
A little while be patient, Lord, and wait.
To-morrow and to-morrow shall we toil.
The shade is pleasing, Lord! Our task is great!”
But ever through the clamor of the mart,
(< An<l ever 011 the playground through the cheers:
“He spurns the gift who guardeth but a part!”
So doth the warning fall on heedless ears.
“GuarjJ ye the treasure if the gift be meet!”
(Loudly we howl the price, we cheer the play.)
“For he who shirks the burden and the heat
Shall glean the harvest of a squandered day!”
VICTORIA.

"Hamer” : The truth escapes occasionally.
It bursts forth and will prevail. The other morning
the S. M. Herald had occasion to take up some
space concerning an outpouring of spiritual blessings,
under the auspices of the Church Mission Society,
upon Binghi up by the Roper River, and the head-
lines to the article were:—
VANISHING BLACKS.
A MISSION ENTERPRISE.
Thus did a great fact cease to refrain from being
obvious.
* * * *
"Jimmie Pannikin”: In reply to “II.O.”
let me say that in my diverse and devious divaga-
tions I have come across many strangely named pubs;
but the eurious cognomens were not on the sign-
boards, nor over the door; they were in the un-
written language of the saltbush sages and the
western wags and wits. These ingenious and inter-
esting bloques did not speak of pubs; they called
all the Outback inns “rubbies," and christened
them with nicknames that often bore an unholy
approximation to the Terrible Truth, such as “The
Blazing Stump,’’ “The Rag and Louse,” “The Bat-
tered Pelican,” “The Dewdrop Inn,” “The Reckless
Rabbit,’ “The Hooshta Halt,” “The Gates of Para-
dise,” “The Hobs-o’-Hell,” “The Carriers’ Curse,”
“The Drop-o’-Good-Stuff.” Some of the other pic-
turesque designations I’ll send along when The
Bulletin is printed on asbestos.
“Two Seas”; This is a dry fact, showing
how a misguided mare added the last straw to a
load of hard luck. There had been no rain for five
months of heat-waves, varied by dry westerly gales.
There was no grass, and there were no crops; no
water remained for the stock, except in one muddy
hole. One cherished tank of rain water was being
saved for household use. The family had just
debated the subject of having a long-deferred
washing day. One small cloud upon the horizon
had raised a hope. When that vanished it was
decided to postpone washing “until to-morrow.”
The same evening a plough-mare broke into the
house-paddock and nosed around the tap of the
tank containing our last drink. Suddenly she put
her ears back, and, turning around, drove her hoof
through the 24-gauge iron as though it were
paper. She got her drink, but it cost 600 gallons
of carefully saved water to provide it, and washing
day is now postponed indefinitely.
* * * *
“Leo Lear” : Can any Abo-scribe assist me
to track the first, earliest, or original cove who set
out in black and white that Australian birds were a
harsh, mournful, songless lot? Poet Campbell, who
died in 1844, wrote in “Lines on Departure of
Emigrants to New South Wales,” in the ’3o’s, I
think
Oft when the tuneless birds scream round forlorn
Regret the lark that gladdens England’s morn.
But he is not the man I’m after, as in a footnote to
these lines an extract is given from one P. Cunning-
ham, author of “Two Years in New South Wales.”
I can’t find the date of his book, but the extract
referred to reads: “We are not moved here with the
deep, mellow notes of the blackbird.... or thrilled
with the warblings of the thrush. .. .none of our birds
rivalling those divine songsters in realising the
poetical idea of ‘the music of the grove’ ; while
parrots chattering must supply the place of night-
ingales singing,” etc. Campbell also had peculiar
ideas about our animals, as in the same “Lines” he
says:—
What spacious cities with their spires shall gleam,
Where now the panther laps a lonely stream.
That panther must have been the first edition of the
dreaded Socialistic tiger.
TO THOSE WHO
SHADOW-SPAR.
“Imagine yourself up
against your worst enemy.”
‘ Leo Lear”: The small black ant men-
tioned by “Doubter” (B. 5/3/T4), that resides in
dry river beds, is one of the puzzles the court of
Abojudicators might solve. I’ve seen these insects
in thousands along the Burnett (Q.). Their houses,
churches and pubs are thick on the sand bank in
the river bed. Just before a flood the work of pro-
duction, distribution and exchange is going on as it
has apparently done since Noah’s boss overflow. Then
the waters come down and wash, roar and swirl over
the rocks and sand for days and weeks. When the
normal returns, and the sand-banks are approachable
with dry feet, the ants are there holding salvage
sales and collecting insurance policies. They may
be 200 yards from the banks, and couldn’t have
trekked and returned before and after the inunda-
tion. Maybe they live in water-tight compartments.
AFTER THE RIOT.
INQUISITIVE OLD PARTY: “Were you one of the men who was injured in the milte?"
BATTERED OBJECT: “No. I’m the bloke that was kicked three times in the public-housed"
“Gouger”: It is some years ago since I
first met “Murder.” None of us dared call him that
to his face, though usually in a gang of N.S.W.
railway navvies there are men who will dare Hell’s
own ganger himself. “Murder” stood six-feet-four,
possessed a gorilla’s chest and strength, and was
Irish-Amcrican by accident. It wasn’t his strength
we were frightened of; it was his face, or what
passed for a face. One day the ganger sent “Mur-
der” back along the line nearly a mile for a couple
of mislaid crowbars. “Murder” came back near
knock-off time, and cursed the ganger. He said the
bars were not there. The boss apologised, and that
is the only time I have known a railway ganger to
be polite. When at last they sacked “Murder” the
man who made the necessary remarks was sup-
ported by a dozen navvies weighted with crowbars,
and they passed “Murder’s” time to him on a long-
handled shovel. I heard of “Murder’s” end quite
recently, away out in one of the mountain tinfields
of North Queensland. An old fossicker, no doubt
through fright, took “Murder” as mate into the
claim which had been his living for years. All went well
for a few weeks, until they struck a very rich patch
of tin. Then one day “Murder” turned on the old
man and said “Get!” emphasising his meaning by
seizing his benefactor’s throat and hurling him
stunned into the creek. Now, the entrance into the
tunnel was underneath a huge cairn of rocks, piled
one upon another in hopeless confusion, and so
placed by Nature that each rock remained in its
position supported solely by one little hundred-
weight stone. One night this stone was bored, a
strong gelignite charge with a small fuse put in,
and the hole plugged up again. Next day, about
dinner-time, while “Murder” was working at the far
end of the tunnel, there was an explosion. It must
have been a fortnight before it was noticed that the
big rock-heap before “Murder’s” tunnel had caved
in and completely blocked the entrance. “Murder”
was hardly worth bothering about then; in fact, he
was hardly worth making a fuss about at any time.
* * * *
“Strathloddon”: Re the value of tree plan-
tations, made with either prison or free labor. Back
in the bad days of the ’9o’s in Victoria, a small
forest of pinus insignis was laid out at Creswick,
mostly, perhaps, to give relief work to the electors of
that constituency. When they were 20 years old the
pines were thinned out, the contractors giving £BO
per acre for the right to cut about half the trees.
This pine was grown on poor, hilly land, almost
useless for any other purpose. I have seen Lombard
poplars, 80ft. in height and 4ft. in diameter, grown
on sluiced-out 'diggings in 24 years, and a royalty
of £1 per tree offered and refused. Planted properly,
an acre should grow 400 similar trees. This poplar
cuts up into a beautiful white wood, without taste
or smell, equal if not superior to Maoriland white
pine—for which timber I have known it sold even
to experienced men.
“Albion” : I had been doing a bit of milk-
ing on a cow farm at Piper’s Creek, and one
morning was rambling over the verdant pastures (as
the poet puts it) with a warrant for the arrest
of a white heifer that invariably dodged us at milk-
ing-time. I came across a swarm of bees in a
swamp-oak, and immediately reported the matter.
My mate was not a bit enthusiastic, but remarked
that Tom Day was sure to know something about
the bagging of bees, as Tom knew a bit about
everything. Tom, by the way, was chief-in-command
of the agricultural part of the entertainment. But
he never did much—it was always either too wet
or too dry to plough. I meekly approached Tom
in regard to the bees. Oh, yes 1 He’d caught
thousands of swarms. He sent me to the hut for a
kerosene box and a cornbag. It was quite simple.
He would knock them off their perch into the box,
and we had to put the bag over them. Thomas,
who was fully 6ft. 6in. in height, wore a paspalum
beard, a slouch hat, a blue singlet and no boots, and
his dungaree pants were tucked up almost to his
knees. He manoeuvred round the oak. In the
meantime, my mate and I were handy—lsoyds.
or so away. Tom eventually gave the swarm a prod
with a bush, and then bolted into the scrub.
While he was scaling logs, jumping fences and
dashing through the scrub, frantic yells of “Put
the bag over them!” “Put the bag over them!”
could be distinctly heard for miles around. Fully
three bees fell into the box. In about an hour
Tom crawled back, looking like a jellyfish after a
mermaid’s dance. He reckoned he was stung in
10,000 places, and I believed him. He still main-
tains that it was all our fault for not putting the
bag over them.
♦ * * *
“Main Bearing”: Concerning this diving
argument: About two years ago, while discharging
cargo at Apia, a case of corrugated roofing iron fell
out of a sling into the water between the ship and
a lighter. A big half-caste Samoan, mate on one
of the small coasting steamers down there, dived to
locate it, and found it had taken a long skid away
from where it dropped. He next took down a chain
sling, and owing to the bottom being comparatively
level, he had a hard job to get it slung. Then he
went down with the cargo hook, hooked on, and the
case was hove up. I sounded the depth, person-
ally, and found it to be 37ft. Some time after-
wards, whilst doing some diving down the coast,
something busted internally, and he passed in his
gun. Also, about eight months ago, in letting go
our anchor, the pin came out of the 15-fathom
shackle. A Tongan dived and made a rope fast to
the loose end of the cable. This happened in 48ft.
I have seen a lot of diving done by natives in the
South Seas, but those two instances stand out as
the best.
“Lagani-Namo”: In the Banks group the
natives have it that there was a time when they were
unacquainted with death. As they aged and their
skins wore out all that they had to do was to shed
them, snake fashion, and the proprietors were im-
mediately rejuvenated. But one day, a mother
having made herself young, was unable to
convince her child of her identity, and she
had perforce to resume her former skin.
She subsequently made many attempts to discard her
weather-beaten apparel, but never succeeded in
accomplishing this without estranging the child. She
recognised eventually that it was futile to continue
the attempts, and settled down resigned to the in-
evitable. As time wore on she, of course, wore out
and ultimately died. From that out death came to
be the common lot of mankind.
* * * *
“Celia Lloyd”: I’m giving notice to all
lovers of the good old flag that I’m not
speaking of immigrants in general, but of just one
that I met lately, and he would drive a Chinaman
from his rice. He says he came from England, but
as it is some months since he landed here, and as
I have never known him to remember anything for
three minutes, I reckon he has forgotten where he
did start from. We are on a building job, and as
he couldn’t think hard enough to sink another post-
hole like the last, we sent him to the camp to fry
the fish. “Breadcrumbs,” said his instructor—
“cover them with breadcrumbs, and don’t get
absent-minded and use sawdust or sand.” I reached
camp ten minutes after the others, and found the
oabinetmaker wildly cursing some dog, cat or
other wild beast for spilling his glue. It’s a fact.
He couldn’t get the breadcrumbs to stick, so he
had gone to the cabinetmaker’s pot and fixed them
that way.
Twill’s Visitant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WILLIAM TWILL was a new chum.
He came to Australia with great
ideas about the wild, free life;
so' he purchased a 6 x 8 calico tent, and set
it up in the backyard of his villa in Ellwood,
Melbourne, and indulged himself in the savage
existence to the extent of sleeping in this rag
dormitory until it deserted him in the follow-
ing peculiar circumstances.
He had retired as usual, and had fallen
asleep with his customary ease, careless of the
dangers to which this primitive living exposed
him.
Sometime in the dark, early hours he was
awakened by the consciousness of a strange
presence* He could see an immense and
hideous head looming vaguely above him, a
darker something in the general darkness. He
could feel the monster’s hot breath stirring
his hair. Then something large and moist,
like two pounds of hot, liquid steak, fell
upon the face of William, and moved softly
and clammily thereupon, like a dove on the
face of the wafers. The unknown horror
was licking Twill preparatory to swallowing
him.
He did not wait for verification of his
conjecture. Rising in a partially slimed
condition he uttered a howl of mortal an-
guish and threw up his arms. He would
have thrown up his legs and his job only he
hadn’t time. The monster was taken by
surprise. Its head went up; its huge
bulk backed precipitately. Then things
began to happen. The tent was in-
continently uprooted, and, torn from
its moorings, Twill’s bed, a collapsible
canvas structure, attached itself to some por-
tion of the prehistoric beast that had invaded
his apartment, and Willie participated in the
subsequent manoeuvres.
A sudden clamor smote sub-section B of
the seaside suburb, and the inhabitants, hur-
riedly ambling to locate it, discovered a
unique line in ghosts charging round Twill’s
back garden. It looked like the spook of an
elephant. It was large and white, it trod
down all obstacles and upended Twill’s hen-
house _in its swoop. It also carried away
one wing of the washery, and buckled a ver-
andah post, and trod a dog shapeless. The
wild ghost was uttering a muffled moaning,
interspersed with frequent shrieks almost
human. When it came near to Swan, who
was perched on a pole, he discovered that it
had horns branching out of its western
gable. Whatever it was it had no conscious-
ness of direction, and in one blind rush pre-
cipitated itself clean through Twill’s fence
into a vacant lot at the back, dropping a
writhing object by the way. On examina-
tion this object proved to be W. Twill in his
collapsible bed. The finding of Cameron’s
cow in the bay next day, drowned and
bundled in a six-by-eight tent, provided the
explanation Willie was incapable of giving.

value of Formamint as a preventive is
appreciated in this house.”

carry Wulfing’s Formamint with me as a
preventive of Sore Throat.”

my testimony to the soothing and curative
effects of your potent remedy—Formamint,
with which lam so familiar. As is my
constant practice, I keep one of your brow u
bottles on my dressing table.’ ’
How to Avoid Infectious Diseases.
Do you realise that most of the dangerous infectious
diseases, such as Diphtheria, Consumption, Influenza, Scarht
Fever, Measles and the like, are caused by germs which gain
entrance to the body through the mouth and throat ?
The minute living organisms which produce such diseases
grow and develop like the seeds of plants, and the mouth and
throat form an ideal hot-house for cultivating them.
The scientist, of course, can grow them outside the body,
artificially. For example, a test-tube culture of the germs
which cause Consumption is depicted here ; and also a bottle
of Wulfing’s Formamint—the means which Science has found
to destroy such germs in the mouth and throat, and so protect
us against the diseases which they breed.
WULFJNG *5 • .
ror mam mt
THE GERM-KILLING THROAT TABLET
These harmless and palatable tablets contain the most
powerful destroyer of disease germs known to Science. They
dissolve in the saliva, and the disinfectant is thus carried to
every little crevice, chink, or cranny in the mouth, gums,
tonsils and throat. Thus all germ growths are sought out and
destroyed, their spread is checked, their power to do harm is
entirely broken.
As a preventive of infectious disease, Formamint Tablets
should be used daily by everyone, in health or sickness, and
they are particularly recommended for school children, who
are so exposed to infection, especially at this time of the year.
Beware, however, of harmful imitations ; there is only one
genuine Formamint, viz. Wulfing’s, and all Chemists sell it at
2s. 6d. per bottle. The manufacturers, A. Wulfing & Co.,
17, Bond Street, Sydney, N.S. W., will gladly send you a Free
Sample and an interesting Booklet, if you write to them,
mentioning this paper, and enclose two penny stamps towards
postage.

MRS. SYDNEY HEL-GRAPH.
Moralisings from Melbourne: —
“Costs” is having, a great time in the matter of
Petek Rodger v. the Vic. Railway Commissioners.
Mcst people have forgotten that Rodger .was the
original contractor for Flinders-street station, and
that his contract was determined in August, 1908
—nearly six years ago-—by the Commissioners, who
finished the job themselves. Rodger thereupon
claimed £29,739 on various items, and the argu-
ment has been proceeding ever since! The Depart-
ment allowed £0463 as justly, due. Then' Justice
Cussen, as a royal commission, inquired into the dis-
pute and awarded Rodger an additional £1546
£BOO9 altogether. Mr. Cussen’s report was made
over two years ago, but either the Department won t
pay. up or Rodger isn’t satisfied, for the-parties are
still arguing. Rodger has been represented all along
by lawyer Cuoker, the well-groomed gentleman with
the subdued hips—and he and learned brother
Macfarlan, representing the Commissioners, are now
worrying out the matter before Railway-engineer
Fraser, as arbitrator. The proceedings are, of
course, in private, but last week the Argus casually
mentioned that lawyer Choker “had just finished an
address which occupied 21 days (!) as a preliminary
to the submission of evidence on behalf of Mr.
Rodger.” So Croicer isn’t rushing the business ;
neither is Rodger showing undue haste ; nor, for that
matter, is the Railway Department breaking its neck
■to get to a conclusion; and there is a fair chance
the dispute will continue till the blessed station is
old and worn out, and suburban railways have been
superseded by aeroplanes and Rodger himself is dead
and forgotten. Even the elegant Croker won’t be on
deck, but some one else will turn up to collect the
costs. “Costs” never dies.
“Solomon Malapkop” writes a letter: —
The N.SAV. Government’s very latest Fair Rent
scheme, which is to lower the cost of living in
Sydney, is to the effect that rents should be fixed
according to the average which the premises fetched
during the seven years before the passing of the Act,
or, if the place isn’t seven years old when the Act
starts, at 10 per cent, per annum on the cost. On
the seven years’ principle rents, of course, would
never rise—at least not openly, though there might
be surreptitious dealing and jobbing in the way of
bonuses —but they would presumably be always
liable to fail. That is to say, the house that started
under the new Act at £1 per week might later, in
some time of general impecuniosity, be let for a
while at 16s. and that interlude might, when better
days arrive, reduce tlie maximum rent to 19s. At
any rate, the bard-hearted man who had charged the
biggest rent during the previous seven years would
come oil best, while the casual philanthropist—if
there are such folk —who had allowed some aged or
battered relative houseroom at a nominal figure
would come off worst. As for the person who had
lived in his own house for (say) ten years and then
wanted to let it, some special arrangement would lie
necessary, for he would neither come under the
seven-year device nor the 10 per cent, dodge. In the
end, of course, Die decline and fall of the house
would end all disputes, and the new premises that
were built on the site would begin life’ on the 10
per cent, basis. Ten per cent, isn’t much of a figure
when allowance is made for insurance, repairs, had
debts, periods of emptiness, rates, taxes (a variable
quality which may increase surprisingly), and for
(tie fact that a house is a perishable property which
in the end ;is v worth nothing, minus the cost of demo-
lition. And 'the •'-administration of the Act will in-
volve a gigantic amount of inspection, else the back-
door fraud of tlie bonus or tlie premium will wipe
out much of the benefit. It might be simpler to try
an experiment at the State ownership of new cities,
for which there is a fair opening at Twofold Bay,
Jervis Bay and Port Stephens. Then tlie State could
hire Christopher Casby, of Bleeding Heart Yard, as
its agent, and collect every copper of rent it could
get, and give back any surplus to the people in the
shane of a diminished public debt and a reduced in-
terest bill.
Tiie Vic. A.N.A. met at Wangaratta this
year and duly passed resolutions anent pre-
ferential voting, wheat in bulk, eradication
of St. John’s wort, extermination of the
house fly, aud a dozen other large subjects
The resolutions will be presented to Premier
Watt —aud that will be the end of the
business. If the Ass. would confine its
attention to one or two proposals on which
it is already decided, such as the need for
Elective Ministries aud the abolition of im-
ported State Governors, and would bog in
until it achieved some result, there would
be a valid excuse for the annual corroborees.
As it is, since the Ass. never by any chance
originates any startling or brilliant idea, it
is, in effect, merely putting a drop of oil on
a machine which someone else has already
built and set going. The oil doesn’t do any
harm, but an awful lot of time is wasted in
putting it on.
The recent regimental insubordination in
Ireland recalls what happened when the
Government of India was transferred from
the East India Company to the Crown in
1859. It was thought fit to transfer the non-
commissioned ranks of the Company’s Euro-
pean regiments, which had been enlisted, and
were bound by their contract of service to
serve the Company, to the service of the
Crown. This change was duly made, and
without the consent of those chiefly affected
by it. The Commander-in-Chief, Sir Hugh
Rose, and the Indian Government took no
notice of the ensuing agitation at first. They
were waiting for orders from Britain —
orders which never came. And then a modi-
fied form of Sheol broke loose. The English
papers of Bengal seethed with infuriated
comment. The publishers were compelled to
give up their soldier contributors. One man
was sentenced to death by court-martial,
and several others to long terms of imprison-
ment ; but at last the justice of the men’s
claim had to be admitted, and they were
discharged wholesale. Contrast the treat-
ment meted out to those soldiers, of half a
century back —men who had done nothing
more than protest against an obvious injus-
tice, who had never refused duty, nor been
guilty of any other overt act of rebellion —
with the treatment accorded to General
Gough and his associates.
“Siniler” Hales wafts a hail from Devon,
where he was at, time of writing, having a
rest after a year’s hard work :
I wrote five big serials last year, besides the life
of May Yolie and the life of Charley Mit-
chell, the latter 100,000 words, and many
short stories and articles. Am hungry to see
old Australia again. There’s no place on Hod’s earth
like Australia after all. It’s a white man’s land.
My dream still is as of old to see Australia an
independent nation, and I’m coming back some day
to fight for it. It is to that end that I have made
war a study in every quarter of the globe. Australia
will never be anything of consequence in the world
until she stands alone. Don’t delude yourself with
the impression that England cares a damn for Aus-
tralia. She would sacrifice Australia to the Japs any
day if it suited her policy.
The Crest.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Aloft, the central summit
Too steep to harbor snow ;
A cone above the mountains
Bare to all winds that blow ;
A crown to all creation —
From his exalted station
lie views the world below.
In summer time or winter,
As each is on the wing,
Their evanescent glories
About his ridges cling;
A starry-pointing linger,
Whereon their pageants linger
From autumn into spring.
For, from his high escarpments,
lie mirrors nights and days—
The sunsets of December,
Or June’s cold, silver haze —
The year in all its phases
About him pales and blazes,
As seasons pale or blaze.
So, hid with distant grandeur,
lie lies beyond the rim
Of changeful things and doubtful
They form no part with him ;
But, drawn in all directions,
They pass as mere reflections —
High glows, or shadows dim.
The cumulus or cirrus,
The rainbow-hue or rain,
About him drift and vanish,
As vapors wax and wane
Like ghosts that scale the ranges ;
For nothing of him changes
In his remote high plane.
For he heeds not of sorrows,
Or any hope or fear;
To one of countless ages
What means another year?
These are not worth the tally;
Heights lie beyond the valley
Not farther off, nor near.
The fevers of progression,
Of birth, and life’s decay,
These are but passing glimmers
A thousand leagues away !
To him the tribulations
Or triumphs of the nations
Are as the moonbeams’ play.
For, wholly of earth’s centre,
The granite child she bore.
He stands immune from changes
Of life, and love, and lore;
Pet high above the surges
Of growths that fret his verges,
Yet scale his sides no more!
So looks lie out and yonder,
Bared of his stony eyes,
To loves of molten ardor
That glimmer through the skies;
Knowing, as one grown older.
That time shall smite them colder,
And stirs not, being wise.
VICTORIA.
C.G.A. COLLES.

TERRIBLE HEADACHES.
“I am a bootmaker,” writes Mr. Frederick Miller,
Great King Street, North Dunedin, N.Z., “and for
many years suffered from terrible headaches and
irregularity of the bowels, due to my sedentary life.
After taking one box of Chamberlain’s Tablets I
was greatly benefited, and they soon effected my
cure. Upon the slightest symptoms of constipation
I take two or three tablets, which always set me
right.”*

Head Office: ROTHWELL CHAMBERS, BRISBANE,
"Rangan”: Among the various reports
afloat re the Australia, one says that Aus-
tralian coal is to be replaced by Maonland
fuel. If our navy is intended some day to
fight for the protection of these shores, then
it would be just as sensible to obtain coal
from Japan as from Maonland. The lie-
fence Department seem to overlook the tact
that different coals demand different con-
stitutional details in furnaces, especially
iu those of a battleship. Therefore, the
peculiarities of the coal Australia intends
to use in the navy should be studied and the
furnaces altered to suit the coal, and not, as
at present, the coal altered to suit the tui-
naces. Even Australian coals show burning
results that vary astonishingly, and a fur-
nace that might give excellent results with
Newcastle coal would give poor results and
deteriorate itself with, say, Dawson coal,
while a different furnace might show the
very opposite results. The coal question is to
be a vital one in the navy, and the sooner
the Department fixes on one. coal the better.
Also, instead of obtaining it in dribs and
drabs, depots should be established at various
strategical points on the coast. If, for in-
stance, Newcastle coal is to be used, how is
the navy to get it if called to northern
waters without such depots? The idea ol
obtaining the navy’s coal from M.D. needs
the heavy foot put on its neck.
— 7
The last local-option poll in N. S. Wales was
a boon to that good old friend, Costs.. Owing
to the alteration of electoral boundaries, pubs
that were ordered to close as a result of the
previous local-option vote, _ were, at the
taking of the last vote, within an electorate
which voted Continuance. Mr. Public-
house made strenuous efforts to retain his
expiring license on the ground that the
position was changed. He failed, however,
and his beer-license was torn brutally
from him. In Mosman, where Reduction
was carried, the agitation was over the way
in which one hotel out of three should be
closed when there were only two hotels, and
it was finally decided that both beer-foundries
should continue. At Granville, after a furi-
ous scrap, followed by a count and a re-
count, Reduction was declared to be carried
by three votes. It was discovered, however,
that the votes of eight absentees 'from other
electorates had been included and as these
might have decided the issue, and they were
indistinguishable from the other votes cast, a
further ballot has to be taken. There have
been other troubles, including a strenuous
but unsuccessful effort by Mr. Public-house
to prove that where Reduction was carried
at the previous poll and Continuance at the
latest, the pubberies ordered to be closed
should get there licenses back. In fact the
whole business has involved a strenuous
item for Costs, and it feels nearly dead.
By “Treponema”
The Royal Commission on Venereal Diseases, now
sitting in England, is collecting some striking evi-
dence concerning the prevalence of these evils in
Great Britain, and their relation to the public health.
Incidentally, too, their relation to the public pocket
through the National Insurance Act and otherwise.
Dr. Love, ear surgeon to the National Bureau for the
Deaf, emphasised the fact that about 25 per cent,
of persons born deaf owe their defect to inherited
syphilis, and this form of deafness is practically in-
curable. It costs at least five times as much to edu-
cate a deaf child as a normal one. Dr. Love also
displayed a series of genealogical trees of syphilitic
families, which showed that two-thirds of the chil-
dren were either born dead, or would have been better
dead, being blind, deaf, or both. Gonorrhoea has for
long been recognised as an important cause of blind-
ness in infancy, but evidence before the Commission
shows that it is responsible for a far greater propor-
tion of all cases of permanent infantile blindness
than any other single cause. Army experience as de-
tailed by Colonel Gibbard, R.A.M.C., and others
points to a very considerable decrease of venereal
disease amongst soldiers, and a much greater de-
crease in the more serious kind of cases. This is
put down to the fact that the men present themselves
for treatment earlier, and are more enlightened with
respect to the methods of avoiding infection. Amongst
62 consecutive cases of soldiers suffering from syphilis
in the primary stage, not one developed the second-
ary stage of the disease. By the use of salvarsan a
syphilitic person can be rendered non-infectious in
from 24 to 48 hours. As syphilis is usually spread
in the earlier stages, prompt and effective treatment
thus has a powerful influence in limiting the infection
of others. Several witnesses emphasised the risk
from treatment by chemists or quacks, whereby the
more severe features of the disease are given time to
develop. The Queensland scheme for dealing with
venereal disease, which has been in operation since
April, 1913, came in for much commendation as the
most practical and effective method yet devised.
Has England really lost its punch? The
air is full of speeches like that of Lord
Charles Beresford, who howled that “if the
army was used against Ulster” (meaning
the foreign element in the eastern half of
Ulster) “it would break the navy as well
as the army”—orations of people who reckon
that the Empire is tottering on the verge of
an abyss or something of the sort because
Belfast threatens to throw 2d. at the eye
of the British Government. All this fuss
about half of nothing looks unmanly. Better
places than Belfast have been wiped off the
earth, and yet the country they belonged to
has gone on and prospered.
A wild wail from Auckland (M.L.) has
been echoing amongst the stars since the
local Famers’ Union received a little bill of
£750, for expenses incurred during the late
industrial scrap along the waterside. The
union is wildly pointing out to all and
sundry that it seized its junk of ironbark
and dashed into the fray at “a time of grave
national peril,” that it did it to “save the
country,” that this action was “in the best
interests of the whole community” ; also, that
it helped to maintain the dividends of the
shipping companies, and preserved lawan-
order. It adds, that “these great services
to the cause of industrial peace” have already
been forgotten, and that all the union ap-
pears to have got are the liabilities totalling
750 quid. The union has called loudly upon
(1) the Government, (2) The Union Co.,
(3) the business community generally, and
so far the only response it has got is (1) the
bill for £750, (2) 13 miles of solid ’silence,
and (3) 321 acres of beautiful, unruffled
calm.
Melbourne’s usual Easter boating disaster
duly arrived last week, five lives being added
to the toll of Port Phillip Bay. They owe
their disappearance to a mania for holding
a piece of string with a bent pin at the
end of it. On Good Friday morning two
enthusiastic fishermen arose at an unholy
hour, caught the first tram from North
Carlton, did the cold ride away down to
St. Kilda beach, and asked a boat-owner to
hire them a vessel in which to “go fishing.”
He considered the atmosphere, viewed the
foam-capped waves and noted the howling
gale, and refused. As he was throwing
away money, that should have been warning
enough to even a fish enthusiast, but just
then a trio of ’couta catchers happened
along, said they were about to go out in
their own private boat, and offered to take
the two. The two accepted, and the five sailed
out, about three-quarters of a mile. Then
an extra violent gust turned the contraption
over. The boat has been recovered, and one
body, and that is all. And at home three
widows and many children wonder at the
inscrutable ways of Providence. Poor
Providence!
The centre of Adelaide is making a fight
for the prominence which its “geographical
position” ought to give it. Commerce and
amusements have hitherto decided to crowd
down on the city’s northern boundary—
carrying on slavishly the tradition of the
beginnings, when all water had to be got
from the river there. The R.C. cathedral, all
the law courts and some Government offices
were placed just beyond Victoria Square, but
a large proportion of the population has
hardly ever gone south of the G.P.O. The
square is waking up now, and its cheap
boarding-houses and fourth-rate offices are
being cleared away. Both of Adelaide’s var-
iety theatres and at least two picture-shows
have to be sought bevond it. The untidy
vacant block onposite the Supreme Court has
developed a palace devoted to chiffons. And
the Glenelg railway, which starts in the
street there, is putting up a stubborn fight
for its existence. S.A. Jockey Club has now
secured a reprieve for it till after Cup Week,
in May, having serious doubts of the tram-
ways’ ability to carry the Morphettville
crowds if the railway terminus is pushed
outside the city boundary. Meantime there
is a disposition to consider electrifying the
whole Glenelg service, instead. It is a hor-
rible thing to be killed by a slow train, but
to be executed by a swift electric car is a
thing which no right-minded person should
worry over.
“G.M.”: Re that par of “Blud’s” (B.
19/3/’l4) about the U.S. notion —-an old,
dead notion revived —of detecting a mur-
derer by photographing the retina of his
victim. The technical difficulties would be
enormous. Consider some of them. The
normal image on the retina is very fleeting,
though somewhat lengthened by a powerful
light-stimulus (a powerful emotional stimu-
lus may increase the effect on the brain-cells
which do the real seeing, but that is another
matter). It is conceivable that instantane-
ous death might fix the retinal image for a
time, though in most cases changes go on in
the cells of the body after what we call
death. Granting that the image is fixed, it
will be very small. A man’s face seen at a
distance of three feet will hardly give an
image more than l-250th of an inch long.
That is, the photograph would need enlarg-
ing 100 times to be the size of the King’s
head on a penny stamp. Also it would have
a network of blood vessels running over it.
Nature dodges these; photography couldn’t.
On the whole it looks as if good old Circum-
stantial Evidence will still be in the running.
IN A THICK VOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Alluding to the trouble with labor, Mr. Mossy Isaacs
congratulated the Board (of a certain shipping co.)
on its achievements during the year. “God knows, ’
he added, “what you will have to face during the
next 12 months from these brutes of men.’’
-—Australian paper report.
Thpeaking ath one who’th made a competently
By thrift an’ thaving up the punterth quidth,
By piling up the cath by penth and penth,
And doing jutht that all hith duty bidtli,
I cannot quite exthpreth by tongue or pen
My hatred for theth brutheth of working men.
By strieteth minding of mine bithineth,
By laying oddth on thtifths at ten to one,
By following the wayth of Ike and Theth
And working cheerfully from thun to thun,
I have amathed of thouth a thcore or ten,
Now theatened by theth bruteth of working men.
They have no thrift —they thave not anything—
But pleatliant toil ith theirth eight hourth a day.
They wathte their thploth on children —quidth they
fling
To wiveth and kidth, an’ trow their gainth away
On furniture an’ food an’ thcoolth. My veary pen
Failth to dethcribe theth bruteth of working men.
Why do they want more wagetli? Thelfithneth
Thutch ath they practith bringth ith punithment
From thith time out they need not wathte their
breath
On me —I’ll lend not under thent per thent.
I will not take their betth nor thee them when
They put a dollar on —thoth bruteth of men.
N.S.W.

THE CONNOISSEUR'S FAVORITE.
POLITICAL
POINTS
This Dominions House foolishness, politely
backed by the Premiers’ Conference, happily,
however, without any financial guarantee, is
due to the hopeless insularity of the Briton,
especially when he is a lord. In Grey’s
mind all “colonials” are alike. They are
not bad fellows on the whole, but not quite
Englishmen. And Grey and his fellow
islanders class them in a lump. Hence his
foolish idea of lumping the lot, for official
purposes, into one big tenement house in
London. Why doesn’t Grey provide a United
Kingdom House on his desirable site, in
which the Scot and the Welshman and the
Irishman and the Englishman and the
Ulsterman and the Manxman and the Manx
cat could quietly fraternise?

Year after year, however, evidence accumulates
that the nation is on the up grade, and that the
Freetrade policy, judged by the only reliable test,
that of results, has been and continues the best one
for the country.— Sydney Telegraph on British
Financial Returns.

“Hamer” : Alas for the dearth of patriot-
ism amongst politicians. At the Premiers’
Conference the only man who appeared to
carry any definite Australianism about him
was Scaddan, of Westralia. Holman,
although representative of a party which is
pledged to Australian ideas, made himself
the leader of the State frighters crowd, and
habitually spoke of the Commonwealth as
if it was some foreign force trespassing on
the preserves of Australian self-government.
Cook and co. weren’t wildly representative
of Australian sentiment either, but as Cook’s
billet is Federal he had to be Federal enough
to keep his billet.
“E.D.” writes: —
During his absence in the Eastern States, West-
ralian Premier Scaddan told some extraordinarily
tall stories about the success of his trading concerns.
For instance, at Bendigo, he affirmed that his
steamers and butcher shop had reduced the price of
meat in Perth by 3d. or 4d. or even fid. a pound.
Previously, he said, steak ranged from 7d. to Is. 2d.
a pound, but now nothing is sold above lOd. Now,
fresh meat never brought Is. 2d. a pound in Perth,
not for a generation or so, anyway. The ruling prices
for years have been: Mutton, fid. to Bd. a pound;
beef, 7d. to lOd. ; and those are the ruling prices still.
Certainly, Scaddan’s meat stall distributes at Id.
below private run shops, but there has never been
enough meat in the stall to supply a good-sized
street. The plain fact is, Premier Jack has made a
howling failure of all his State enterprises, excepting
the pubs. And he is a Rechabite.
JOKOOK’S TOPHET.
A recent happening in Sydney was the
withdrawal of the Furniture Trades Union
from the Sydney Labor Council because it —
the Council —“lacked militancy.” It isn’t
disorderly enough. It doesn’t strike often
enough, or break the law often enough, or
dash things around sufficiently. Incidentally,
the speakers at the meeting which decided'
that there should be more breakages and
more dashing of things around included “re-
presentatives of the British Fabian Society
and the American Federation of Labor, and
a member of the German Social Democratic
party.” What all these bulls are doing in
our china shop this paper knowetli not.
“Red Tape, Junior,” writes: —
Seeing that Premier Holman is con-
sidering a Fair Rents Bill for N.S.W.,
according to which the stipend payable
for the hollow but inhabitable shack is to
be fixed at the average of the remunera-
tion paid during the last seven years, I
would like to ask these questions: (1)'
AVill the men who have to be called in
now and then to repair or paint the
place, or to mow the lawn be paid accord-,
ing to the average wages of the past seven
years? (2) Will the local rates and taxes
levied by the Holman Government be no
more than the average of the last
seven years? (3) Will the price of the
landlord’s and tenant’s food, the frequency
and compensation of the tenant’s domestic
Mary Ann, also the tram and railway
fares which the landlord and tenant have
to pay, the state of repair of the road
outside the house, the rate of interest on
the mortgage, and similar considerations
'remain no better or worse than they were
on the average of the last seven years?
(4) Will Holman guarantee never to ask
any more salary than he has received on
the average of the last seven years ? (5)
Does he know hew to prevent me be-
coming any older or balder than I was 3$
years ago, which is the seven years’ aver-
age once more. (N. 8.: This question
about enacting that the domestic Mary
Ann shall be no scarcer or more costly
than she has been on the average of the
last seven years suggests an idea for a
Fair Drudge Bill. I will draft it.]
From a recent N.S.W. Parliamentary
report:—
Mr. Bagnall resented some laughter from his own
side when he mentioned widows who were employed
as gatekeepers on the railways sometimes sup-
porting children, sometimes sick husbands.
It was hardly a subject for laughter. Many
a widow supports a sick husband when his
own wife has given up the job, or eloped, or
S? n e to the picture show. There are no
kinder folk on earth than widows.
It is a curious fact that at every Easter
Show the most distinctive Australian animal
is missing. That is the Dead Horse which
Posterity will find lying on its verandah
when the credit is stopped and the loans are
falling due.
“Ricardo”: The Cook Government, urged
on by its brightest member, Glynn, propounds
a comprehensive scheme for Northern Terri-
tory railroads. They will start on a total to
reach 19S0 miles. These lines are: Oodna-
datta to Pine (1070), Newcastle Waters to
Camooweal (360), Newcastle Waters to
Westralia (320), and Anthony to Pellew
Islands (230). Total expenditure (in course
of time) extending from £12,000,000 to
£14,000,000. Apart from the comparative
beeline to Pine Creek, there will be consid-
erable discussion on variations of route.
A mere humble trifle of £7,522,100 is the
borrowing proposed for N.S.W. during the
next financial year. It doesn’t sound very
much if the remark is made suddenly, but it
is a lot for a Government which attained to
office on the reduced-borrowing ticket, and
which, after promising to establish a greatly
increased sinking fund, suspended the miser-
ably-inadequate sinking fund which the Lib-
erals set up. Even school buildings and
teachers’ residences (£250,000) are to be
charged to loans without any sinking fund
against them. Yet things of that sort don’t
last very long.' And to make Posterity pay
for the ramshackle structure in which its
grandfather was caned, and probably for
the cane which whacked its grandfather,
looks a reckless species of finance.
“Vandemonian” writes: —
They run small Ministries in Tasmania. The
smallest was that of Richard Dry, which lasted
nearly three years, and included only three men
from first to last. The new Labor Ministrj' will con-
sist of five men. Two of them —Premier Earle and
James Ernest Ogden—were in the first Tasmanian
Labor Ministry, the one which lasted from October
20 to October 27, 1909. Apparently the letter J is
unlucky—perhaps on account of Judas in the New
Testament, the man who held the one portfolio—
that of Treasurer—in the Apostles’ moving Parlia-
ment. That first Tasmanian Labor Ministry con-
sisted of .T. Earle, J. Jensen, J. Ogden, J. J. Long
and J. Cheek. This one consists, at time of writing,
of J. Earle, J. Ogden, J. Lyons, and J. Belton, the
Upper House man not having been selected. And it
came into power because J. Whitsitt was weary of
A. E. Solomon and all his works, and refused to have
anything more to do with G. H. Butler or H. J. M.
Payne.
Sydney Telegraph clamored again last
week for a continuance of the one-big-city
policy in these words :
Where can the wool trade be decentralised away
from Sydney? Where are the commercial centre,
the market, the port for the shipping and the other
essentials of the trade?
In_ other words, where is the new city before
it is built? And for that matter, where was
Sydney before it was built? And where was
the Sydney Telegraph before it started?
The old paper’s pose is that God made Syd-
ney naturally, whereas any other big port
which may be founded in N.S.W. must be
made by man artificially. Which is rather
foolish. Convicts made Sydney. And, now
that N.S.W. is far too great to concentrate
its foreign trade at one port, free men think
of starting another Sydney.

THE FLOWERS OF PATRIOTISM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“We have no desire to hold office,” said Mr.
Flowers, “unless we can at the end of our term be
able to claim that to a large extent our policy has
been carried into law. The Government are deter-
mined that the policy which they placed before the
electors, and upon which they were returned, shall
be, so far as they are concerned, carried into
law.”— Daily paper.
Like a large, blushing rose, he blows,
While leaves of Hansard fall,
And violets wither in the throes
Of Something by the Wall.
They do not care for tended beds
And cool sprays snugly stowed;
They would quite gladly hang their heads
Out in the arid road.
They, grow in beauty side by side;
While Lib’ral weeds are rank,
And thistledown blows far and wide,
They hug their watered bank.
But when no more they scent the air
With Nielsen jobs and such,
From moistened depths their roots they’ll tear
And shun the gardener’s touch.
Meanwhile they do their best to bloom,
Though some harsh critics say
Their scents remind them of the tomb
And those who’ve passed away.
When Labor dies (all things must die)
And Earth of it is rid,
Whatever flowers it grew will lie
Upon its coffin-lid.
Like o large, blushless rose he blo'.cs,
While leaves of Hansard fall,
And violets wither in the throes
Of Something by the Wall.
N.S.W.
THE GARDENER.
THE VIOLET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“If,” said Mr. Langford, “there are billets going,
and a man is a McDougall, an O’Flaherty or an
O’Donnell or a Murphy, he puts down his name and
pushes his nationality for all it is worth and gets
the billet. But if he is a plain Mr. Johnson or a
Mr. Biggs he does not get the billet, because he is
so very humble and so reticent and so reserved. Yet,
gentlemen, it is the plain Englishman who is the
solid backbone of the Empire, and it is the plain
Englishman who, in times of crisis, has saved the
Old Land and cemented the Empire with his blood.”
—Report of St. George Society Lunacy, Melbourne.
There was never a McDougall or a Murphy or a
Flarty,
Did a thing for Empire in the North or South;
And at Waterloo the same was a very absent party
When the English beat Napoleon with their mouth.
The Englisher, so humble and reserved and reticent,
That a brass band blares ere ever he can die,
These qualities have made him loved upon the Con-
tinent.
And in Canada: “No English need apply.”
He’s a shy, retiring blighter is the modest English-
man,
And his mouth is large and splay and full of noise.
And (on the stage) he always bravely leads the battle
van,
And (on the stage) a deadly calm, his poise,
So reticent and humble, and so modest and reserved
That in France his virgin beauty dims the eye.
And in the States that low advertisement is unde-
served :
“Wanted labor; but no English need apply.”
N.S.W.

FOUL BREATH.
MISFORTUNE FOR MAN OR WOMAN.
The old saying that “Distance lends en-
chantment” was never more aptly to the
point than when applied to those people
whose breath is laden with evil-smelling and
offensive odors. Many persons appear most
attractive at a distance; but produce a feel-
ing of pity and disgust when close to, be-
cause of the condition of their breath. For
instance, it makes little difference how beau-
tiful a woman may be or hojv charming her
manner, if her breath is foul; her charm is
gone, and she is at a disadvantage whenever
she comes in contact with other people. Foul
breath in woman arouses in man a feeling
of repugnance and a desire to get out of the
company of such a woman as quickly as
possible; and even women will avoid her.
It is, therefore, important for every woman
who values her personal attraction to take
such care of herself that this unpleasant
and unnecessary complaint is avoided or
corrected. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
will be found a searching and cleansing
remedy, which will overcome this complaint.
They give you clean bowels, a healthy
stomach, a lively liver, and blood that is
rich and red. They are a safe and reliable
remedy, and will prevent many of the com-
plaints so common if taken once or twice a
week to keep the system in a healthy condi-
tion. They are purely vegetable, and their
action is not accompanied by any nauseous
or griping sensation, but is mild and gentle.
For all complaints arising from imperfect
digestion and impure blood Dr. Morse’s Ip*
dian Root Pills are unexcelled,*
SPORTING
NOTIONS
The beauties and advantages of betting
with bookmakers were borne home to a horde of
N.S.W. and Victorian punters last week. Wallalo
was first favorite for the Sydney Cup. Hundreds
had backed him straight out, and scores had him in
doubles of various sorts. He ran fourth in the High-
weight Handicap at the recent Hawkesbury meeting,
and on Tuesday, April 7, the stewards wiped out
horse, owner, trainer and jockey for two years on a
charge of dishonest practices within the meaning of
rule 171. It was said that the punters had got
ahead of the stable, and a bad performance might
help things out. In betting with the bookmaker the
chief thing is to get in early and get the long price.
So the army that regarded AVallalo as the surest
sort of thing the racecourse ever carries got in
early, and, in return for a vast increase of risk, got
a very slight increase in price. The slight increase
in price was out of all proportion to the vast increase
in risk, because, no matter how far off the day of
the race may be, the bookmaker shortens the odds in
proportion to the demand. Now that AVallalo is
disqualified the punters have lost their money with-
out a run for it, and to what extent can be guessed
from the fact that over £7OOO of it has poured into
one Sydney club alone. In betting with the public
at large through the medium of the Tote there is no
advantage in getting in early, as to do so will not
affect the starting price one scrap. Moreover, the
machine does not do business in advance, but only
on the day of the race, when the horse is on the
ground ready to start, and it does business over
starters only. #
The A.J.C. Autumn Meeting opened
badly for punters pn Saturday last, only two favorites
getting home (luring the afternoon. In the Autumn
Stakes the public hurled its swag on board Alured
so enthusiastically that his price fell as fast as
Lucifer did from Heaven, till it bumped six to four
against. Still, a very huge swag went on Cagou at
threes, but that son of Ayr Laddie grievously disap-
pointed his backers by running sixth—a performance
considered quite unworthy of either him or his dis-
tinguished sire. The winner was excellently ridden
by McLachlan, who practised a masterly restraint,
let others make the pace, and at the right moment
worked his mount into the psychological position to
take charge from Aurofodina, Barlow and Bon Ton
on entering the straight. Bon Ton was the danger,
and Killorn brought him with a splendid rush at
the distance. Then there was a tremendous struggle,
but McLachlan had timed his effort exquisitely, and
the threatening Maorilander was worn down nust in
time for the favorite to sail home with half a neck
to spare. The other favorite to win was Radnor, in
the St. Leger. For days he had been the hottest of
pots, and he went out at the impossible price of
seven to two “on.” As there were only three
starters this hideous price was less villainous than it
looked ; but it drove Jots of people to back Beragoon,
who was understood to be at anything but his best, at
threes. AVhat Judas Mcßrian thought of the chances
of the third nag, Simon Frisco, can be gauged by
the fact that the noble steed went out at 33 to one.
This is the biggest risk the Bag-carrier was ever
known to run in Australia —for the otheV two might
have died of rheumatism on the road. The forecast
was realised. Beragoon could not pace it with Rad-
nor. That animal was simply a crack splitting
through the 'atmosphere, and he built a new record
(3min. 44sec., as against the 3min. 4fsec. of Cadonia
in 1911"). Beragoon was beaten by three lengths.
Simon Frisco was left about a week and half a eon-
tinent and a whole paere of history behind. When
last seen he ' was iust getting through the Anglo-
Saxon period of British history.
* * *
As investments at the recent Sydney races
I lie Doncaster, the High-weight, Handicap and the
Sires’ Produce Stakes ranged from threadbare to naked
and impoverished. There may have been some tickets
on Imshi in the Sires’ Produce Stakes, as the un-
suspected brute went out at 10’s ; but the other two
must have been veritable skinners, considering that
First Principle, winner of the Doncaster, started at
any old price between 20’s and 30’s; and Silver Lau,
first home in the High-weight, was begging at 16s.
It is a matter of sober fact that when the bookie
reports his starting price at anything over 10’s, it
means there is practically no demand. The way
the price falls on the slightest demand is one of the
most bewildering things in creation. Compared to
it a working definition of the fourth dimension is
simplicity concentrated. In the Two Year Old race,
Mountain Knight and Eugeny were made favorites,
but the persistency of the demand for the Knight,
assisted possibly by the fact that McLachlan was
in the saddle, brought his price down to twos,
while Eugeny went out at fours. Eugeny led the
way into the straight from Sir with Moun-
tain Knight third. McLachlan lifted his mount to
the front at the distance, and lit out for the
boodle. But Imshi, son of Persian Knight by
Cramari, unnoticed since the start, dropped from
the clouds, came through like a whirlwind, and flew
past the favorite by a length and a quarter, with
Eugeny three or more lengths behind. As for the
Doncaster, First Principle was fifth entering the
straight. At the distance there were about eight horses
in a line. First P. suddenly appeared in front, and
then he and Gigandra hurtled out of the bunehr
locked together, and swept, neck and neck, past
the half distance, where F.P. got his head and half
his neck in front and kept it there, with Tannter a
head behind second. It was great sport for the people
who kept out of it.
* * * *
The curious feature of the Sydney Cup,
run on Easter Monday at Randwick, is that, out-
side the stable, hardlv anyone had a bean on the
chestnut son of Martagon and Chiffon. The im-
ported creature is five years old, and he carried
Bst. Blb. The bookmakers quoted his price as fives,
the same as they offered against the popular favor-
ite, Sinapis, the Maorilander. Belove went out at
sixes, and carried a tremendous swag of public
wealth, as did Multifoil, although the last-named
is said to have started at eights. AVriter knows
ardent punters who wished to back Multifoil, hut
could get no more than sixes, and decided that
Sinapis was a better investment at fives. On form
and weight Multiform, with his 6st. 71b. as against
Belove’s 7st. lllb. and Sinapis’s Bst. 41b., was a
much more promising investment. The more so
considering that Sinapis is practically only a ponv
and the distance was two miles. But, as it turned
out, none of them had a hope with Lilyveil, who,
with the conquering McLachlan up, beat Multifoil
by four lengths and Belove by seven. As for
Sinapis, that noble nag was right out of the hunt,
and finished eighth. Still, Lilyveil last year won
four races out of seven starts, the wins being the
Farewell Welter at Helenavale (W.A.), Hannan’s
Handicap at lialgoorlie, the Hampton Handicap at
the same meeting, and the Boulder Cup—aH West-
ralian races. The Sydney and Melbourne punter does
not seem to seriously consider the chances of any
gee-gees excepting those who race at Sydney and
Melbourne, with, perhaps, a passing glance at Maori-
landers. Even Adelaide horses do not have their
records looked up. It was an angry crowd that lost
its money on Monday, and it said so very dis-
tinctly as the Westralian steed was led in.
Whatever else it might he, the jockey’s job
is a well-paid affair. W. H. McLachlan's success on
Lilyveil, in Sydney Cup, yielded £226 15s. for
3min. 264 sec. labor —something under £7O per
minute! Which suggests the thought that parents
might consider the mannikinship when wondering
what to do with undersized boys.
* * * *
Auckland (M.L.) went fairly to the dogs
at its Easter horse meeting, when £96,882 was
hurled through the totalisator —an increase of
£22,829 on last year. At Randwick (Sydney) there
were probably five times as many horse-followers at
a similar gathering ; but there is no way of fathom-
ing the amount of boodle handled. Neither were
there any profits to anybody but the Bag; and the
foolish public takes whatever odds are graciously
handed out.
