W. F. Trembath, Maoriland’s champion
pro. ped., has definitely retired from the athletic
arena. After two years retirement he came back
this season in great style and mopped up, amongst
others, the following events: 220, 440 and 880 yards
pro. championships at Auckland Exhibition ; 880yds.
handicap, off scratch, at Dunedin; and an 880yds.
handicap at Auckland, off scratch, in lmin. 65 4-sth
secs.—his best performance over half a mile.
* * * *
Glenn Curtiss, the Yankee airman, seems
to be on the track of London Daily Maii/s £lO,OOO
prize for a flight across the Atlantic within 72 con-
secutive hours. He is building, on the lines of his
famous flying boat, something with which he hopes
to do the trick and collect the cash. The wina-s of
the new craft will have a span of 75ft., it will be
of the biplane type, and the boat (fixed beneath the
lower plane) will be 50ft. long by sft. wide. A 200-
h.p. Curtiss engine will be fitted, and is expected to
give the Dreadnought of the skies a speed of over
65 miles an hour. There will be a wireless installa-
tion capable of shouting for help over a 50-mile
radius, but able to “receive” from a much greater
distance ; electric light to read the instruments (and
a prayer book) by ; two sets of controls for the two
pilots; a wind-screen to protect the airmen (strangely
enough such a device was suggested in The Bulle-
tin a week or two ago!) ; a supply of food, petrol and
oil; two pilots and three passengers. The whole
thing will weigh over a ton, but it is expected that
the 200-h.p. engine will hurl it through the atmo-
sphere at such a rate that it will eat up the 1800
miles between Newfoundland and Old Ireland in one
non-stop flight of under 30 hours! Anyhow Curtiss
seems to ho having success with his flying: boat.
Plentv of them are used in Uncle Samland, France,
Germany and Russia, as adjuncts to the navy and by
private syndicates carrying passengers for cash. One
good point about the aerial boat is that, if anything
goes wrong, it can come down and float about until it
is picked up, of repairs enable it to rise again.
POOR DAVE!
After tolerating
Dcnje Smith for nine.
rounds, Eddie McGoorty
hits him all over at once.
“Hick”: Young Scotland, the Maoriland
aviator, is learning a lot about his countrymen. At
Dunedin he found on his ascent day a very small
“gate” and a trying wind. He refused to go up,
or rather his manager refused to permit him. After
a long wait for the wind to drop, the gatekeepers
grew weary and departed. In swarmed the dead-
heads. They, true to their kind, demanded an
immediate flight, and Scotland decided to give it a
trial. Luckily, all went well. Then he struck
Wellington—windy Wellington—where he was billed
to fly on a recent Saturday. He was unable to
start, and the crowd took to him and his machine as
being fakes. John Hop, who endeavored to protect
the youngster (he is only 22) got his wrist broken.
Eventually the trouble ended by the mob being
handed pass-out checks with “leave to come again.”
On the mid-week half-holiday Scotland adveriised
another attempt. It was blowing a roarer from
the south—one of the capital’s best days—and a
large crowd turned up at the selected patch to see
the flight. Scotland is nothing if not daring; in
fact, he is harebrained. Despite his manager’s ad-
vice, he decided to evade a scene similar to the one
of some days before. He got away all right, but the
wind soon had him (as he knew it would) and he
crashed into some trees, smashed his £7OO machine
to matchwood, and nearly broke his neck. Then
the crowd went home satisfied.
* * # *
The French have a handy law by which
any foreigner resident in France can be* sued for
debts contracted in another country, if any French
citizen buys up the liabilities. Most of Jack John-
son’s big English and American creditors have ob-
tained judgments in their own countries and sold
them to French agents. To keep ahead of these
gentry Johnson has drawn his theatrical payments
in advance. AYhen he arranged to fight Frank
Moran in Paris, in the month of June, he wanted
the 175,000 francs paid beforehand. But the Yankee
syndicate which was putting up the brass remem-
bered what happened to the National Sporting Club
when it paid Johnson in advance, and said it would
see him dead in the middle of Africa first. It offered
to deposit the 175,000 francs in any one of a long
list of reputable hanks. Johnson, who was his own
THE GLADIATORS.
Pat Bradley fails to get there.
black lawyer, saw danger ahead. He demanded that
the cash should be lodged at the Credit Lyonnais
to his own name, with the proviso that it could be
lifted by his wife at 2 p.m. the day of the fight.
In order to safeguard himself another clause was in-
serted so that the money would come to him in the
event of his wife’s death. Then Johnson went
home and smiled softly and shed the light of his
golden teeth over the landscape. All the Americans
were vastly impressed by his cuteness, but there was
a rush of French lawyers to explain that Johnson
had done the trick the wrong way about. He should
have had the money deposited to his wife’s credit with
the proviso that only he could lift it at 2 p.m. the
afternoon of the fight. As it was the, contract was
a direct acknowledgment that he has 175,000 francs
at the Credit Lvonnais, which was the best evidence
on which to base an application for the French
equivalent to a writ of attachment. Johnson is in
the soup—so much soup that it is hardly possible to
see his head above it. #
Owen Moran apparently reached the end
of his long fistic career when he met Young Shugrue
in New York lately. Of Moran’s superior skill and
boxing ability there was no doubt, but his vitality
gave out. For seven rounds he put it all over the
Jersey boxer: but that indomitable youth kept com-
ing again for more, and at last Moran realised he
had no more to give. He was played right out,
and the knock-out through sheer attrition loomed in
front. And, rather than take it, Owen stopped
short and announced his retirement. _ As the last
echo of his voice died down a hog in the gallery
squealed: “Open the door. Moran’s goin’ out!
But Moran had gone.
* * * *
When Dave Smith and Eddie McGoorty
entered the ring at. Sydney Stadium on Saturday night
11,997 people stood up and adjured Smith to Knock
him out.” Weights were announced as list s*lb.
McGoorty, and list. 51b. 2oz. Smith. Harald Baker
refereed. No waiting. Preliminaries were rushed
through. A ton of silence fell on the place with a
crash. It sounded like an elephant picking up a hair-
pin in a dead world. Smith looked anxious. McGoorty
looked just like himself. For a man of Irish descent
McGoorty is strangely calm. He has the placidity the
Anglo-Saxon is said to have but hasn’t. He advanced
to the battle as if it was a sandwich. Smith charged
him with a straight left and forestalled the dreaded
left hook. McGoorty backed away, and Smith advan-
ced in close order. Then he followed up with his
right and seemed to smite McGoorty heavily. As they
fell into a clinch the pent-up feeling broke forth in a
torrent of cheering. Dave Smith had survived half a
round. When the gong went, and Smith was still on
his feet and ahead on points, the audience went quite
mad. It didn’t cheer —it howled. The second round
found Smith still anticipating. McGoorty seemed slow.
Smith continued to hit him, but failed to leave any
mark, and McGoorty would hit back wildly, almost
clumsily. In the third round Smith opened with three
straight lefts to the mouth. Eddie was calm, and
when he went to his chair his second, Mat Wells,
seemed quite dumb and gave him no advice. Was
it possible that McGoorty had deliberately planned
to let Smith win the first few rounds? His curious
inability to hit, his awful timing, seemed to sug-
gest it. In every other fight McGoorty had always
returned blow for blow, but now not only had he
ceased to smite but had failed to land when he pro-
fessed to smite. In the fourth chapter of the dis-
turbance - Smith got all over his man, and increased
the pace. In the fifth McGoorty seemed to be quite
outclassed; and by the seventh, people thought
he had simply come into the ring to float a
bank or take up land, or do some other financial
transaction. But, looking closely, writer observed
that whenever Smith got really troublesome McGoorty
planted a solid body punch at close quarters that
steadied the hostile array a lot. In the eighth,
McGoorty startled Smith and the audience by putting
in left hooks in rapid succession. Smith blocked
them, but McGoorty continued to shoot them in. He
was coming out of his shell, although still in retreat.
Smith’s speed seemed to slacken, and his face began
to wear an expression of anxiety. When the gong
sounded for the ninth, McGoorty stepped out lightly
-—a very different fighter from the one who had done
the last eight rounds. Instead of the sluggish, somewhat
clumsy battler he was a gladiator from head to heels.
He had come to attack, and the way he squared up
showed it. Smith hit the boards with a bang, and the
surprise of tbe boards, which had imagined Smith to
be having everything his own way, was obvious.
Smith scrambled up and charged McGoorty with the
left. McGoorty took no heed of his left or his right,
but just hit. Smith clinched and tried to hug.
McGoorty declined to be hugged. He said he was en-
gaged to another girl. He hit while Smith held, he
hit while Smith tried to dodge, and he hit at other
times. Also he hit when he wasn’t otherwise em-
ployed. He dominated the proceedings. In the 10th
McGoorty threw himself at a ruined Smith. The
latter clung desperately to his conqueror, and then out
of the break he struck wildly and by some good luck
smote McGoorty on the nose. But as he came in to
follow it up McGoorty countered to the body and
drove a whirlwind of blows. There was a sort of
mist, or avalanche, or drizzle of fists. Then Smith
took sitting exercise, and that ended it.
Although the insistent demand of its patrons to
give Dave Smith another chance, which demand in-
duced the management to put on the third meeting
with McGoorty, speaks well for the loyalty of the
Stadium patrons, it is a pity a section of it cannot
remember what is due to a visitor. The crowd was
unfair. It cheered Smith when he landed. It
cheered him when he missed. And when he stopped
blows it stamped its feet and repeated hideously,
“Dave, Dave, Dave, Dave!” until 999 times. All the
which was grossly unfair to a stranger who was appar-
ently fighting a losing battle. There is no dispute
as to Smith’s winning all along the line up to the
eighth, when McGoorty seemed to wake up. McGoorty
took no heed of points, for he had determined to win
by a knock-out, and he did; and the bias of the
crowd affected hii.i not one iota. When he finally
sent Smith to the boards he walked to his corner,
and nodded to his seconds, and stood with his back
turned on his fallen opponent while the count was
being put on him —a marked contrast to some other
fighters who have to be held back by the referee
while the man who is down gets up. Presumably his
next battle will be a return match with Jeff Smith,-
to see which of them will first meet the tremendous
Clabby.
* * * *
The fight between Pat Bradley and elusive
Jeff Smith, on Easter Monday night, did not draw
the house expected. In fact, considering the night
and the prowess of the combatants, the mansion was
downright miserable. It was akin to Lazarus and
Moreover, the dog that licked his sores. The fact
may have been due, of course, to the circumstance
that, during the afternoon, the bookmakers at Rand-
wick had skinned the bulk of the population clean.
There was only four ounces difference in the weights,
Jeff Smith being the heavier at list. 341 b. Harald
Baker handled the contest. Jeff Smith, the Apollo
.Belvedere of the boxing ring, stepped out and flicked
Patrick Bradley on that with which he inhales
the perfume of the rose. It was a light, airy, play-
ful touch, but Bradley seemed peevish, or in-
furiated, or mad, or something, and replied
with a terrific right on the neck and cheekbone,
and Jeff Smith reeled. Pat, over eager, rushed in
and sent along something that should have slain an
elephant, but J. Smith was not there. He ducked
and hit like lightning. In vain. Bradley took no
notice of his hitting. He returned blow for blow,
and from everyone that landed J. Smith heaved like a
shin in a gale. He went to his corner after the first
gong—a badly-rattled man, and apparently one whose
rounds were numbered. In the second, Jeff Smith
changed his style of fighting. He continued to hit
when opportunity offered, but for the most part he
played for safety. He put in the straight left against
Bradley’s swings and hooks, and used it with all the
speed he was capable of. There was a time when
Bradley was as quick as any of them —but that time
has passed away. In his anxiety to send in a killing
blow he hits too slowly. So the second round became
an exhibition of a man armed with a short dagger
punch against an ironclad gladiator armed with a
club, one blow of which was sufficient to kill —could
he but land it—and it was Jeff Smith’s chief object
in life to see he didn’t land it. The narrow escapes
were frequent. When Patrick did get home he rattled
his man very badly indeed. The third saw some
heavy fighting at close quarters, in which Smith
proved altogether too quick for Bradley, and he
evaded a lot of things. In between evading he
plugged Bradley whenever lie could with safety. It
was Smith’s first round. In the fourth Smith com-
menced to attack by hurling in the left, and then
ducking and smothering. The tactics were emin-
ently successful, and Pat’s wild swings began to
go far awry, and often through the anticipatory
wallop on the nose the blows he had started to swing
were never sent along. In the fifth the same tactics
were even more successful, and the way Jeff Smith
came in showed he was recovering confidence. Brad-
ley was no longer returning blow for blow. He was
falling into arrears at a rate that, by the 20th round,
would leave him hopelessly bankrupt. In the sixth
Bradley warmed up a bit and ladled out hostilities
with truly Hibernian hospitality. For 90sec. it
looked as though Jeff Smith might lose his job at
any time. But by smart footwork, head-ducking
and good guarding he managed to weather the blizzard.
In the sixth Bradley again lost his punch, and Jeff
got in with some energy. Out of a wild mix-up
Bradley stepped back to swing, and, before he remem-
bered to step forward again, his opposing colleague
smote him twice and sent him down. Pat took
eight and then scrambled up again, and was once
more levelled, horizontalled or proned. In fact, he
was laid low. Yet P. Bradley rose to see the round
out. I-Ie reeled about and made what defence he
could, and Jeff Smith danced round him and showered
blows like hail. But he could not out the iron man
in front of him, and at last Pat started to fight back
in a feeble wav, till the gong went and saved him.
His recuperative powers are amazing. He came
out in the eighth and sparred for time. And he did
it successfully. But the fight was really over.
Bradley threatened to kill his seconds if they threw
in the towel, and swore to see it out. In the ninth
he began to hit hard once more, but he did not hit
often enough, and always waited for Smith to lead.
In the 10th he once more lost his offensive power
and never properlv regained it. In the 14th the
fickle crowd counted Bradley out, and some of them
hooted him. Whole rounds passed without Bradley
landing one effective blow, and in the 16th he was
again knocked down. He was scrambling up to
resume the discussion when the police interfered and
said the job had better have a permanent rest.

Permanently Removing
Superfluous Hair.
“Toilet Gossip.”
How to permanently, not merely temporarily, re-
move a downy growth of disfiguring, superfluous
hair is what many women wish to know. It is
a pity that it is not more generally known that
pure, powdered pheminol, obtainable from the
chemists, may be used for this purpose. It is
applied directly to the objectionable hair. The
recommended treatment not only instantly removes
the hair, leaving no trace, but is designed also to
kill the roots completely. * * * The natural allacite
of orange blossoms makes a capital greaseless face
cream. It holds the powder perfectly, and the
natural odor of this product is, of course, most
delightful. Many of the smartest women are now
using it exclusively, because it does not encourage
a growth of hair on the face. * * * The annoying
body odor sometimes resulting from perspiration-can
be instantly neutralised by the occasional application
of powdered (white) pergol.
A Strange Shampoo.
“Cosy Corner Chats.”
* * * I was much interested to learn from this
young woman with the beautiful glossy hair that
she never washes it with soap or artificial shampoo
powders. Instead, she makes her own shampoo by
dissolving a teaspoonful of stallax granules in a
cup of hot water. “I make my chemist get the
stallax for me,” said she. “It comes only in sealed
packages, enough to make up 25 or 30 individual
shampoos, and it smells so good I could almost eat
it.” Certainly this little lady’s hair did look wonder-
ful, even if she has strange ideas of a shampoo. I
am temnted to try the plan myself. * * * For an
actual lfair-grower nothing equals pure boranimn.
It is quite harmless, and sets the hair roots tingling
with new life. * * * The use of rouge is almost
always obvious, but powdered colliandum gives a
perfectly natural color and defies detection.
Renewing Complexions
with Oxygen.
“Handy Recipes.”
Everyone knows that oxygen consumes waste
matter" in the body. A poor complexion is merely
the accumulation of half deadened or waste matter
upon the surface of the skin. It sticks on stubbornly,
showing in the form of sallowness, moth patches
and a generally lifeless appearance. Oxygen may
be used with advantage in removing this disfigura-
tion. Any chemist should be able to supply mereol-
ized wax. This contains oxygen in a form that is
released when it comes in contact with the skin.
Naturally the oxygen attacks the deadened matter
but does not affect the healthy skin at all. Thus a
few applications of this perfectly harmless' substance
soon begins to clear the skin and reveal the beautiful
complexion which every woman has just underneath
the ugly one. The process is pleasant and harmless,
and involves no inconvenience whatever. * * * To
bring a natural red color ’to the lips, rub them
with a soft stick of prolactum. * * * For hot, tired
or perspiring feet, use a teaspoonful of powdered
onalite in a foot bath.
What Women Hate.
“Helpful Gossip.”
Every woman hates a shiny nose and a dull or
greasy complexion. Few know that there is an
instantaneous remedy at hand in the home, one
that is absolutely harmless, and that defies detection
even under the closest scrutiny. If you have no
cleminite in the house get about an ounce from your
chemist, and add just sufficient water to -dissolve it.
A little of this lotion anplied to the face will in-
stantly cause the greasiness to disappear, and the
skin will have a i>evfectly natural, velvety, youthful
bloom that any woman might envy. The effect will
last for many hours, and no powder is required, even
under the most trying conditions, indoors or out.
To prepare the and arms for a long even-
ing in a hot ballroom, nothing can compare with
this simple home-made lotion. * * * To make the
eyelashes grow long, dark and curling, apnlv a
little mennaline with the finger-tips occasionally. It
is absolutely harmless, and beautifies the eyebrows
as well. * * * Pilenta soap is the most satisfactory
for all complexions. It even works well in cold or
hard water.

The Inky Way.
James Francis Dwyer, an Australian pressman who is
Said to have “made good” in the United States, and is
now hack in Australia on a holiday, spoke an interest-
ing piece in the Australian Journalist (25/3/ 14).
One extract is as follows: “I was warned by cei-
tain people in Sydney to never trust American edi-
tors ! Well, I have never been done out of three
cents in the States. It was an old joint, especially
of The Bulletin, to say that Yankee editors were
the fleecers. Bah ! Australian editors are the fleecers.
In Sydney I had to wade through 48 pages to And
a ninepenny paragraph in one weekly. And when I
had to pay sixpence for the paper all I really got
out of the deal was threepence. In America I
have got my cheque for 350 dollars, or whatever it
was, the day after my story was accepted.” If Mr.
Dwyer is alluding to the old Sydney Mail as the
paper which charged him 6d., it can only he hoped
that there is some mistake. There are one
or two men on The Bulletin ?tatt who have
written for the Mail, and they say that, however
had its politics may he, its business dealings are
prompt and liberal. At all events it is pleasing to
know that he doesn’t refer to The Bulletin. When
Mr. Dwyer contributed to this paper he was always
on the free list. Mr. Dwyer made his literary
beginnings in this paper, with a series of short
stories which appeared under a pen-name. 1' c-r
reasons that need not be particularised only a tew
people had any idea of the author’s identity, rhe
stories ought certainly to be republished in book
form, and the author should have credit for them.
They might be a trifle crude, but they were virile.
They were all paid for on acceptance, and at a
much higher rate than that paid in those days,
by any other Australian paper. The publica-
tion was necessarily slow, owing to lack of space.
In fact, one or two, though purchased years
ago, have not yet got into print, in which
respect J. F. Dwyer has shared the -ill-luck of
the late Victor Daiev. The Bulletin still trea-
sures some unpublished fragments of that great
artist’s work, though, unfortunately, they are very
few. #
“Billex”: Brisbane Courier imparted the following
mysterious item of news to its readers recently, in
the guise of a telegram from Adelaide reporting a
shooting trial. “Murray threw tumblers in the cap-
tain’s cabin, and threatened McDonald, who fired at
the ground in self-defence, but Murray made a rush,
and the bullet entered his leg.” No details are
vouchsafed as to what the ground was doing to
McDonald, or why it was in the ship’s cabin; but
the fact that McDonald needed to fire at it m his
own defence argues that it was behaving riotously.
It’s a strange yarn altogether, but the strangest part
of it is the fact that when McDonald fired at the
ground he was such a rotten shot that he plugged
Murray. Perhaps there was a very heavy sea running.
“Wang” : Sub-editing cablegrams seems to be a
sort of guessing competition. This from Melb. Age
the other morning: “The quarter of Copenhagen
known as Frederick’s Place, or circus, containing
four symmetrical palaces occupied by the King and
Roval family, has been destroyed by fire. One of
the four palaces is inhabited by the King, the second
and third by other members of the Royal family,
while the fourth comprises the coronation and state
rooms. The extent of the damage is £lOO,OOO. No
lives were lost.” And this from the Argus: An
extensive circus has been destroyed by fire in Copen-
hagen. The damage is estimated at £lOO,OOO. For-
tunately, all the animals were removed from the
building in time to escape destruction.” *
“O’Cork”: The following notice has been issued
from the P.M.C.’s Department:—
“Memorandum.
“Ceneral Post Office,
“Sydney •
“I have to inform you that the period ot six
months for which correspondence addressed to you
may be sent to this office will expire on the •>
lfii , and it will therefore be necessary for you to
make other arrangements for its delivery by having
it sent direct to your address, or elsewhere, or by
renting a private box, when it will be dealt with
in accordance with your instructions, otherwise cor-
respondence addressed to you at the_ General Post
Office, will be returned to the sender after
the date named.”
Which is to say that the period of six months being
about to expire, the addressee should make other
arrang'ements for the delivery of the period of six
months bv having the period of six months sent
direct to his, or her, address, or elsewhere, or by
renting a private box, when the period of six months
will be dealt with in accordance with liis, or her,
instructions; and, if the addressee fails to make
other-arrangements for the delivery of the period of
six months, his or her correspondence will be re-
turned to the sender after the expiration of the
period of six months.
“0.C.C.: If you listen carefully you will hear a
low wail, or soft moan, or mild complaining eman-
ating from the Thief Papers and the Pirate Maga-
zines. The reason is that the big illustrated papers
of the world, led, as far as I can ascertain by that
energetic old stayer, the Illustrated London News,
are taking to printing their masterpieces in colors
which defy the camera of the block-maker and “re-
producer.” Very soon the moan, or wail, will
become a howl. The pirate rags and mags, will
find most of their occupation gone'; not to speak of
their circulation, when they are Tillable to range at
will among the expensive pictures of their present
victims. It is going to be a cold day for the Para-
site and the Weekly Steal and the Refuse Maga-
zine, for they will have to go and get work, and
pay for it.
“Ginger”: Such is fame! I tramped into Carra-
tliool (N.S.W.) and found a cheque signed James
Fairfax and Sons waiting for me at the post office.
In honor thereof I decided to bestow a bottle of
whisky on a deserving bloke —myself. The hotel-
keeper picked up the cheque, and scanned it long
and carefully. Finally he shook his head. “This is
no good to me. I don’t know these people,” he said.
“They may be all right; but I don’t know them from
a bar of soap, and I don’t take risks with strange
cheques.” “Those people,” I said, “can put up a
pound for every copper you’ve got. They own the
Sydney Morning Herald.” Bung laughed the laugh
of the not-to-be-taken-down, and replied. “I thought
a company owned the Herald.” (Then confiden-
tially.) “Say, mister, spring it on somebody else.”
Accordingly I sprang it on the storekeeper, who sold
the Herald. Later in the day I heard the publican
relating to a commercial how a flaming bagman had
tried to ring a crook cheque in on him.
llearst’s Magazine, New York, publishes some
interesting statements about its loathsome and rep-
tile contemporary. A few circulation figures:
American Sunday Magazine, over 2,150,000;
Saturday Evening Post, 2,086,000 ; Woman's
World (monthly), over 2,000,000; Ladies' BoME
Journal (monthly), 1,685,000; Associated Sunday
Magazine, 1,400,000 ; Illustrated Sunday Maga-
zine, 1,300,000. Of the publications better known
to Australia Munsey's Magazine runs out 400.000 a
month; Everybody's, 600,000 ; McClure's,
500,000 ; Pearson's (monthly), 150,000; Century,
85,000; Harper’s (monthly), over 100,000;
Review of Reviews, over 175,000; Smart Set,
60,000 ; World’s Work, 127,000 ; Strand, 100,000 ;
Wide World, 100,000 ; Delineator, 900,000 ;
Collier’s Weekly, 600,000 ; Scientific American,
62,000. Advertising rates are quoted : Saturday
Evening Post, Ladies’ Home Journal and
Woman's World, eight dols. a line, or 4500 dols.,
6000 dols. and 5000 dols. a page respectively;
Delineator, 3500 dols. a page, or five dols. a line;
Cosmopolitan, 1000 dols. (4.50 dols.). Most of
these statements are probably inventions. The
newspaper circulation liar with his circulation lie is
a wonderful person. When U.S. magazines spread
themselves about each other’s greatness the proposi-
tion seems to be: “If you scratch my unparalleled
back I’ll scratch your unprecedented back in re-
turn.”

Try Kriischen Salts with early
morning tea.
Many users of Kriischen
Salts are in the habit, of
adding from one quarter to
a half teaspoonful of Kriis-
chen Salts to the cup of tea
they take before rising.
This is an excellent habit
and is strongly endorsed by
doctors —indeed many doc-
tors take Kriischen Salts
that way themselves.
During the summer, es-
pecially, it is a practice
which has many advantages.
Hot tea co-operates with
the action of the salts in a
most satisfactory manner,
and hastens the removal of
uric acid poison from the
system —poison which tends
to accumulate much more
rapidly in summer than in
winter.
Prevention is better than
cure, and by adopting this
method of preventing uric
acid accumulations in the
system it is quite easy to
guard against
Liver and Kidney Disorders, Gout,
Rheumatism and Lumbago.
and other troubles caused
by uric acid. The
result is a sensation of
brightness, vivacity and
vigour: worries disappear,
indifference vanishes, and
depression gives way to a
feeling of confident op-
timism.
Kriischen Salts are non-
efFervescent and can be
taken without causing the
slightest flatulence or dis-
comfort. They are there-
fore most suitable for the
numerous people who can-
not take aerated or “ fizzy”
drinks.
The Ingredients of Kriischen Salts are necessary.
It must be borne,in mind that
the ingredients of Kriischen Salts
are necessary for healthy life. Your
doctor will tell you so if you will
show him the analysis (on every
bottle). Your body must, of
necessity, obtain these ingredients
from somewhere or you could not
live.
Normally your system should
extract those vital salts from your
food—meat, bread, fruit, vege-
tables, milk, eggs, and so on; but,
as a matter of fact, owing to
impaired digestion, errors of diet,
overwork, anxiety, worry, seden-
tary occupation, and many other
causes, your system does not extract
from food the correct proportions
of these essential lifegiving salts.
And what follows ? Depression,
or headaches, or constipation, or
disordered liver, or inactive kid-
neys, or Rheumatism, or Gout, or
Sciatica, or Lumbago, or Eczema
—many of which arise from excess
of Uric Acid, the cause of more
trouble than many people suspect.
Ivriischen Salts should be your
safe-guard. Besides cleansing
the body of impurities gently,
surely, and painlessly, they possess
a wonderful power of giving new
life and vitality to the countless
millions of cells of which every
body is composed.
Flesh, blood, bone, cartilage,
brain and nerve are all made up
of minute cells, and every cell
requires one or more of the nu-
merous constituents of Kriischen
Salts for its healthy life.

W. B. (“Doc.”) Carr Las been for the
11th time re-elected as Chairman of Adelaide
Stock Exchange. He is president also of
the Adelaide Racing Club, and was for
years—after Moxon Cook, and before
Hadrian Moody—sporting editor of the
Register.
Governor Latrobe’s house (Latrobe got
to Melbourne long before State cocked hats
were cheapened by the Commonwealth) is to
be pulled down. Bedggood, son of the
early pioneer Bedggood, now departed,
owns the ruins of the Latrobe house,
as well as the ground it stands on. The
big, red walls of the Bedggood boot factory
overshadow it, and when the relic is gone it
will tramp on to the site and take posses-
sion. Bedggood is generously giving any
preservable parts of the house to the asso-
ciations that are likely to treasure them.
There is a solid mahogany door that some
modern vandal has painted with white lead,
and there are fireplaces with solid cedar
frames (the cedar hidden under a coating
of preservative tar). Some casement win-
dows with frameworks of wrought iron and
simple bar fastenings also await the right
sort of owner. The old house itself is
weatherboard. Several early pilgrims
arrived and retired disappointed; they had
looked to see an ivy-clad ruin. The
last occupant was a Bedggood carter, who
had it rent free, but he told the boss con-
fidentially that “them [tigers?] drove him
out.” Alas for romance! When fashion-
able loyalists and sentimental old-timers
crowd round the owner and bewail the pro-
posed destruction, lie tells them the story
of the carter. There is a galvanised iron
fence round the house, but it (the fence) is
gone at the knees. An old-fashioned gate
opens into an ivy-covered path. Then you
see a bow window,, green latticed, and, stuck
in the ivy, a pair of boot-trees. Some grape
vines show ancient, gnarled roots; a visit-
ing expert prodded them with his walking-
stick and said they were at least 50 years
old. Some of the old out-liouses are wattle
and daub, with later brick additions. The
main building has walls of solid cedar pan-
nelling, which had been long hidden, but
are now disclosed. Some brilliant house-
holder of the past had covered the rare w T ood
with hessian and cheap wall paper. The
wall paper had been renewed many times
and the panelling was completely buried.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
out upon the drink-drowned sand,
Splay-footed Eulalie,
And eat the peanuts from my hand
I purchased, love, for thee.
The little whisky-colored flies
Are humming ragtime tunes,
And in the skies are stars the size
Of private-bar spittoons.
The bug-bear on his native moss,
Bowed down with aching void
(Like Socrates at Charing Cross),
Quaffs liquid celluloid.
While drearily behind a veil
Ten million Cabots flop,
Changing from green to thin vermeil
At ev'ry jump and hop.
Glad-hearted as an Arab dog
The star of dawn springs forth,
Then travels down the East incog.
Like Yeddah in his wrath!
Mary McCommonwealth is here !!!!!!
Iler parasol of glee,
With woodland nettles dipped in beer,
Bestraddles half the sea.
0 shadow-hearted infidel—
O Elephant of Bletch!
Though breakfast food and asphodel
Thy cankered belly stretch,
The frigid fingernail of Doom
Hath sprawled across the sky,
The Nogi of the Afghan Tomb,
“Bel-Allah-no-llal-Gye.”
N.S.W.
ANGELINA BANANA.

IN BANANA LAND
M.L.C. Fowles, just returned from a trip
in Europe, informed the Brisbane Y.M.C.A,
that in his travels he had found “two things
ring true —the English sovereign and Chris-
tianity.*’ This collocation of God and Mam-
mon is in the true spirit of pietism.
Rev. James Williams, secretary of the
Queensland Temperance Alliance, died in
Adelaide, where he was representing his
State at the Australian Convention. The
water he had barracked for so long turned
doe on him at last. A cold bath, acting on a
weak heart, took him off at 68.

At present tlie State Treasurer manipulates the
public accounts in JvAe, to prevent the year showing

Warwick Argus, after having been in the
hands of the Morgan family . for 50 years,
has been purchased by Dunn, of the Mary-
borough Chronicle, whose son will assume
the editorship. The Argus is one of the
few Queensland papers that have preserved
a semblance of political independence of
late years.
The South African Government, which re-
cently deported certain indignant Labor men,
was not always so uncivil to the party. Three
years ago, when Fisher and some friends
were touring in Boerland sumptuous corri-
dor saloon carriages, with dining, sleeping
and observation compartments, were provided,
and a cook and steward were told off to
accompany them. Fisher isn’t half so well
treated in his own Bananaland.
In the matter of gigantic coal seams
Queensland easily wipes out the rest of Aus-
tralia. Close to Gladstone lie seams of great
thickness and extensive area. Blair Athol
(Clermont) can show its 40ft. of solid black
diamond, while Mount Mulligan already-brags
about an infant seam of 30ft. Bowen, if all
reports be true, bids fair to out-rival New-
castle in its future output, and to redeem its
neglected port history by a big mineral pro-
duction.
Cherry Bloom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Is was a little crooked street, with houses
walled about
And rags stuffed in the window frames to
keep the showers out,
With scraps of paper, gutter-borne to some
sunk grating’s gloom,
And yet a wind came dancing here and sig-
nalled “Cherry bloom !”
(O cherry bloom! O cherry bloom! I guess
the way you grow.
The rain has played a wizard trick and
turned your green to snow.
O cherry bloom! O cherry bloom, the sly
wind told me so!)
It was a mean and ugly street, the haunt
of rags and dirt,
Of stagnant and uncovered drains where
broken pipings squirt.
A cabman’s yard adjoined the street, a-hum
with bloated flies,
And rubbish pyres that offered up an acrid
sacrifice....
Wise people did not walk this way, for vice
might harbor here.
The womenfolk had unkempt hair, the men-
folk reeked of beer;
But where a sick child’s window turned a
blind eye from her room
That rampant Socialist, the wind, came
shouting “Cherry bloom!”
(O cherry bloom ! O cherry bloom! You
float above the grass,
The daisies lift their fring&d heads to let
your shadow pass.
O cherry bloom! O cherry bloom, and warm
sweet smell of grass !)
It was a crowded tenement with misery
brooding over,
And yet the breeze climbed into it, caress-
ing like a lover.
The sick child clapped her pallid hands, for
all her squalid room
Seemed carpeted with sunny grass, and
walled with cherry bloom!
(O cherry tree! O cherry tree! the angels
o’er you trod.
It was to clasp their golden feet you
climbed up from the sod.
O cherry bloom! O cherry bloom! The
first Spring kiss of God!)
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 11, 1914.
My Dear Thar'gomindaii,—
There is a great scattering of the Mac-
gregor clan this Eastertide. His Ex. is
spending his holiday with a chosen few down
the Bay, and Lady Macgregor and the fair
daughter are away in the Roma district.
Last Friday week they hied them off to
Urella station, where their august presence
maketh glad the hearts cf the Donald
Fletchers. They will return to vice-regal
Fernberg in time to entertain a party on
board the Lucinda, at Henley, on the ISth.
The big ripple on the social sea this week
was the tea-party given by the wealthy E. D.
Miles sept, at their Ascot mansion, as a fare-
well to Mrs. Arthur Earl and Mrs. Railway-
Commissioner Evans, previous to their trip
to Peckham Rye and other places in Eng-
land.
The charity performance at the Dandies
came off triumphantly last Monday, and
AFRAID OF FLOOD.
MANLY RESIDENT (proudly exhibiting the ocean to Outback friend): “Ain't it awe-
inspirin', magnificent?”
FRIEND : “My oath. But I wouldn ’ like to live ’bout here in flood-time.”
the beauteous sweet-sellers did a howling
trade. Governor Macgregor, wearing his
grass-widowerliood with equanimity, came
up to time and circumstance, supported by
his trusty understudy, Arthur Morgan, and
liis missus (who captured the vice-regal
bouquet of crimson roses). Mrs. Mayoress
Jenkinson and Mr. “Dandies” McCallum
received the visitors, and all went well.
That is to say, business was brisk, compli-
ments were at par and geniality was skying
the market.
This brilliant city of ours is invaded by
a cheery horde of visiting bowlers, and some
of their wives. All the bowling-greens have
been treated to a shave and a hair-cut, and
look in the pink of condition. The East
Brisbane Bowling Club was particularly
pleased with itself, for it had just finished
its new pavilion in time for the Carnival.
Last Saturday its president and Mrs.
Faulkner entertained all the metropolitan
bowlers and croquet players. They came in
crowds, and the opinion was freely ex-
pressed that the South would have difficulty
in showing a better green. The tournament
games commenced yesterday (Good Friday),
to the disapprobation of many, but to the
pagan onlooker it seems a most blameless
and virtuous game, almost as innocent as
marbles.
The races this afternoon attracted a
goodly crowd, a large proportion of it being
country visitors. The dressing was in keep-
ing with the sultriness of the day, the
womenfolks present having presumably
raked up the remains of their summer ward-
robe.
Yours perspiringly,
AUSTRALIAN JANE.
“Chasuble”: Wyndham Heathcote scores
as the new Melbourne Unitarian pastor,
but is even less of a Unitarian than Walters,
Rignold’s “Joseph of Canaan” man, who
quarrelled with the Melbourne congregation
because he was not Unitarian enough. In
short, Heathcote strikes me as a complete
agnostic. He even seems to object to
Jehovah, as a human invention, and to be a
Pantheist, so far as tangible. For long the
Unitarians had a tomblike little place, until
Walters, to whom they were so ungrateful,
was instrumental in building the present
fine little church. Apart from him, the line
of pastors has been dismal in non-apprecia-
tion : Higginson, Pillars, Martha Turner
(H. G. Turner’s sister, afterwards Mrs.
Webster), Lambley, Sinclaire and Reid.
Wybert Reeve, the actor, preached there on
one or two Sundays.
Although the breezy Melbourne suburb of
Northcote contains some 20,000 inhabitants,
and quite as many beer foundries as the next
burgh or bailiwick, only eight residents were
last year convicted locally of tanglefooted-
ness. The most popular explanation of the
mystery is that the prevailing brick dust
coats'the stomach of the Northcoteite with a
spongy lining which acts as a peerless beer-
absorbent. It was a Northcote “brickie” who
said that it was only after his 17th matutinal
pint that his mouth felt moist enough to
tackle his breakfast.
The Wimmen’s Liberal Leagues of Aus-
tralia, and other bodies which are in the
habit of charging Sosh with irreligion, and
designs upon Christianity and the Home,
should read Aaron Hoskin’s new anti-
Socialistic work, “With Other Races.” Some
extracts :—-
“Love of Rest is the root of Socialism, which is
a parasite that grows upon civilisation.” “Socialism
will sweep over Australia and perhaps the world,
with the same disastrous effects that early Christian-
ity swept over the Roman Empire.” “The saints went
barefooted and worshipped at this horrible swindle of
a Holy Sepulchre.” “Now Saladin was one of those
creatures who stand out and redeem the human race
at an age when it appears destitute of human
attributes.” “Christ was only a religious and Social-
ist crank —nothing more.”
The Bulletin isn’t so imbecile as to sug-
gest that because one anti-Socialist writer
thinks this way that therefore a hatred of
Christianity is the keynote of the anti-Sosh
propaganda—in which respect it displays a
more sportsmanlike spirit than Liberalism
has done in the past. Still it would do the
Wimmen’s Leagues good, on general prin-
ciples, if they studied Mr. Hoskin’s volume.
The gullibility of the spirit rappers is per-
fectly amazing. A book has just been pub-
lished in Maoriland by one of the foremost
snook experts in the great Fan land, in it
is printed (amongst hundreds of others) a
message supposed to have been received by a
M.L. “circle” £»om a Chinaman who died
2 a oo years ago in Central China. It is in
English. More wonderful, it is in “pidgin”
English. Of course, English wasn’t in-
vented then. Neither was the Chinaman,
lie was invented quite recently.
The Equivalent of Pouring £100 per
Week into Sydney Harbor.
Eveby now and then the taxpayer of N. S.
Wales reads in a daily paper that his im-
ported State Governor has been visiting the
Jenolan Caves, or lodging a mild, Sabbath-
school shaft in the vitals of pugilism; or
opening a Twilight Home or Resort for
Strayed Goats or what not; and thereby the
taxpayer is enabled to remember that he is
still disbursing good money for a luxury
which he was assured would be abolished
upon the arrival of Federation. By the
present look of things Unification and Recon-
struction will have to come before there is
any hope of scotching the ancient anomaly;
and even then there is a likelihood of the
usual Jingo idiot stepping forward and advo-
cating the importation of English knights or
J’s.P. to act as stage monarchs of the re-
distributed States, x'he trouble in this con-
nection arises from the fact that State Labor
Ministries appear to be fully as eager to
retain the imported State figurehead as Lib-
eral ones are —and this despite the fact that
the abolition of the office of State Governor
is a leading plank on the Labor platform.
According to the daily papers Lady Edelink
Strickland’s health is so poor in Australia
that she is returning presently to England.
In this event her husband may be expected
shortly to tear himself away as well. The
opportunity will be an excellent one for W.
A. Holman to show himself more stiff-
necked on an issue of very considerable im-
portance than his predecessor was. It is no
secret that ex-l’remier McGowen and his
family were very successfuly nobbled by the
Colonial office during their recent visit to
London. “Honest James,” or whatever his col-
leagues call him, began by hammering rivets
on an East End boiler, and ended up by buy-
ing a Windsor uniform. He returned to N. S.
Wales a passionate upholder of the con-
ventional Imperial point of view—one phase
of which is that the Jenolan Caves can’t be
visited, or pugilism assailed, or Twilight
Homes opened by any Governor who is not
a British man of title ; that is if the Empire
is to remain secure. The. end of Sir Gerald
Strickland’s term here will give Premier
Holman the chance to prove the imbecility
of this aged superstition. Why Australia
has not long since adopted the sensible Cana-
dian system of appointing its State Governors
is to this paper a standing mystery. In
Canada the State Governor is selected prim-
arily by the Governor-General in Council.
Supposing a flagrantly partisan appointment
is made, the Governor-General has the right
to veto it. In the unlikely event of no com-
promise being arrived at between the Gov-
ernor-General and his advisers, the matter
goes to the Crown for adjudication. This
arrangement is calculated to obviate the
appointment of anyone like Harry Holland,
on the one hand, or Henry Dangar, M.L:C.,
on the other. It operates in practice to pro-
cure the selection of fairly desirable men. As
a matter of fact, the job of finding a satis-
factory Governor is a childishly easy one.
Under a Constitution such as that of N. S.
Wales almost anyone with sufficient intel-
lect and moral character to conduct a whelk
stall is capable of govving for an indefinite
period. What blithering foolishness it is
in the circumstances to go on importing
nonentities from England for the billet, and
paying them on the same basis as though
they were first-rate men struggling with vast
responsibilities!
«
A Sydney paper interviewed the great
Colonel Lassetter a few days since re the
breaks in the railway gauge, and published
this information: —
The standard gauge all over the world is 4ft. B£in.
The Victorians have the wider gauge simply be-
cause, in the old days of inter-State rivalry, they
had always to go one better than N.S.W. With
Queensland, their narrower gauge was simply a
matter of cheapness.
It may not be a matter of much importance,
yet even in the small matter of a railway
gauge it is worth while to tell the truth.
Victoria wasn’t trying to “go one better
than N.S.W.” when the disastrous break of
gauge was started. The trouble was that
N.S.W. never would keep faith. It broke
the agreement re a uniform gauge just as it
did the one re a uniform tariff, and just as
it broke its pledges over the first Federal
Constitution. The original bargain, made
with the advice of the British Government,
which interfered in a lot of details in the
earlier days, provided for a uniform 4ft. SUn.
gauge over N.S.W., Victoria and S.A. Then
N.S.W. got up on its bumptious ear, and
intimated that the Irish gauge (sft. 3in.)
was going to be its choice. Victoria and
S.A. grumbled at this high-handed dictation,
but they gave in for the sake of peace, recog-
nising that Ma was a very important sort
of female, and they got to work very hard.
Meanwhile N.S.W. rested for a year or two.
Finally the Ma State really started railway
building, but having changed its mind again
it went back to the 4ft. BUn. gauge—the one
it had bullied Victoria and S.A. into abandon-
ing. After that no more agreements were
attempted, and any old gauge was regarded
as eood enough.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MSS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
R.T.: In reference to our offer to pay
£lOO to the “only patriotic Australian”—if such
there be—you say with a shrinking, flower-like
modesty that appeals to ys considerably: “I am the
only Australian with brains and com-
bined. I was responsible for Federation. I smashed,
by my letters, the attempt to seize the name of
Australia for N. S. Wales. I answered all the at-
tackers of Federation till the last of them caved in.
I sent an open letter (paid for as advt.) denouncing
the attempt to make Lyne first Prime Minister, and
Barton cut me dead later on after being largely the
means of his becoming such, and that because I
publicly claimed to be the Father and Founder of
the movement for Unity. I have strenuously fought
for an Australian Navy and Compulsory Training
when the burden of replying in the dailies to cease-
less attacks was left wholly to me. Finally, I claim
that there would have been no Federation but for me,
that there would have been no Australian Navy, no
Compulsory Training and no general White Aus-
tralia policy, which I inaugurated in a pamphlet I
published in 1880. Now send along your £lOO. I
know you will not send a copper, and that on to
the end it shall be mine to sow where others reap.”
r> -ML*? imposing record, but your supposition re the
£lOO is correct. As a matter of fact, a rival got in
before you. He claimed to have made the entire
continent with his own hands; also Tasmania,

St. Joseph’s Band of Launceston (Tas.),
is the oldest brass band in the Common-
wealth, this being its 68th year. Sixty-eight
times has St. Jo’s played “St. Patrick’s Day
in the Morning, ’ and its records, mostly kept
in a book that looks as if it came from a
grocer’s counter, shows that it was used to
serenade John Mitchell, while he was lying
low in Launceston, waiting to make his
escape from the island. Mitchell was in
hiding in the Cornwall Hotel—once the pro-
perty of John Pascoe Fawkner— and the
band was ordered to practise in the Cornwall
Assembly Rooms next door —now the hotel
dining-room. The band knew nothing
of John Mitchell. It played Irish airs with-
out design, and so serenaded the Irish patriot
unbeknownst. A few years back the old in-
stitution split over a change in conductor-
ship, and the schism had to be settled in
court with a pro forma action for illegal de-
tention of a tambourine or a copy of the
“Maiden’s Prayer,” or something. Senator
Keating anneared for half the band, with
Clarke on the other side, and the breach was
finally healed. To-day St. Jo’s blows “St.
Patrick s Day” with a blast unanimous.

The whole issue of 30.000 shares was underwritten by Mr. A. W. Palfreyman for a remuneration of
6d. per share, after Mr. Tlios. Horton, of Sydney, had, on the underwriter's behalf, check-bored certain
portions of the company’s property.
Report by Mr. R. T. Hugger.
The property comprises an area of approximately 720 acres of tin-bearing land at Bruas, near
Trong, Perak, Federated British Malay States.
SITUATION.—The property is situated 26 miles from Taiping, the capital, town of the State of
Perak, Fed. Malay States. It lies to the cast of and between the 25th and the 26th milestone on the
macadamised main road from Taiping to the Dindings, which form its western boundary.
TITLES.—The whole of the land is taken up under licenses issued by the Perak Government. This
license reserves the sole right to apply for and receive the usual renewable mining lease for 21 years
issued by the Perak Government, and which will be subject-to the ordinary conditions and-terms-of
labor employment or its equivalent in machinery, rent and prg duties. , . • * ■
AREA PROSPECTED.—Out of the 720 acres, approximately 150 acres have been close bored and
found to contain highly payable values, 180 acres have been wide bored, and found to contain ‘payable
values; while approximately 250 acres of suitable ground remains to be proved. -* v
DESCRIPTION.—The property consists of a large, swampy flat, on to which several valleys debouch
from the high granite hills and mountains east of the flat. Two of these valleys are included in the
property, and can be worked as well as the flat by the same plant without any dismantling. Small
streams continually running down several ravines in the granite ranges at the heads of and on the north
and south sides of each of the valleys form a swamp, through which runs l a never-failing stream through-
out the entire length of each valley. These streams join the River ltotan on the flat, whose course is
from north to south along the flat, and about 20 chains to the west of the valleys’ openings. Both the
valleys and the flat are covered with light timber and the ordinary jungle growth, while the ranges
abutting carry heavy hardwood timber highly suitable for fuel. ■
TRANSPORT.—The property is excellently situated as regards transport, being but 13 miles from a
small ietty at Trong, by the well-made and well-kept main Government road, or 25 miles by the same
road from Taiping, the capital town of Perak. This road forms the western boundary of the property,
and is practically level from Taiping. '
PROSPECTING. —One valley only, viz., the northern one, has been close bored. Ninety-two bores at
3x4 chain intervals have proved an area of approximately 150 acres to be highly payable; while over
30 bores at 6 x 6 chain distances show that the body of wash extends over at least 180 acres, m the
valley to the south, and to carry payable values. From the boring plan it will be immediately seen that
no rich run of ore has been followed : but the bores were put down at as regular intervals as possible, and
along the lines cut through the jungle for the purpose. • ■* ' ... ~
WASH DIRT The karang, or wash, is a clean, coarse granitic sand and gravel mixed with small
waterworn stones‘up to a size of 3in. x liin. (say, a duck’s egg), underlying a Jxody of jalt and fine
sand and resting on an even bottom of soft, decomposed granite. In the northern valley close boring
proves'* the * body 0 of karang over an area of' approximately 150 acres to be lift, thick, of an average
1 « noil. i-• .• J „ -trovrl TVIO Q Vfl T 1 (T IS f>nVf
valueof 2 361b' of tin oxide per cubic yard. The karang is covered by an average depth of 10ft. of silt
and fine sand ‘(the former being about 6ft. deep and the latter which carries fine ore not saved in
boring 4ft deep), thus making the whole an average depth of 21ft. from surface to bottom with an
average value of 1 251 b. of tin oxide per cubic yard overall. Wide boring in the southern valley proves
the karang to extend there over at least an area of 182 acres. The boring shows it to average 16ft. thick,
of an average value of 1.161 b. of tin oxide per cubic yard. The karang rests on an even bottom of soft,
decomposed granite, a soft sand, or a soft clay, and underlies a friable, sandy overburden, averaging
Bft. deep, making a depth of 24ft. from surface to bottom, averaging 81b. of tin oxide per cubic yard
overall This ore and karang is similar in all respects to that in the northern valley. # >
QUALITY AND GRADE OF ORE. —The ore is practically free from the usual impurities found in
these States, and has the high metallic value of 744 per cent, to 75i per cent. A purity of 73 per cent,
will easily be reached, and maintained, by treatment in bulk. Moreover, the ore is of an easy, saving
grade as a series of carefuLtests showed that 20 per cent, of the ore was so coarse as to prevent it from
passing through a sieve whose mesh was 20 holes to the square inch, that 60 per cent, of the ore was
between 20 and 50, 10 per cent, between 50 and 80, 7 per cent, between 80 and 100; while 3 per cent,
was finer than the last-mentioned measurement.
AREA UNBORED. —The valley close bored, and the one wide bored, as well as a third now being
profitably worked by some 230 Chinese coolies, debouch on to the large flat before mentioned. This flat
contains approximately 300 acres, which should be prospected, as all the valleys are to all purposes
merely arms or extensions of the large, swampy flat. .
ADJOINING AND PAST WORKINGS. —Evidences of work done in times long past are visible in the
ranges in the shape “of ground sluices, watercourses, etc. At the mouth of the northern valley, the
remains of some old Malay smelting works are also to be seen. The ore from the ranges alone was
apparently treated as both the valleys and the flat are quite virgin. On the north of the granite hill
(Bukit Dusan), forming the northern boundary of the close-bored valley, a third valley debouches on to
the flat This valley contains several Chinese open cast mines, employing about 230 coolies, who are
doing well. The ore is coarse, and of a high metallic value. Their average sales are on a basis of 73
per cent metallic, with ore running to 74 per cent, and 75 percent, metal lip,/: j
CONCLUSION —Every feature relative to a successful and highly-profitable bucket-dredging proposi-
tion is contained in the area, as will readily be seen from the following summary, and given a modern
machine with average running times, say 70 per cent, per month, sound profits must be shown.
Summary.
COARSE, SANDY MATERIAL, WITH NO STICKY CLAY.
A FINE BODY OF TRUE VIRGIN KARANG:
ECONOMICAL DEPTH.
HIGHLY PAYABLE ORE CONTENTS. HIGH-GRADE COARSE ORE, EASILY SAVED AND DRESSED.
SOFT, EVEN BOTTOM. . ***-—-• ■- -- '
NO BOULDERS, SUNKEN TIMBER OR OTHER. OBSTRUCTIONS TO DREDGING.
AN ABUNDANCE OF CLEAN, FRESH WATER. SUITABLE FOR BOILERS AND ALL PURPOSES.
NO SPECIAL APPLIANCES FOR ORE TREATMENT OR SAVING.
HARDWOOD FUEL ON AND SURROUNDING THE PROPERTY IN QUANTITIES THAT WILL MORE
THAN LAST THE LIFE OF THE WHOLE MINE. ...
EASY TRANSPORT. "
NO ONEROUS LEASE CONDITIONS TO OBSERVE.

Legal Notices.
PURSUANT to an Order of the
Chancery Division of the High
Court of Justice made in the matter of the
estate of Robert Townsend Hippisley de-
ceased Hippisley v. Hippisley 1912 H. 1515
and dated the 12th October 1912 the follow-
ing inquiry is directed, viz.: An inquiry
whether CHARLES ERNEST HIPPISLEY
named in the will of Robert Townsend Hip-
pjsley is living or dead and if dead when he
died and whether he left any and what will
and whether he was ever married and if so
when and to whom and whether there were
any children of the said marriage and who
are his legal personal representatives.
Notice is hereby given that the said
Charles Ernest Hippisley and all persons
claiming through or by descent from him
are personally or by their solicitors on or
before the 4th day of July 1914 to come in
and prove their claims at the Chambers of
Mr. Justice Joyce and Mr. Justice Eve,
Room 692 Royal Courts of Justice, Strand,
London, England, or in default thereof they
will be peremptorily excluded from the bene-
fit of the said order. Wednesday the 15th
day of July 1914 at half-past two in the
afternoon at the said Chambers is appointed
for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims. Dated the 15th day of December
1913.—A KEEN, Master of the Supreme
Court.
ROBBINS, OLIVEY & LAKE,
218 Strand, London, W.C.,
For ABBOT, POPE & ABBOT,
Bristol,
Solicitors for the Plaintiffs.
Note.—The above-named Charles Ernest
Hippisley who was a son of the testator
Robert Townsend Hippisley a solicitor of
Bristol England is now if alive about 51
years of age. He went to Australia in Feb-
ruary ISB9 and was there employed by
Messrs. Robert Reid & Co., and afterwards
till May 1897 by Messrs. Sands & McDougall
(Limited). He resided at Rochester Lodge,
Collins-place, Melbourne. He left Mel-
bourne in May, 1897, by the Orient Steam-
ship Orizaba for London, where he stayed
one or two months, and is believed to have
resided at 123 Fulham-road, London. In
October, 1897, he left London, and until
April, 1898, was employed by Messrs. J.
Garlick & Co., of Alderley-street, Cape
Town, South Africa, and resided at 43 St.
John-street,. Cape Town. He returned to
Melbourne in 1898, and expressed his inten-
tion of going to Queensland or Sydney. He
is alleged to have been seen in London by an
old schoolfellow in 1908.
Should the above advertisement, which
has reference to benefits accruing to him
under his father’s will, meet the eye of the
said Charles Ernest Hippisley, or in the
event of his death that of his widow or
children, he is (or they are) earnestly re-
quested to communicate with the least pos-
sible delay with his brother Alfred Edward
Hippisley, of 8 Herbert-crescent, London,
S.AV., or with the said Messrs. Abbot, Pope
& Abbot, solicitors, Bristol.

Branches in all parts of the world.
An energetic parishioner was discovered in
a Maoriland church the other day making a
few improvements. He had installed a
gramophone in the vestry and cut a hole into
the hall of worship large enough to accommo-
date the funnel. Around the walls he had
artistically pasted a varied selection of illus-
tx'ations from magazines and newspapers.
He explained that it was his fixed intention
to brighten up the services somewhat, and
that he had in his pocket a fully loaded re-
volver and a supply of cordite to help along
the good cause. He emphasised his suspicion
that the singing hadn’t been all it ought to
have been lately, and said he intended to go
farther into the matter. Likewise, if the
parson didn’t show a marked improvement
he would be loaded down with lead and
remorse. As for anyone in the congregation
who sang out of tune, all harmony would be
at end in this life. Then the police arrived
and had the interesting citizen removed for
examination. The doctors said he was in-
sane,_ but they’re in an overwhelming mino-
rity in the neighborhood.
The tale of “F.C.” :
A really absent-minded woman occurred at Ben-
digo (Vic.) the other day. After a pleasant after-
noon s shopping she proceeded to take a tram home,
in company with a go-cart, a baby and a handbag
containing a purse with money, a railway ticket, a
recipe for making lemon honey and a piece of tussore
silk she wanted to match. She deposited her valu-
ables beside her till the tram came along, then
stepped lightly aboard. She had gone about a mile
when it occurred to her that she had forgotten her
hand-bag, so she hastily bestowed the infant on an
accommodating stranger, caught the next returning
tram and recovered her property. She was so upset
by her good fortune that she indulged in a cup of
tea and had reached the middle of the refreshment
when it dawned on her that her baby was missing, so
she took another tram back to where she had first
got off and recovered the child. She then left for
home and had almost got there when she recollected
that she had once had a go-cart about her person.
So she fled wildly back and annexed the vehicle.
It is understood that she reached home ultimately in
fairly good order, and with all her accoutrements
about her. On the other hand, the allegation may
be a mere baseless rumor.

Business, Robbery, etc.
One of the matters that was favorably considered
at the Melbourne Premiers’ Conference was a request
for compulsory unionism. The trouble arose over
the frequency of fraudulent jewellery. The Retail
Jewellers’ Ass. pointed out that the institution of
an Australian hallmark Or Government stamp
would be little, if any, protection tb the buyer
against “brummy” stuff, especially where jewellery
is sold in public auction rooms and similar places,
where the maker cannot be traced, and no guarantee
is given. It explained further that it had a system
under which each manufacturer attached to every
article made by him not only the hallmark, but also
a mark which is registered against his name with
the Retail Jewellers’ Ass. Unless that is done no
associated retail firm will buy from him. The
result is, of course, that all such jewellery can be
traced, and, under the agreement, any manufac-
turer selling fraudulent or under-standard jewellery is
liable to a heavy penalty, which is paid into the
Ass.’s funds. Unfortunately, however, some retail
jewellers don’t belong to the Ass. Its idea is that
they should be compelled to be good unionists, by
having the system legalised throughout the six
States. The Ass.’s objective is eminently commend-
able, but it seems a roundabout way of getting there.
Meanwhile, until the long day arrives when the six
State Insects will all agree to do the proper thing
simultaneously, the Ass. might notify the public of
the names of its members, so that that deluded
entity might know just where to go when it wanted
15-carat trinkets, and not articles that are stamped
15 carat, but are really half lead and half dead and
part brass, with a nine-carat wash to deceive the
eye.
* * * *
“C.E.”: It is schemed to build the largest bridge in
the world across the Hudson, uniting New York and
New Jersey. Cost £8,250,000. Towers almost com-
parable to the Eiffel. Single Span, With accommoda-
tion for 400,000 passengers an hour. Eight tracks.
Two for subway traffic, two elevated, four for surface
trains: Highways for 10 vehicles abreast. Deck
200 ft. wide. Towers 550 ft. above water, 250 ft.
under water. Biggest ocean-liners can go underneath.
Total length, a mile and a half. Clearing the land
will cost £1,000,000.
* * * *
“Melb.”: G. Calder, of Victoria —just back from
a six-months’ tour of the Northern Territory—is
much impressed with the Victoria River district.
The Victoria is a beautiful river, navigable for 90
miles, of which 50 can be traversed by large boats
to the natural harbor of Blunder Bay, where there
is an anchorage of from 11 to 13 fathoms. The rest
is navigable by boats of 3 ft. draught. Meatworks at
Blunder Bay, with transit, would lead to enormous
business. The Vic. is the outlet for 47,000,000 acres
of country. Besides the cattle country there are
hundreds of thousands of acres of rich chocolate
flats that will grow anything.
“Without Reserve”: A Sydney police magistrate
has sentenced a Brummagem “auctioneer” to a
month’s remuneration in the calaboose for selling
under the hammer a brass chain after describing it
as 9ct. gold. The transaction took place at one of
the many Cheap Jack auction rooms which are an
unrighteous odor in the city. By some kink in the
law the crooks who run these joints can go to some
outlying suburb and obtain auctioneers’ licenses,
which entitle them to take down the highest bidder
all over the State. As the police are unable to
check the issue of these licenses a conviction can
only be obtained with the assistance of a duped
customer, and only rarely will such a one come up
to the scratch. Even when a conviction is obtained,
the magistrate is inclined to be lenient. The pur-
chaser who bids ss. 6d. for a “real English lever,
spring balance, jewelled in every hole, with solid
silver case,” deserves, so the Bench argues, to fall
in, while sympathy is seldom expressed for the lady
who buys an 18ct. gold ring or brooch set with real
pearls for 3s. 9d. In the conviction above referred
to the magistrate who surprised the “auctioneer”
with a month without the option, relieved his (and
the other fellow’s) mind by suspending the sentence
under the First Offenders Act, or First Time Caught
Act. Still, for him, auctioneering will in future be
a risky business.
Several Australian firms are pushing a “garden
fertiliser” in tablet form at the rate of 6d. per
ounce. This works out at eight hundred and ninety-
six pounds (£896) per ton. An analysis of the
tablets shows that, taking their chemical constitu-
ents at market rates, a liberal estimate of their
value would be £6 15s. per ton. The equivalent in
the form of a mixed complete fertiliser could be pur-
chased from any old manure merchant at £8 per
ton at the outside. It is this sort of thing that
indicates the reason why retailers retire early and
build large houses and do themselves well in other
respects. There is just one cheap and infallible
fertiliser. You slay the local grocer and bury him
under the rose-bush along with the midnight cat and
the early-morning rooster.
* * * *
“Billy T.”: Had a few words with a blind beggar
in our Melbourne suburb the other day. He was
bitterly abusive of “These here electric trams.”
“They’re ruinin’ our profession,” he said. I gathered
that his profession included all mendicants, and won-
dered how. He explained: “There was a time when
a copper amounted to nothing in this country. People
could do scarcely anything with it, so they dropped
it into the hat of the poor blind bloke on the corner,
and it was worth while being blind them days. But
now everybody wants pennies for the damn penny
sections, and things are so bad with me I’m think-
ing of filin’ me schedule. I’ve shifted my pitch
twice, hunted down by penny trams, and I expect I’ll
be getting under one, one of these dark nights, and
finishing it.” I would have given the poor fellow
a penny, but that would have left me with nothing
less than half a crown, and tram conductors are so
insolent if you offer them big money.

JIU-JITSU WHITE CHAMPION
OF THE WORLD.
9
1
y
VS
Mr. W. A. HILL
(from a photo.).
Mr. W. A. Hill, the only white man to hold a Mikado medal for
Championship honors in Jiu-Jitsu, the Japanese method of self-
defence, is universally acknowledged the Champion White Exponent
of this art, as well as one of the greatest athletes living.
Mr. Hill introduced Jiu-jitsu into the West Point Military College
in America, and has, amongst others, instructed ex-President Roose-
velt. and the South Australian Police. With all this experience
behind him Mr. Hill is an authority on athletics, and he says that
he has never found anything to train with so good as Rexona; he
uses it daily himself, and recommends it to all his pupils, and says
that, amongst the many thousand things he has tried in the
training-room, nothing has ever approached Rexona.
All Athletes will be interested in the letter of this Champion, who
writes as follows:
Rexona Proprietary Company, March 19, 1914.
SYDNEY.
Dear Sirs, —
I have been using Rexona, the Rapid Healer, for the past
eight months, and I want to tell you of how much value I
have found it. 1 first came across Rexona when it was
being used by the Masseur in the School, Mr. Noel, and was
so pleased with it I at once got a large-size tin, and have
used it constantly ever since. Taking on, as I do, all-
comers in Wrestling versus Jiu-Jitsu, etc., some thirty
times a day, naturally I meet with numerous kinds of acci-
dents, consisting of Scratches, Abrasions, Strains, Wrenches,
etc., all of which require immediate attention, to prevent
blood-poisoning or stiff muscles. I make it a rule every
night to apply Rexona to every injury, whether large or
small, and always use Rexona to have a good rub-down
when I have finished my day's work, to relieve the tired and
strained muscles. I think I have tried pretty well every
preparation on the market, and there is nothing I have met
that has given half the satisfaction that Rexona has given.
I have a very high opinion of Rexona, and will certainly
always recommend it to all my pupils, for I consider it just
the thing we need in my business.
Wishing Rexona the success it deserves,
I um, Yours truly,
(Sgd.) W. A. HILL,.
ALL ATHLETES use REXONA
No training- or dressing-room is complete without a pot of Rexona, the Rapid Healer. It
is invaluable as a rub-down for strained and tired muscles, for it quickly removes all stiffness and
tiredness resulting from over-exertion. Wrestlers, Boxers, Swimmers, Oarsmen, Cricketers and
Footballers have all added their praise of Rexona. Rexona is an excellent remedy for Cuts
Wounds, and other lacerations ; removes the discoloration and soreness from Bruises of all descriptions
and is in fact a reliable, quick-acting panacea for all possible accidents of the athletic field!
Rexona is sold everywhere in triangular pots at 1/G, or in pots holding nearly four times the
quantity, at 3/-
